Defining Knowledge by Division and Example
Commentary on the method of defining knowledge by division and example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Islamic theologians define absolute knowledge by using the methods of division (قسمة) and example (مثال).

Core Idea
Knowledge is defined by distinguishing it from other beliefs through a division tree.

This passage explains two methods for defining absolute knowledge: division and example. The commentator shows how division systematically separates knowledge from conjecture, ignorance, and blind following, and how an example like 'perception of the inner eye similar to the outer eye' illustrates it. The lesson also addresses objections to these methods.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the flow. Notice the technical terms: قسمة (division), مثال (example), and the three criteria: جازم, مطابق, ثابت.
2
Trace the division tree
Follow the divisions: Belief → decisive/non-decisive → matching/non-matching → stable/unstable. Identify where knowledge (certainty) emerges as the stable, matching, decisive belief.
This tree shows how knowledge is distinguished from conjecture (non-decisive), compound ignorance (non-matching), and unstable blind following.
3
Understand the example
The example 'knowledge is the perception of the inner eye similar to the outer eye' provides a likeness. Consider why it is problematic and how the commentator recasts it as a descriptive definition.
The example is not a perfect definition but a way to convey the concept.
4
Reflect on the objections
The passage notes objections: the division only distinguishes assentive knowledge, not absolute knowledge; the example may be a definition. The commentator responds that absolute knowledge is known through its divisions and that example is just a likeness.
This shows the sophistication of the method.

قوله : ( فهي أن تميزه إلخ) يعني لا اشتباه للعلم يسائر الكيفيات النفسانية، ولا للعلم التصوري إنما الاشتباه للعلم التصديقي، والقسمة المذكورة تميزه عنها، فحصل معرفة العلم المطلق بأقسامه فلا يرد أن الكلام في العلم المطلق، والقسمة إنما تميز العلم التصديقي عن الاعتقادات فلا تكون مفيدة لمعرفته .

The Second Observatory – On the Definition of Absolute Knowledge 
74
His saying: ('Because the support by it is illusory') i.e., imaginary, not real, so the word 'perhaps' is for minimization, and the minimization is in terms of quality. This is on the assumption that 'difficulty' is meant as the opposite of ease. But if it is meant as 'not easy', then it includes the impossibility of definition as well, so the minimization is because the mentioned proof is a sophism. 
His saying: ('And its method of knowing is division and example.') As for division, it is that you distinguish it from the beliefs that are confused with it. So we say, for example: Belief is either decisive or not decisive; the decisive is either matching or not matching; the matching is either stable or not stable. So there emerges from the division a decisive, matching, stable belief, which is knowledge in the sense of certainty. And it is distinguished from conjecture by decisiveness, from compound ignorance by matching, and from the blind following of the correct decider by stability that is not removed by questioning. As for the example, one would say: Knowledge is the perception of the inner eye similar to the perception of the outer eye, or it is said: It is like our belief that one is half of two. And this saying is far because both—i.e., division and example—if they afford a distinction of the essence of knowledge from what is other than it, they are suitable as a definition and a limit for it, since what is meant here is not its specification. 
His saying: ('So it is that you distinguish it, etc.') means that there is no confusion of knowledge with other psychic qualities, nor of conceptual knowledge; rather, confusion is with assentive knowledge, and the mentioned division distinguishes it from them. So knowledge of absolute knowledge is obtained by its divisions, so it is not objected that the discourse is about absolute knowledge, while the division only distinguishes assentive knowledge from beliefs, so it is not beneficial for knowing it. 
His saying: ('Knowledge is the perception of the inner eye, etc.') Apparently, the similarity is a description of the perception of the inner eye to exclude the perception of the inner eye that is not similar to the perception of the outer eye, i.e., that which contains doubt. Then it is objected that this is a definition of knowledge as a descriptive definition composed of common and distinguishing elements, while the discourse is about the example that benefits its knowledge. So the appropriate way is to make his saying 'perception of the inner eye' an explanatory apposition or a substitution for 'knowledge' to specify the intended meaning, for it may be applied to the faculty and to the known, and his saying 'similar' is the predicate. This is supported by what is in Sharh al-Maqasid: 'As for the example, it is that the perception of the inner eye is similar to the perception of the outer eye.' 
His saying: ('Or it is said: it is such and such') He benefited that the example in al-Ghazali's speech may be in the sense of likeness and counterpart, or in the sense of a particular of knowledge, and his mention of it in al-Mustasfa does not indicate exclusivity. His saying: ('They are suitable as a definition and a limit') based on the established truth that what necessitates knowledge of something is knowledge of it. 
His saying: ('So it emerged from the division, etc.') If he means that nothing emerges from the division, it is rejected; otherwise, the restriction of the method to division and example then is rejected, so reflect. His saying: ('They are suitable as a definition') It was objected: Mere affording distinction does not necessitate suitability for definition, even if definition by description; it would only necessitate if they afford a necessary concomitant that is evident, and what is obtained by division, for example, are not evident necessary concomitants; otherwise, it would not be unknown.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the method of knowing absolute knowledge through division (قسمة) and example (مثال). It begins by explaining the phrase 'the support by it is illusory' from the previous section, clarifying that 'perhaps' indicates minimization because the proof is a sophism. Then it presents the main idea: the way to know knowledge is by division and example. Division proceeds by distinguishing knowledge from beliefs that are confused with it: belief is divided into decisive/non-decisive, then decisive into matching/non-matching, matching into stable/unstable. The stable, decisive, matching belief is knowledge (certainty). This distinguishes it from conjecture (non-decisive), compound ignorance (non-matching), and sound but unstable blind following (non-stable). Example: knowledge is the perception of the inner eye similar to the outer eye, or like our belief that one is half of two. However, this example is considered far because if division and example afford distinction, they could serve as a definition (حد) or description (رسم). The commentator then addresses objections: (1) The division distinguishes assentive knowledge, not absolute knowledge; answered by saying that absolute knowledge is known through its divisions. (2) The example of 'inner eye' may be a descriptive definition; the commentator suggests parsing it differently to avoid that. (3) The example may be interpreted as a likeness or as a particular of knowledge. (4) Merely affording distinction does not necessarily make them suitable as definitions unless they provide evident necessary concomitants, which division may not provide; otherwise, it would not be unknown. The passage ends with this critical point.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The division method gives a complete definition (حد) of knowledge.
Correction: The passage states that division only provides distinction (تمييز), not a specification (تحديد). It is a method of recognition, not a formal definition.

Checkpoint
According to the division described, what distinguishes knowledge from compound ignorance (جهل مركب)?
A) Decisiveness (جزم)
B) Matching reality (مطابقة)
C) Stability (ثبوت)
D) Both A and B

Key Takeaway
The method of defining absolute knowledge through division and example is a way to recognize it by systematically eliminating other beliefs and providing a familiar likeness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of your religious beliefs are merely decisive and matching but not stable when challenged? What would it take for you to root them so deeply that questioning does not shake them?
?
The passage warns against blind following (taqlīd) even if the opinion is correct. In what area of your faith are you guilty of this, and what is one concrete step you can take today to seek certainty instead?
?
If you were to apply the division method to your belief in God's existence, would you classify it as decisive, matching, and stable? If not, which component is weakest, and what are you willing to do to strengthen it?

Two Ways to Define Knowledge: Division and Example
Explains the two methods of knowing absolute knowledge: division (qisma) and example (mithal).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how division (qisma) and example (mithal) can serve to define absolute knowledge (al-ilm al-mutlaq) by distinguishing it from other beliefs, and why such methods are sufficient for a definition.

Core Idea
Division and example define knowledge not by genus and species, but by what sets it apart.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif introduces two ways to recognize the essence of knowledge without a strict logical definition. The author shows that grouping beliefs into categories (division) and giving an analogy (example) can suffice to identify what knowledge truly is, as long as they uniquely mark it. We'll see how division works step by step and why even a simple example can be valid.

Guided Path
1
Understand the purpose of defining knowledge
The author is not seeking a formal genus-differentia definition. Instead, he explains two methods that distinguish the essence of knowledge from other things. Begin by recognizing that the goal is mere recognition (ta'rif), not a strict logical definition.
2
Trace the division tree
Follow the divisions: belief is either decisive or not; decisive is either correspondent or not; correspondent is either stable or not. Knowledge emerges as decisive, correspondent, and stable. Notice how each subdivision eliminates confusion.
The final category is 'knowledge in the sense of certainty' (al-yaqin).
3
See how division distinguishes from other concepts
The passage explicitly states how knowledge is distinguished from opinion (by decisiveness), compound ignorance (by correspondence), and the correct belief of a follower (by stability that resists doubt). Understand each distinction.
This shows that division is a powerful tool for demarcation.
4
Consider the example and the final point
Read the two examples given. Then note the objection (it seems far-fetched) and the author's response: if division and example provide distinction, they are suitable as a definition. The goal is recognition, not a perfect logical definition.
The author defends the validity of these methods for defining knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما القسمة فهي أن تميزه عما يلتبس به من الاعتقادات فنقول مثلاً: الاعتقاد إما جازم أو غير جازم، والجازم إما مطابق أو غير مطابق، والمطابق إما ثابت أو غير ثابت، فقد خرج عن القسمة اعتقاد جازم مطابق ثابت وهو العلم بمعنى اليقين، وقد تميز عن الظن بالجزم، وعن الجهل المركب بالمطابقة، وعن تقليد المصيب الجازم بالثابت الذي لا يزول بالتشكيك. وأما المثال فكان يقال: العلم إدراك البصيرة المشابه لإدراك الباصرة، أو يقال: هو كاعتقادنا أن الواحد نصف الاثنين. وهذا القول بعيد، فإنهما أي القسمة والمثال إن أفادا تميزاً لماهية العلم عما عداها صلحا معرفاً وحداً لها، إذ لا يعني ها هنا بتحديدها سوى تعريفها.
As for division, it is that you distinguish it from beliefs that are confused with it. For example, we say: belief is either decisive or non-decisive; decisive is either correspondent or non-correspondent; correspondent is either stable or non-stable. Then there emerges from the division a decisive, correspondent, stable belief, and it is knowledge in the sense of certainty. It is distinguished from opinion by decisiveness, from compound ignorance by correspondence, and from the decisive belief of one who hits the truth by stability that does not disappear by doubt. As for example, it is said: knowledge is the perception of the inner eye similar to the perception of the outer eye, or it is said: it is like our belief that one is half of two. This statement is far-fetched, for division and example, if they provide distinction for the essence of knowledge from what is other than it, they are suitable as a definition and a limit for it, since here by defining it nothing other than recognizing it is intended.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage explains the two methods of knowing absolute knowledge (al-ilm al-mutlaq) as mentioned by the author: division (qisma) and example (mithal). It begins by describing division: you distinguish knowledge from other beliefs by classifying belief into decisive vs. non-decisive, then decisive into correspondent vs. non-correspondent, then correspondent into stable vs. non-stable. The result is that knowledge is decisive, correspondent, and stable. This distinguishes it from opinion (which is non-decisive), compound ignorance (non-correspondent), and the correct belief of a follower of authority (decisive and correspondent but not stable). Then it gives two examples of knowledge: (1) knowledge is the perception of the inner eye similar to perception of the outer eye, (2) knowledge is like our belief that one is half of two. The passage then addresses an objection: some say this is far-fetched. But it argues that if division and example provide distinction of the essence of knowledge from other things, they suffice as a definition (ta'rif) and a limit (hadd), because here 'defining' only means 'recognizing' it, not a strict logical definition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A proper definition must have genus and differentia in the strict logical sense.
Correction: The author argues that any method that successfully distinguishes the essence of a thing from other things—such as division or example—is sufficient as a definition. The purpose here is recognition, not a perfect logical definition.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does division (qisma) distinguish knowledge from opinion (zann)?
A) By stability that does not disappear by doubt
B) By correspondence with reality
C) By decisiveness
D) By being a perception of the inner eye

Key Takeaway
Knowledge can be defined by systematically distinguishing it from other beliefs through division, or by analogy through example, as long as the method uniquely identifies the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you willing to examine every belief you hold about Allah and test it with the criteria of decisiveness, correspondence, and stability? Which belief would you be most afraid to put to this test—because it might be a comfortable taqlīd rather than certain knowledge? Commit this week to question one such belief and seek evidence to make it stable.
?
The passage says knowledge is distinguished from the belief of one who follows authority (taqlīd al-muṣīb) by stability that does not disappear by doubt. If a sincere doubter questioned your faith today, would your conviction remain unshaken, or would it crumble? What step can you take today to transform your belief from inherited imitation to certainty?
?
If knowledge is like the perception of the inner eye (as in the example), ask yourself: do you truly 'see' the truths of your faith with clarity, or are you just repeating words? Describe one moment when you felt that inner sight—when the reality of Allah, the Hereafter, or your purpose became as clear as seeing your hand in front of you. How can you cultivate more such moments?

Ghazali's Method: Division and Example for Defining Knowledge
Ghazali's method for defining knowledge using division and example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that a strict real definition of knowledge is difficult and how he uses division and example as alternative methods for identification.

Core Idea
Knowledge cannot be strictly defined, but can be understood through division and example.

Imam al-Ghazali, a towering figure in Islamic thought, acknowledged the difficulty of giving a precise, real definition of knowledge that captures its essence. This passage from Ghazali's al-Mustasfa reveals his practical approach: instead of a rigid definition, he proposes using division (taqsīm) to remove ambiguity and example (tamthīl) to convey the reality. The commentary clarifies that Ghazali's aim is identification (taʿrīf), not a strict logical definition. Let's explore why this matters and how these methods work.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall flow. Then read the translation. Notice how Ghazali first states the difficulty, then proposes two methods.
The passage includes both Ghazali's original words and commentator's clarifications.
2
Identify the problem with defining knowledge
Focus on Ghazali's statement that a precise definition including genus and differentia is difficult even for sensible things. Ask yourself: why would this be even more difficult for knowledge?
This sets the stage for why he seeks alternatives.
3
Understand the two methods: division and example
Find where Ghazali says that division cuts off ambiguity and example makes you understand the reality. Think about how dividing knowledge into categories (e.g., necessary, acquired) helps distinguish it, and how comparing it to visual perception clarifies its nature.
Notice that these methods do not give a full definition but serve identification.
4
Distinguish between strict definition and identification
The commentator emphasizes that Ghazali only means identification (taʿrīf), not a real definition (taḥdīd ḥaqīqī). Reflect on the difference: a real definition requires essential genus and differentia, while identification only needs to make the thing distinguishable.
This corrects a common misunderstanding that Ghazali denies defining knowledge outright.
5
Review the checkpoints
Answer the quiz question to check your understanding. Then consider the common misunderstanding listed: knowing that Ghazali allows identification despite denying strict definition is key.

The Second Observatory - On the Definition of Absolute Knowledge. Except for its definition (otherwise knowledge of it would not be obtained) of the essence of knowledge, because the attainment of knowledge of something must necessarily convey its distinction from other things, since it is impossible to obtain knowledge of it without its distinction. Know that Imam al-Ghazali, may God have mercy on him, stated in al-Mustasfa that it is difficult to define knowledge with a precise expression that includes the genus (jins) and the differentia (fasl) essential (dhatiyyan), for that is difficult in most things, even in most sensory perceptions, so how would it not be difficult in subtle perceptions? Then he said: The mentioned division (taqsīm) cuts off knowledge from the loci of ambiguity, and the analogy (tamthīl) by the perception of the eye makes you understand its reality. So the thing appeared, then it is a definer of it, and the condition of equality and its being a necessary clear attribute is only for its perfection; otherwise it would entail that logic is not the totality of the laws of acquisition. His saying: (He does not mean, in the third person) i.e., Ghazali does not mean by the definition anything other than identification (taʿrīf), since he built on something else that the way to know it is division and example; and if he had intended the real definition (taḥdīd ḥaqīqī), he would have had to say: the way to know it is the descriptive definition (rasm). His saying: (He does not mean by its definition anything other than its identification) there is no doubt that the point of dispute is the reality of knowledge, and for this reason he answered the argument of the group that claimed its necessity by saying that assent (taṣdīq) only depends on the conception (taṣawwur) of the two extremes in some way. His saying: (the genus and the differentia essential) he restricted the genus and the differentia to essential because the ancients used to call that which is shared a genus, like 'breathing' for animal, and that which distinguishes a differentia, like 'laughing' and 'speaking'. And by this it appears that understanding the real definition from Ghazali's statement in al-Mustasfa is not understanding the restricted from his speech that indicates that. This, and it may be said: The speech of the Imam in al-Burhan is explicit in intending the difficulty of definition absolutely, and there is no doubt that the doctrine of Ghazali and the Imam is one. And it is supported by their saying: The way to know it is division and example, for then the most apparent is that it should be said: The way to know it is the descriptive definition, without deviating from it if possible to what is not conventional. The bottom line is that preventing definition by expression and preventing descriptive definition by indication involves transmitting the descriptive definitions and invalidating them, then moving to the less conventional is open to reflection. His saying: (And the analogy by the perception of the eye makes you understand its reality) there is reflection in it, because making the reality understood in its essence is not obtained from the example, and its clarification is not confined to it, since it is obtained by division and other things, so there is no basis for specification. The Second Observatory - On the Definition of Absolute Knowledge: The fact that he only said: 'The real definition is difficult, not the identification absolutely,' and this is a verified statement with no objection, but it applies to things other than knowledge, as he acknowledged:
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage is an extended commentary on Imam al-Ghazali's approach to defining knowledge (ʿilm) and the methods of division (taqsīm) and example (tamthīl). The author begins by noting the importance of a definition that distinguishes the thing from others, then quotes Ghazali's statement from al-Mustasfa that a precise definition including genus and differentia is difficult even for sensible things, let alone subtle intellectual perceptions. Ghazali offers an alternative: division cuts off ambiguity and example (like visual perception) conveys the reality. The commentator clarifies that Ghazali uses 'definition' loosely to mean 'identification' (taʿrīf), not a strict real definition (taḥdīd ḥaqīqī). He explains that Ghazali's method is to use division and example because they serve to identify knowledge without meeting all conditions of a proper definition. The commentary then addresses the terms 'genus' and 'differentia' as used by ancient logicians, emphasizing that 'essential' restricts them to their technical sense. It discusses how division, by including common and distinctive elements, can yield definitions of categories, and example reduces to a descriptive definition (rasm). The author acknowledges arguments about Ghazali's consistency with other works like al-Burhan, concluding that Ghazali and the Imam (likely Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) agree that a real definition is difficult, and that the way to know knowledge is through division and example, though a descriptive definition is also possible if one rejects both precise definition and descriptive definition. The passage ends by critiquing the notion that example alone can convey the essence, as clarification comes through division as well.

Key Terms
تعريف (taʿrīf)
تحديد (taḥdīd)
تقسيم (taqsīm)
تمثيل (tamthīl)
الجنس (jins)
الفصل (fasl)
رسم (rasm)
العلم (ʿilm)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume that Ghazali rejects any definition of knowledge.
Correction: Ghazali only rejects the possibility of a strict real definition (taḥdīd ḥaqīqī). He accepts identification (taʿrīf) through division and example.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what method does Ghazali propose for understanding the reality of knowledge instead of a strict real definition?
A) Axiomatic demonstration
B) Division and example
C) Sensory intuition
D) Consensus of scholars

Key Takeaway
Ghazali argues that a strict real definition of knowledge is impossible, but we can identify it through division and example, which provide sufficient understanding.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If even a scholar like Ghazali admits that the true essence of knowledge cannot be captured in words, does that humble your own certainty when you claim to 'know' something? When you say 'I know this deen is true', are you aware that you are using indirect identification, not full comprehension? How can this awareness increase your humility and your trust in Allah, the All-Knowing, rather than your own intellect?
?
The passage says that division cuts off knowledge from 'loci of ambiguity'. What is one area of your religious understanding that feels ambiguous or confused? Can you apply Ghazali's method of careful division—separating what you truly know from what is unclear—to gain clarity? What practical step will you take today to distinguish between certain truth and mere assumption in that area?
?
Ghazali uses the example of sight to help understand knowledge, yet the commentator warns that an example never conveys the full reality. Think of a time you explained your faith to someone using an example or analogy (e.g., 'belief is like a seed'). Did that example give them a complete understanding, or did it risk being misleading? How can you more responsibly use analogies in your own da‘wah without oversimplifying or distorting the truth?

The Mu'tazilite Definition of Knowledge as Belief
Presents the Mu'tazilite definition of knowledge and its refinements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite definition of knowledge as 'belief in a thing as it is,' why it required the addition 'by necessity or proof,' and how commentators addressed objections regarding taqlid and probable belief.

Core Idea
Knowledge as belief is incomplete without the conditions of necessity or proof.

This passage presents the third doctrine on the nature of knowledge, attributing to some Mu'tazila the definition of knowledge as 'belief in a thing as it is.' The commentator immediately identifies problems: it includes blind acceptance (taqlid) and probable belief (ẓann). By examining the required refinements, we see how rational theology carefully delineates the boundaries of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the initial definition
Read the first sentence where the definition is given: 'It is belief in a thing as it is.' Notice how it seems straightforward.
This definition is attributed to some Mu'tazila.
2
Identify the problem with taqlid
The commentator points out that this definition is not exclusive because it includes taqlid (blind following) if the belief matches reality. Find the phrase 'غير مانع لدخول التقليد'.
The problem: a muqallid's belief may accidentally be true.
3
See the added condition
To exclude taqlid, the condition 'by necessity or proof' is added. Note how this attempts to fix the definition.
But even with this, probable true belief remains included.
4
Understand the restriction to certain belief
The final refinement is to restrict 'belief' (iʿtiqād) technically to certain belief (jāzim), excluding opinion (ẓann). This solves the remaining issue.
Thus knowledge becomes certain belief based on necessity or proof.
5
Examine the responses to further objections
Read the commentator's responses to objections about necessity being 'general' and the nature of reflection (naẓar). See how the definition is defended.
The correct answer distinguishes between the proof in itself and the muqallid's proof.

[ المذهب الثالث ] : أنه نظري لا يعسر تحديده ) وذكر له تعريفات الأول لبعض المعتزلة أنه اعتقاد الشيء على ما هو به (وهو) أي هذا التعريف (غير مانع لدخول التقليد فيه إذا طابق ( الواقع ( فزيد ) لدفعه ( عن ضرورة أو دليل) ، فاندفع دخول التقليد ( لكن بقي الاعتقاد الراجح المطابق أعني الظن الصادق الحاصل عن ضرورة أو دليل ظني داخلاً فيه إلا أن يخص الاعتقاد بالجازم اصطلاحاً ) فلا يدخل الظن فيه قوله : ( اعتقاد الشيء على ما هو به أي على وجه ذلك الشيء متلبس به في حد ذاته من الثبوت والانتفاء، والمراد بالشيء الموضوع أو النسبة الحكمية . قوله : ( عن ضرورة أو دليل أي كائناً ذلك الاعتقاد المطابق عن ضرورة أو دليل واعتقاد المقلد، وإن كان ناشئاً عن دليل لأن قول المقلد حجة للمقلل إلا أن مطابقته ليست ناشئة عن دليل بل اتفاقي ولذا يقلده فيما يصيب ويخطئ فاندفع ما تحير فيه الناظرون من أن التقليد إذا لم يكن عن ضرورة أو دليل يلزم أن يكون تقسيم العلم بمعنى الصورة الحاصلة إلى الضروري، والنظري غير حاصل لخروج التقليد، وتكلفوا الدفعة بما تمجه الأسماع . قوله فاندفع دخول التقليد فإن قلت : حصول مطلق الإدراك لا يخلو عن ضرورة أو نظر، ما بال التقليد خلا عنهما قلت أجيب بأن مبنى كلامه على أن المتبادر من التعريف الضرورة العامة فيخرج التقليد لأنه ليس عن ضرورة عامة، ولا عن دليل بنظرية المسألة في نفس الأمر، فإن قول المقلد ليس دليل المسألة في نفس الأمر، والمراد بالضرورة في قولهم لا يخلو عن ضرورة أو الضرورة المطلقة، وفيه أنه يخرج أكثر العلوم الضرورية إذ لا ضرورة عامة في الحدسيات والتجربيات مثلاً وأيضاً تخرج الإلهيات إلا أن لا يقول : المعتزلة بها، أو بعلميتها كعدم قولهم بعلم الله تعالى والصواب في الجواب أن يقال : اعتقاد المقلد نظري لان الدليل عنده قول المقلد كما صرح به في التوضيح، لكن قول المقلد : ليس الدليل الذي يستنبط منه التقليد الحكم في الواقع والمراد بالدليل هو الدليل في نفس الأمر بقرينة المقام، فيخرج التعريف، وأما المراد بالنظر في قولهم مطلق الإدراك لا يخلو عن ضرورة أو نظر، فهو النظر المطلق سواء كان صحيحاً أو فاسداً فلا محذور فتأمل.
[The Third Doctrine]: That it is theoretical (naẓarī) and its definition is not difficult. And definitions were mentioned for it. The first is by some Muʿtazila: that it is belief (iʿtiqād) in a thing as it is. (And it) i.e., this definition, (is not exclusive) because it includes taqlīd (blind following) if it matches (reality). (So it was increased) to repel it (by necessity or proof). Thus the inclusion of taqlīd was repelled. (But the predominant matching belief, i.e., true opinion (ẓann ṣādiq) obtained by necessity or probable proof, remains included in it, unless belief is restricted to certain (jāzim) terminology), so opinion does not enter into it. His saying: (belief in a thing as it is) i.e., in the mode of that thing as it is in itself in terms of affirmation or negation. And by 'thing' is meant the subject or the predicative relation. His saying: (by necessity or proof) i.e., that matching belief is by necessity or proof. And the belief of the muqallid, even if it arises from a proof because the statement of the muqallid is evidence for the muqallid, its match is not from proof but coincidental; hence he is followed in what he hits and misses. And thus was repelled what the inquirers were perplexed about: that if taqlīd is not by necessity or proof, then the division of knowledge (in the sense of the form obtained) into necessary (ḍarūrī) and theoretical (naẓarī) is not obtained because taqlīd is excluded, and they forced a repugnant answer. His saying: 'Thus the inclusion of taqlīd was repelled.' If you say: The attainment of absolute perception is not devoid of necessity or reflection, why is taqlīd free of them? I say: The answer is that his statement is based on the fact that the immediately understood from the definition is general necessity, so taqlīd is excluded because it is not from general necessity, nor from a proof based on the theoretical nature of the issue in itself. For the statement of the muqallid is not the proof of the issue in itself. And by 'necessity' in their saying 'it is not devoid of necessity or reflection' is meant absolute necessity. And there is in it that it excludes most necessary sciences, since there is no general necessity in, for example, intuitions (ḥadsiyyāt) and experiences (tajribiyyāt). Also, the divine sciences would be excluded, unless the Muʿtazila do not affirm them, or do not affirm their scientific status, like their not affirming God's knowledge. The correct answer is to say: The belief of the muqallid is theoretical because the proof for him is the statement of the muqallid, as stated in al-Tawḍīḥ. But the statement of the muqallid is not the proof from which the ruling in reality is derived, and what is meant by 'proof' is the proof in itself, by context of the situation, so the definition excludes. As for what is meant by 'reflection' in their saying 'absolute perception is not devoid of necessity or reflection,' it is absolute reflection, whether valid or invalid, so there is no problem. Reflect.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage presents the third doctrine (al-madhhab al-thālith) regarding the nature of knowledge (ʿilm): that it is theoretical (naẓarī) and can be defined without difficulty. It then gives the first definition from some Muʿtazila: knowledge is 'belief (iʿtiqād) in a thing as it is.' The commentator immediately raises an objection: this definition is not exclusive (ghayr māniʿ) because it includes taqlīd (blind acceptance) if the belief happens to match reality. To block this, they add the condition 'by necessity (ḍarūra) or proof (dalīl).' However, another problem remains: probable true belief (ẓann ṣādiq) obtained by necessity or probable proof would also be included. The solution is to restrict 'belief' (iʿtiqād) in technical terminology to certain (jāzim) belief, excluding opinion (ẓann). The commentator then explicates each phrase: 'belief in a thing as it is' means the belief corresponds to the thing's actual state, and 'thing' refers to the subject or the predicative relation. The condition 'by necessity or proof' is explained: the muqallid's belief, though coincidentally matching reality, does not arise from a proper proof, so taqlīd is excluded. The commentator addresses a further objection: if all perception involves necessity or reflection, then taqlīd would not be excluded. The answer is that the definition implicitly refers to 'general necessity' (ḍarūra ʿāmma), and the muqallid lacks that. But this would exclude many necessary sciences like intuitions and experiences. The correct answer is to consider the muqallid's belief as theoretical because he relies on the statement of his authority as proof, but that proof is not the true proof in itself, so the definition still excludes taqlīd. Finally, 'reflection' (naẓar) in the objection means absolute reflection, valid or invalid, so no contradiction arises.

اعتقاد (iʿtiqād)
تقليد (taqlīd)
ضرورة (ḍarūra)
دليل (dalīl)
ظن (ẓann)
جازم (jāzim)
نظري (naẓarī)
اعتقاد جازم (iʿtiqād jāzim)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilite definition 'belief in a thing as it is' is sufficient by itself.
Correction: The definition requires the addition 'by necessity or proof' and the restriction of 'belief' to certain belief to exclude taqlid and opinion.

Checkpoint

C) To include divine knowledge
D) To make it applicable to theoretical matters

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite definition of knowledge requires multiple refinements to exclude taqlid and opinion, ultimately equating knowledge with certain belief (i'tiqād jāzim) based on necessity or proof.

What Is Kalam?
beginner
5 min
The Mu'tazilite Definition and the Condition of Necessity or Proof
Presents the Mu'tazilite definition and its refinement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite definition of knowledge as belief in a thing as it is and why the condition 'from necessity or proof' was added to exclude blind following (taqlid).

Core Idea
Knowledge is not just correct belief; it must be based on necessity or proof.

The Mu'tazilites defined knowledge as belief in a thing as it is, but this definition was too broad. It included blind following (taqlid) if the belief happened to be correct. To fix this, they added the condition that the belief must arise from necessity or proof. This lesson explains that refinement.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how the definition is presented and then critiqued.
2
Identify the original definition
Find the Mu'tazilite definition of knowledge. It is 'belief in a thing as it is'. Note that this is a simple, direct definition.
3
Understand why it fails
The definition fails because it includes taqlid (blind following) when the belief happens to match reality. Think of an example: a person who follows a scholar blindly and believes something true without proper evidence.
Taqlid is not knowledge because it lacks a reliable basis.
4
See how the condition solves it
The condition 'from necessity or proof' excludes beliefs that are coincidentally correct. The belief must arise from immediate necessity or a valid proof. This removes taqlid because a taqlid belief is not based on the real proof of the matter.
5
Reflect on the meaning
Consider what 'necessity' and 'proof' mean. Necessity refers to self-evident truths, proof refers to valid reasoning. Together they ensure that knowledge is securely grounded.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - في تعريف مطلق العلم
أنه إنما قال : يعسر التحديد الحقيقي دون التعريف مطلقاً، وهذا كلام محقق لا بعد فيه لكنه جار في غير العلم كما اعترف به :
[ المذهب الثالث ] : أنه نظري لا يعسر تحديده ) وذكر له تعريفات الأول لبعض المعتزلة أنه اعتقاد الشيء على ما هو به (وهو) أي هذا التعريف (غير مانع لدخول التقليد فيه إذا طابق ( الواقع ( فزيد ) لدفعه ( عن ضرورة أو دليل) ، فاندفع دخول التقليد ( لكن بقي الاعتقاد الراجح المطابق أعني الظن الصادق الحاصل عن ضرورة أو دليل ظني داخلاً فيه إلا أن يخص الاعتقاد بالجازم اصطلاحاً ) فلا يدخل الظن فيه
قوله : ( فظهر أنه إنما قال : إلخ) لأن كلامه المنقول نص في ذلك، وما ذكر سابقاً ظااهر في إرادته التعريف مطلقاً، فيجب صرفه عن الظاهر بأن مراده، فطريق معرفته المتحقق المعول عليه القسمة والمثال، وإن كان يمكن معرفته بالرسم أيضاً إلا أنه لم يقع حيث ظهر فساد الرسوم التي ذكرها القوم .
قوله : ( اعتقاد الشيء على ما هو به أي على وجه ذلك الشيء متلبس به في حد ذاته من الثبوت والانتفاء، والمراد بالشيء الموضوع أو النسبة الحكمية .
قوله : ( عن ضرورة أو دليل أي كائناً ذلك الاعتقاد المطابق عن ضرورة أو دليل واعتقاد المقلد، وإن كان ناشئاً عن دليل لأن قول المقلد حجة للمقلل إلا أن مطابقته ليست ناشئة عن دليل بل اتفاقي ولذا يقلده فيما يصيب ويخطئ فاندفع ما تحير فيه الناظرون من أن التقليد إذا لم يكن عن ضرورة أو دليل يلزم أن يكون تقسيم العلم بمعنى الصورة الحاصلة إلى الضروري، والنظري غير حاصل لخروج التقليد، وتكلفوا الدفعة بما تمجه الأسماع .
قوله فاندفع دخول التقليد فإن قلت : حصول مطلق الإدراك لا يخلو عن ضرورة أو نظر، ما بال التقليد خلا عنهما قلت أجيب بأن مبنى كلامه على أن المتبادر من التعريف الضرورة العامة فيخرج التقليد لأنه ليس عن ضرورة عامة، ولا عن دليل بنظرية المسألة في نفس الأمر، فإن قول المقلد ليس دليل المسألة في نفس الأمر، والمراد بالضرورة في قولهم لا يخلو عن ضرورة أو الضرورة المطلقة، وفيه أنه يخرج أكثر العلوم الضرورية إذ لا ضرورة عامة في الحدسيات والتجربيات مثلاً وأيضاً تخرج الإلهيات إلا أن لا يقول : المعتزلة بها، أو بعلميتها كعدم قولهم بعلم الله تعالى والصواب في الجواب أن يقال : اعتقاد المقلد نظري لان الدليل عنده قول المقلد كما صرح به في التوضيح، لكن قول المقلد : ليس الدليل الذي يستنبط منه التقليد الحكم في الواقع والمراد بالدليل هو الدليل في نفس الأمر بقرينة المقام، فيخرج التعريف، وأما المراد بالنظر في قولهم مطلق الإدراك لا يخلو عن ضرورة أو نظر، فهو النظر المطلق سواء كان صحيحاً أو فاسداً فلا محذور فتأمل.
The second observatory - on the definition of absolute knowledge.
That he only said: 'Real definition is difficult without definition absolutely', and this is verified speech with no objection, but it applies to other than knowledge as he acknowledged.
[The third doctrine]: It is theoretical (naẓarī) and its definition is not difficult. Mentioned for it are definitions. The first by some Mu'tazila: It is belief (iʿtiqād) in a thing as it is. (And it) i.e. this definition (is not exclusive due to the entry of blind following (taqlīd) into it if it matches reality. So it is added) to repel it (from necessity or proof). So the entry of taqlīd was repelled. (But there remains the preponderant matching belief, meaning true opinion (ẓann ṣādiq) obtained from necessity or probable proof entering into it, unless belief is specified by decisive belief (jāzim) terminologically, so opinion does not enter it.)
His statement: (So it appears that he only said etc.) Because his transmitted speech is explicit in that, and what was mentioned previously is apparently intended for definition absolutely, so it must be diverted from the apparent meaning to the effect that his intention is... so the verified method relied upon for knowing it is division and example, although it can be known by description (rasm) also, but it did not occur when the corruption of the descriptions mentioned by the people became apparent.
His statement: (Belief in a thing as it is) i.e. in the manner that thing is clothed with in itself regarding affirmation or negation, and by 'thing' is meant the subject or the predicative relation.
His statement: (From necessity or proof) i.e. that matching belief being from necessity or proof. The belief of the blind follower (muqallid), even if it arises from a proof because the statement of the blind follower is evidence for the blind follower, but its matching is not from proof but coincidental, hence he follows him in what is correct and wrong. So was repelled what the viewers were confused about: that if taqlīd is not from necessity or proof, then the division of knowledge in the sense of the acquired form into necessary and theoretical would not hold due to the exclusion of taqlīd, and they burdened themselves with a repulsive defense.
His statement: (So the entry of taqlīd was repelled). For you say: The occurrence of absolute perception is not free from necessity or view (naẓar), why is taqlīd lacking both? I say, it may be answered that his speech is based on that the understood from the definition is general necessity, so taqlīd is excluded because it is not from general necessity nor from proof in the theoreticality of the matter in itself, for the statement of the blind follower is not the proof of the matter in itself. And by necessity in their statement 'not free from necessity or view' is meant absolute necessity. But this would exclude most necessary sciences since there is no general necessity in intuitions and experimental matters for example, and also the divine matters would be excluded unless the Mu'tazila deny them or their being sciences like their denial of God's knowledge. The correct answer is to say: The belief of the blind follower is theoretical because the proof for him is the statement of the blind follower as clarified in al-Tawḍīḥ, but the statement of the blind follower is not the proof from which the blind follower derives the judgement in reality, and by 'proof' is meant the proof in itself by context, so the definition excludes it. As for what is meant by 'view' in their statement 'absolute perception is not free from necessity or view', it is absolute view, whether correct or corrupt, so there is no problem. So reflect.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage is a definition and refinement of the definition of knowledge. The author first notes the difficulty of real definition but then presents the third doctrine that definition is possible. The Mu'tazilite definition is given: 'belief in a thing as it is.' The author immediately critiques it as not exclusive because it would include taqlid (blind following) if the belief happens to match reality, even if not based on proper evidence. To exclude taqlid, the condition 'from necessity or proof' is added, meaning the belief must arise from either immediate necessity or a valid proof. However, this still allows true opinion (ẓann ṣādiq) based on probable proof. To exclude that, 'belief' is specified as 'decisive belief' (jāzim) terminologically. The commentary then explains the reasoning: the phrase 'from necessity or proof' is meant to exclude taqlid because a taqlid belief—though it may have some proof (the authority's word)—is not based on the actual proof of the matter in itself; it is coincidental. The commentator also addresses a potential objection that all perception involves either necessity or view, so why is taqlid excluded? The answer is that the definition intends 'general necessity' (ḍarūra ʿāmma), not absolute necessity, and taqlid lacks that. However, using 'general necessity' would exclude many necessary sciences (like intuitions and experiments) and also divine matters unless the Mu'tazila deny them. The correct answer is that taqlid is actually theoretical (naẓarī) because its proof is the authority's word, but that proof is not the real proof of the matter; the definition's 'proof' refers to the real proof. Thus taqlid is excluded. The passage then notes that 'view' (naẓar) in the objection means absolute view, so there is no inconsistency. The lesson concludes with an invitation to reflect.

Key Terms
اعتقاد (iʿtiqād)
تقليد (taqlīd)
ضرورة (ḍarūra)
دليل (dalīl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that simply having a correct belief is enough for knowledge.
Correction: The passage shows that correct belief alone is insufficient; it must be grounded in necessity or proof to be knowledge.

Checkpoint
What condition was added to the Mu'tazilite definition of knowledge to exclude taqlid?
A) From necessity or proof
B) From tradition (naql)
C) From consensus (ijmāʿ)
D) From intuition (ḥads)

Key Takeaway
Knowledge requires a correct belief that is grounded in necessity or proof, not just any correct belief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you hold any beliefs about your faith simply because you were taught them, without ever examining the proof? Will you honestly identify one such belief today and seek its proof?
?
When you affirm your beliefs, can you sincerely say they are built on necessity or proof, or are you relying on comfortable familiarity? What would it cost you to lose that comfort and demand proof for your conviction?
?
If you have been following a scholar or teacher in matters of aqeedah without understanding the evidence, are you content with taqlid—or will you take a step to learn the proof behind what you believe, starting with this lesson?

What Is Kalam?
intermediate
6 min
Refining the Definition: Excluding True Opinion and Clarifying Necessity
Refines the definition further to exclude true opinion and clarifies the meaning of necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite definition needed to be further refined to exclude true opinion (ẓann ṣādiq) and how the concept of 'necessity' is understood in this context.

Core Idea
True opinion, even if based on probable proof, is not knowledge unless it is decisive belief.

Even after adding 'from necessity or proof', the definition still included true opinion (ẓann ṣādiq) that arises from probable proof. To exclude this, the term 'belief' was specified as decisive belief (jāzim). Additionally, the passage discusses what 'necessity' means—general necessity or absolute necessity—to avoid excluding many valid sciences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two issues addressed: true opinion and the meaning of necessity.
2
Understand the problem with true opinion
The refined definition still includes true opinion (ẓann ṣādiq) that comes from probable proof. True opinion is not certain; it is merely preponderant belief. Knowledge requires certainty.
True opinion is not decisive belief (jāzim).
3
See the solution: specify 'decisive belief'
The author says that if we restrict 'belief' terminologically to 'decisive belief' (jāzim), then true opinion is excluded. This shows that knowledge must be certain and firm, not just probable.
4
Analyze the discussion on necessity
The passage then examines the condition 'from necessity or proof'. Some objected that taqlid might still be considered theoretical if the follower's proof is the authority's word. But the real proof is the proof in itself, so taqlid is excluded. Also, 'necessity' is clarified as 'general necessity' (ḍarūra ʿāmma), not absolute necessity, to avoid excluding fields like intuitions and experiments.
General necessity means what is necessary for everyone, not just the individual.
5
Reflect on the overall refinement
The definition now reads: 'Decisive belief in a thing as it is from general necessity or a real proof.' This ensures that knowledge is both certain and properly grounded.

المرصد الثاني - في تعريف مطلق العلم
أنه إنما قال : يعسر التحديد الحقيقي دون التعريف مطلقاً، وهذا كلام محقق لا بعد فيه لكنه جار في غير العلم كما اعترف به :
[ المذهب الثالث ] : أنه نظري لا يعسر تحديده ) وذكر له تعريفات الأول لبعض المعتزلة أنه اعتقاد الشيء على ما هو به (وهو) أي هذا التعريف (غير مانع لدخول التقليد فيه إذا طابق ( الواقع ( فزيد ) لدفعه ( عن ضرورة أو دليل) ، فاندفع دخول التقليد ( لكن بقي الاعتقاد الراجح المطابق أعني الظن الصادق الحاصل عن ضرورة أو دليل ظني داخلاً فيه إلا أن يخص الاعتقاد بالجازم اصطلاحاً ) فلا يدخل الظن فيه
قوله : ( فظهر أنه إنما قال : إلخ) لأن كلامه المنقول نص في ذلك، وما ذكر سابقاً ظااهر في إرادته التعريف مطلقاً، فيجب صرفه عن الظاهر بأن مراده، فطريق معرفته المتحقق المعول عليه القسمة والمثال، وإن كان يمكن معرفته بالرسم أيضاً إلا أنه لم يقع حيث ظهر فساد الرسوم التي ذكرها القوم .
قوله : ( اعتقاد الشيء على ما هو به أي على وجه ذلك الشيء متلبس به في حد ذاته من الثبوت والانتفاء، والمراد بالشيء الموضوع أو النسبة الحكمية .
قوله : ( عن ضرورة أو دليل أي كائناً ذلك الاعتقاد المطابق عن ضرورة أو دليل واعتقاد المقلد، وإن كان ناشئاً عن دليل لأن قول المقلد حجة للمقلل إلا أن مطابقته ليست ناشئة عن دليل بل اتفاقي ولذا يقلده فيما يصيب ويخطئ فاندفع ما تحير فيه الناظرون من أن التقليد إذا لم يكن عن ضرورة أو دليل يلزم أن يكون تقسيم العلم بمعنى الصورة الحاصلة إلى الضروري، والنظري غير حاصل لخروج التقليد، وتكلفوا الدفعة بما تمجه الأسماع .
قوله فاندفع دخول التقليد فإن قلت : حصول مطلق الإدراك لا يخلو عن ضرورة أو نظر، ما بال التقليد خلا عنهما قلت أجيب بأن مبنى كلامه على أن المتبادر من التعريف الضرورة العامة فيخرج التقليد لأنه ليس عن ضرورة عامة، ولا عن دليل بنظرية المسألة في نفس الأمر، فإن قول المقلد ليس دليل المسألة في نفس الأمر، والمراد بالضرورة في قولهم لا يخلو عن ضرورة أو الضرورة المطلقة، وفيه أنه يخرج أكثر العلوم الضرورية إذ لا ضرورة عامة في الحدسيات والتجربيات مثلاً وأيضاً تخرج الإلهيات إلا أن لا يقول : المعتزلة بها، أو بعلميتها كعدم قولهم بعلم الله تعالى والصواب في الجواب أن يقال : اعتقاد المقلد نظري لان الدليل عنده قول المقلد كما صرح به في التوضيح، لكن قول المقلد : ليس الدليل الذي يستنبط منه التقليد الحكم في الواقع والمراد بالدليل هو الدليل في نفس الأمر بقرينة المقام، فيخرج التعريف، وأما المراد بالنظر في قولهم مطلق الإدراك لا يخلو عن ضرورة أو نظر، فهو النظر المطلق سواء كان صحيحاً أو فاسداً فلا محذور فتأمل.

?

The Objection That Knowledge Excludes the Impossible
Presents the objection and a weak defense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection against the Mu'tazilite definition of knowledge that it excludes knowledge of the impossible, and the initial response that knowledge does not attach to the impossible.

Core Idea
The definition fails if it cannot account for knowledge of the impossible.

This lesson presents the objection that the Mu'tazilite definition of knowledge as 'belief in a thing as it is' excludes knowledge of the impossible, because the impossible is not considered a 'thing' by consensus. Some defenders claim that knowledge cannot attach to the impossible, so there is no refutation. We will examine this claim and set the stage for the author's rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the objection and the initial answer. Then read the translation.
Focus on the terms 'shayʾ' (thing) and 'mumtaniʿ' (impossible).
2
Identify the objection
The objection states that the definition excludes knowledge of the impossible because the impossible is not a thing. Note that this relies on a specific meaning of 'thing'.
3
Understand the initial answer
Some defenders of the definition respond by claiming that knowledge does not attach to the impossible, so the definition is not refuted by this exclusion.
The author indicates he will refute this answer.
4
Prepare for the rebuttal
Observe that the author points to a rebuttal with 'fa-ashāra ilā raddihi bi-qawlihi' (so he indicated his refutation by his statement). This sets up the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( ويرد عليهم ) أي على أصحاب هذا التعريف ) خروج العلم بالمستحيل عنه فإنه ليس شيئاً اتفاقاً بخلاف informations الممكنة التي اختلف فيها، وقد أجاب بعضهم عن هذا بأن العلم لا يتعلق بالمستحيل فلا نقض به فأشار إلى رده بقوله ( ومن أنكر تعلق العلم بالمستحيل فهو مكابر لبديهة العقل فإن كل عاقل يجد من نفسه الحكم باستحالة اجتماع الضدين ،والنقيضين ولا يتصور ذلك إلا مع كون اجتماعهما المستحيل معلوماً بوجه ما ( ومناقض) لكلامه أيضاً ( لأن هذا) أي إنكاره تعلق العلم بالمستحيل ) حكم ) على المستحيل بأنه لا يعلم (فيستدعي) هذا الحكم
قوله : (خروج العلم الخ يعني أن الظاهر أن المراد بالشيء ما هو المصطلح، لأنه المعنى الحقيقي عندهم فيلزم خروج العلم التصديقي المتعلق بالمستحيل كالعلم بأن النقيضين يستحيل اجتماعهما، وبأن شريك الباري محال سواء أريد بالشيء في تعريف العلم الموضوع أو النسبة لأن النسبة إلى المستحيل مستحيل أيضاً لامتناع ثبوت النسبة في الخارج مع عدم ثبوت المنسوب إليه
قوله : ( فإن كل عاقل الخ) يعني إنه يتعلق به العلم التصديقي، وهذا الحكم تصديقي يقيني ناشئ عن الضرورة متعلق بالموضوع والنسبة المستحيل واستحالتها بمعنى امتناع وجودها في الخارج لا ينافي مطابقتها للواقع، فما قيل إن أراد أن إنكار تعلق العلم التصديقي بالمستحيل، كقولنا : اجتماع النقيضين واقع وارتفاع النقيضين واقع مثلاً مكابرة، فهو باطل قطعاً إذ الإدراك المتعلق به جهل لا علم وإن أراد به تعلق العلم التصوري فمسلم، لكن لا جهة لتخصيص الاعتراض بخروج العلم بالمستحيل إذ مطلق التصوري خارج عنه، وأيضاً يصير قوله : نعم قد يعتذر الخ، باطلاً إذ على تقدير تسمية المستحيل شيئاً لا يدخل العلم به، أعني تصوره في التعريف لأنه ليس باعتقاد فمنشؤه عدم الفرق بين استحالة النسبة وصدقها فتدبر. قوله: (ولا يتصور إلخ ذكر استطرادي للمبالغة في الرد على من أنكر تعلق العلم بالمستحيل بإثبات تعلق نوعيه به وإلا فلا دخل له في النقض .
قوله : ( ومن أنكر تعلق العلم بالمستحيل قال الأستاذ المحقق إن أراد أن إنكار تعلق العلم التصديقي بالمستحيل كقوله : اجتماع النقيضين واقع وارتفاع النقيضين واقع مثلاً مكابرة فهو باطل قطعا، إذ الإدراك المتعلق به جهل لا علم وإن أراد تعلق العلم التصوري كما يظهر من كلامه وكلام الشارح فمسلم لكن لا جهة لتخصيص الاعتراض بخروج العلم بالمستحيل، إذ مطلق التصور خارج عنه كما صرح به الشارح آخراً وأيضاً يصير قوله : نعم قد يعتذر لهم إلخ باطلاً، إذ على تقدير تسمية المستحيل شيئاً لا يدخل العلم به أعني تصوره في التعريف لأنه ليس باعتقاد لا يقال : قولهم اعتقاد الشيء على ما هو عليه معناه اعتقاد المحكوم عليه على ما هو به من الحكم، وحينئذ لا يصدق اجتماع النقيضين بأنه محال إلا بالاعتذار المذكور، لأنا
(And it is objected against them) i.e. the proponents of this definition (the exclusion of knowledge of the impossible (al-mumtaniʿ) from it, because it is not a thing (shayʾ) by agreement, unlike possible informations which are disputed. And some of them answered this by saying that knowledge does not attach to the impossible, so no refutation. So he pointed to its refutation by his statement: (And whoever denies the attachment of knowledge to the impossible, he is a stubborn denier of the obvious of the intellect, for every rational person finds in himself the judgement of the impossibility of the combination of two opposites and two contradictories, and that cannot be conceived except with their impossible combination being known in some way.) (And it contradicts) his statement also (because this) i.e. his denial of the attachment of knowledge to the impossible (is a judgement) on the impossible that it is not known (so this judgement necessitates...)
His statement: (Exclusion of knowledge etc.) means that the apparent meaning is that by 'thing' is meant the conventional thing (al-shayʾ al-muṣṭalaḥ), because it is the true meaning according to them, so it entails the exclusion of assertoric knowledge (al-ʿilm al-taṣdīqī) related to the impossible, such as knowledge that the two contradictories cannot combine, and that a partner of God is impossible, whether by 'thing' in the definition of knowledge is meant the subject or the relation, because the relation to the impossible is also impossible, due to the impossibility of the relation's existence in the external while the related does not exist.
His statement: (for every rational person etc.) means that assertoric knowledge attaches to it, and this judgement is certain assertoric knowledge arising from necessity, related to the subject and the impossible relation, and its impossibility meaning the impossibility of its existence in the external does not contradict its correspondence to reality. So what was said—that if he meant that the denial of assertoric knowledge of the impossible, like our saying 'the combination of two opposites is real and the removal of two opposites is real' for example is stubbornness—it is absolutely false, because the perception related to it is ignorance, not knowledge. And if he meant by it the attachment of conceptual knowledge (al-ʿilm al-taṣawwurī), then it is conceded, but there is no reason to specify the objection to the exclusion of knowledge of the impossible, since all conceptual knowledge is outside it. And also, his statement 'Yes, they may apologize' etc. becomes false, because on the assumption that the impossible is called a thing, knowledge of it—i.e. its conceptualization—does not enter the definition because it is not belief. So its source is the lack of distinction between the impossibility of the relation and its truthfulness. So reflect. His statement: (And it cannot be conceived etc.) is a digression to exaggerate in refuting whoever denies the attachment of knowledge to the impossible by proving the attachment of both types of knowledge to it; otherwise it has no bearing on the refutation.
His statement: (And whoever denies the attachment of knowledge to the impossible) The distinguished professor said: If he meant that the denial of assertoric knowledge of the impossible, like his saying 'the combination of two opposites is real and the removal of two opposites is real' for example is stubbornness, then it is absolutely false, because the perception related to it is ignorance, not knowledge. And if he meant the attachment of conceptual knowledge, as appears from his statement and the commentator's statement, then it is conceded, but there is no reason to specify the objection to the exclusion of knowledge of the impossible, since all conceptual knowledge is outside it, as the commentator stated later. Also, his statement 'Yes, they may apologize' etc. becomes false, because on the assumption that the impossible is called a thing, knowledge of it—i.e. its conceptualization—does not enter the definition because it is not belief. It cannot be said: Their statement 'belief in a thing as it is' means belief in the thing judged about as it is in terms of the judgement, and then the statement 'the combination of two opposites is impossible' would not be true except with the mentioned apology. For we...
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer concerning the Mu'tazilite definition of knowledge. The objection is that the definition excludes knowledge of the impossible (al-mumtaniʿ), because the impossible is not considered a 'thing' (shayʾ) by agreement. Some defenders of the definition respond by claiming knowledge cannot attach to the impossible, so there is no refutation. The author vigorously refutes this, calling such denial sheer stubbornness (mukābara). He argues that every rational person knows the impossibility of two opposites coexisting—this is knowledge of the impossible. Moreover, the denial itself is a judgement about the impossible, which would require knowledge of it. The commentary then explains the two types of knowledge: assertoric (taṣdīqī) and conceptual (taṣawwurī). The objection concerns assertoric knowledge, such as knowing that the combination of two contradictories is impossible. The denial that such knowledge exists is false because it is a certain judgement arising from necessity. If the defender means conceptual knowledge, that is conceded, but the objection is about assertoric, not conceptual, knowledge. The commentary also notes that the definition's term 'belief' (iʿtiqād) implies assertoric knowledge, so conceptual knowledge of the impossible (like simply conceiving the impossible) is outside the definition anyway. The passage concludes with a note that some statements are digressions for emphasis, and that the objection is only coherent if one distinguishes between the impossibility of the relation and its truthfulness.

Key Terms
مستحيل (mumtaniʿ)
شيء (shayʾ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that knowledge can only be of existing things and therefore the impossible cannot be known.
Correction: The passage clarifies that knowledge includes judgements about impossibilities, which are genuine knowledge even though the subject matter does not exist.

Checkpoint
What is the main objection presented in this passage?
A) The definition of knowledge is too narrow.
B) The definition excludes knowledge of the impossible because the impossible is not a thing.
C) The definition includes too many things.
D) The definition is based on consensus.

Key Takeaway
The objection argues that the Mu'tazilite definition fails because it cannot account for knowledge of the impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever defined something so narrowly that it excluded a truth you knew deep down? How did you reconcile that contradiction?
?
When you encounter a definition or belief system that cannot account for a known reality, what is your first instinct: to abandon the definition, or to defend it with a weak excuse? Which path leads to truth?
?
Consider a time you defended a flawed position because you were committed to it. In light of this lesson, what would it take for you to honestly admit a definition is incomplete and seek a better one?

The Rebuttal: Knowledge of the Impossible Is Necessary
Refutes denial of knowledge of the impossible and distinguishes knowledge types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's refutation that knowledge of the impossible is a rational necessity, and the distinction between assertoric (taṣdīqī) and conceptual (taṣawwurī) knowledge.

Core Idea
Every rational person knows the impossibility of opposites; this is knowledge of the impossible.

In this lesson, we examine the author's powerful rebuttal: denying knowledge of the impossible is intellectual stubbornness. The commentary clarifies that assertoric knowledge (such as knowing that two opposites cannot coexist) is genuine knowledge, whereas conceptual knowledge (simply conceiving the impossible) is a different type. We will explore these distinctions and see how they defend the definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the rebuttal passage
Read the Arabic passage from 'wa-man ankara...' to the end of the paragraph. Then read the translation.
Pay attention to the terms 'taṣdīqī' and 'taṣawwurī'.
2
Understand the rational intuition argument
The author argues that every rational person finds in themselves the judgement (ḥukm) of the impossibility of two opposites. This is a clear example of assertoric knowledge of the impossible.
3
Distinguish assertoric and conceptual knowledge
The commentary explains that if the defender meant knowledge as conceptual (taṣawwurī), then it is conceded but irrelevant because the definition uses 'belief' (iʿtiqād) which implies assertoric (taṣdīqī) knowledge. Therefore, the objection only applies to assertoric knowledge, which the author proves is possible.
4
See the conclusion
The passage concludes that the denial of knowledge of the impossible is itself a judgement that presupposes such knowledge, exposing the flaw in the defense.

The Objection of Knowledge of the Impossible and Its Refutation
Al-Jurjani refutes the claim that knowledge of the impossible escapes the definition of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Jurjani refutes the objection that knowledge of the impossible is not knowledge by demonstrating that it is a necessary judgment of the intellect and that denying it is self-contradictory.

Core Idea
The judgment of impossibility is itself certain knowledge, so the impossible is known.

In this passage, Al-Jurjani addresses a critical objection to the definition of knowledge as belief of a thing as it is. The objectors argue that knowledge of the impossible (e.g., that two opposites cannot coexist) is not knowledge of a thing, since the impossible is not a thing. Some defenders of the definition claim that knowledge does not pertain to the impossible at all. Al-Jurjani refutes this by appealing to intellectual intuition and logical consistency, showing that such a denial is both obstinate and self-contradictory.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the objection and the two refutations.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Understand the objection
Identify the objection: knowledge of the impossible escapes the definition because the impossible is not a thing. Note the attempted answer: knowledge does not pertain to the impossible.
3
Analyze the first refutation
Al-Jurjani says denying knowledge of the impossible is obstinate because every rational person necessarily judges the impossibility of two opposites. This judgment is assenting knowledge.
This appeals to intellectual intuition (badiha).
4
Analyze the second refutation
The denial itself is a judgment about the impossible, which requires knowledge of it. Thus, the claim is self-contradictory.
5
Distinguish between types of knowledge
The passage clarifies that assenting knowledge (tasdiqi) pertains to the impossible, while conceptual knowledge (tasawwuri) is excluded. Understand why this distinction matters.
This reinforces the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويرد عليهم) أي على أصحاب هذا التعريف) خروج العلم بالمستحيل عنه فإنه ليس شيئاً اتفاقاً بخلاف المعلومات الممكنة التي اختلف فيها، وقد أجاب بعضهم عن هذا بأن العلم لا يتعلق بالمستحيل فلا نقض به فأشار إلى رده بقوله ( ومن أنكر تعلق العلم بالمستحيل فهو مكابر لبديهة العقل فإن كل عاقل يجد من نفسه الحكم باستحالة اجتماع الضدين ،والنقيضين ولا يتصور ذلك إلا مع كون اجتماعهما المستحيل معلوماً بوجه ما ( ومناقض) لكلامه أيضاً ( لأن هذا) أي إنكاره تعلق العلم بالمستحيل ) حكم ) على المستحيل بأنه لا يعلم (فيستدعي) هذا الحكم العلم به لامتناع الحكم على ما ليس معلوماً أصلاً. قوله : (خروج العلم إلخ يعني أن الظاهر أن المراد بالشيء ما هو المصطلح، لأنه المعنى الحقيقي عندهم فيلزم خروج العلم التصديقي المتعلق بالمستحيل كالعلم بأن النقيضين يستحيل اجتماعهما، وبأن شريك الباري محال سواء أريد بالشيء في تعريف العلم الموضوع أو النسبة لأن النسبة إلى المستحيل مستحيل أيضاً لامتناع ثبوت النسبة في الخارج مع عدم ثبوت المنسوب إليه. قوله : ( فإن كل عاقل الخ) يعني إنه يتعلق به العلم التصديقي، وهذا الحكم تصديقي يقيني ناشئ عن الضرورة متعلق بالموضوع والنسبة المستحيل واستحالتها بمعنى امتناع وجودها في الخارج لا ينافي مطابقتها للواقع، فما قيل إن أراد أن إنكار تعلق العلم التصديقي بالمستحيل، كقولنا : اجتماع النقيضين واقع وارتفاع النقيضين واقع مثلاً مكابرة، فهو باطل قطعاً إذ الإدراك المتعلق به جهل لا علم وإن أراد به تعلق العلم التصوري فمسلم، لكن لا جهة لتخصيص الاعتراض بخروج العلم بالمستحيل إذ مطلق التصوري خارج عنه، وأيضاً يصير قوله : نعم قد يعتذر الخ، باطلاً إذ على تقدير تسمية المستحيل شيئاً لا يدخل العلم به، أعني تصوره في التعريف لأنه ليس باعتقاد فمنشؤه عدم الفرق بين استحالة النسبة وصدقها فتدبر. قوله: (ولا يتصور إلخ ذكر استطرادي للمبالغة في الرد على من أنكر تعلق العلم بالمستحيل بإثبات تعلق نوعيه به وإلا فلا دخل له في النقض .
And it is objected against them (i.e., against the proponents of this definition) that knowledge of the impossible escapes it, for it is not a thing by agreement, unlike possible known things about which there is disagreement. Some of them answered this by saying that knowledge does not pertain to the impossible, so it is not a refutation. So [the author] pointed to its refutation by his saying: 'And whoever denies that knowledge pertains to the impossible is obstinately opposing the primordial intuition of the intellect, for every rational person finds in himself the judgment of the impossibility of the conjunction of two opposites and two contradictories, and that is not conceivable except with the impossible conjunction being known in some way.' And it is also contradictory to his own statement because this (i.e., his denial that knowledge pertains to the impossible) is a judgment upon the impossible that it is not known, so this judgment necessitates knowledge of it, for judgment upon what is not known at all is impossible. His saying: ('Escapes of knowledge' etc.) means that it is apparent that what is meant by 'thing' is the technical term, because it is the real meaning according to them, so it follows that assenting knowledge pertaining to the impossible is excluded, such as knowledge that the conjunction of two opposites is impossible, and that a partner to the Creator is impossible, whether by 'thing' in the definition of knowledge is meant the subject or the relation, because the relation to the impossible is also impossible due to the impossibility of the subsistence of the relation in external existence without the subsistence of that to which it is related. His saying: ('For every rational person' etc.) means that assenting knowledge pertains to it, and this judgment is certain assenting knowledge arising from necessity, pertaining to the impossible subject and relation, and its impossibility meaning the impossibility of its existence in external existence does not contradict its correspondence to reality. So what has been said — that if he intended that the denial of assenting knowledge pertaining to the impossible, like our saying 'the conjunction of two opposites is actual' or 'the absence of two opposites is actual' for example, is obstinate denial — it is absolutely false, for the perception pertaining to it is ignorance, not knowledge. And if he intended the pertaining of conceptual knowledge, then it is accepted, but there is no reason to specify the objection to the exclusion of knowledge of the impossible, for conceptual knowledge in general is excluded from it. Also his saying 'Yes, one may excuse them' etc. becomes false, for upon the assumption of naming the impossible a thing, knowledge of it, i.e., its concept, is not included in the definition because it is not a belief. So its source is the failure to distinguish between the impossibility of the relation and its truth. So reflect. His saying: ('And it is not conceivable' etc.) is a digression for exaggeration in refuting whoever denies that knowledge pertains to the impossible, by proving that both types of knowledge pertain to it, otherwise it has no bearing on the refutation.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection raised against the definition of knowledge (as belief of a thing as it is). The objectors say that knowledge of the impossible (e.g., that two opposites cannot coexist) is not knowledge of a thing, since the impossible is not a thing, so it is excluded from the definition; yet such knowledge is clearly valid. Some defenders of the definition replied that knowledge does not pertain to the impossible at all, so there is no refutation. The author refutes that reply in two ways. First, the denial is obstinate (mukabara) because every rational person necessarily judges that two opposites cannot coexist, and this judgment itself is knowledge — it is a certain assenting judgment arising from necessity. Second, the denial is self-contradictory: in saying 'the impossible is not known', one is making a judgment about the impossible, which presupposes knowledge of it. The commentary then explains that the impossible is known by assenting knowledge (tasdiqi) and that this judgment is certain and corresponds to reality. It clarifies that if the objector meant assenting knowledge, his denial is false; if he meant conceptual knowledge (tasawwuri), then it is true that such knowledge is not included in the definition, but then the objection is not specific to the impossible because all conceptual knowledge is excluded. The author also notes that the excuse that the impossible is called a thing linguistically does not help, because even if it is called a thing, its conceptual knowledge is not a belief and thus still excluded. The digression about both types of knowledge pertaining to the impossible reinforces the refutation.

Key Terms
العلم بالمستحيل
العلم التصديقي
العلم التصوري
البديهة العقلية
المستحيل
المكابرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the impossible is not known at all, or that knowledge of the impossible is not truly knowledge.
Correction: The rational judgment of impossibility (e.g., that two opposites cannot coexist) is itself certain assenting knowledge, so the impossible is known.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the claim that knowledge of the impossible is not knowledge?
A) By saying it is conceptual knowledge only.
B) By showing that the judgment of impossibility is a necessary assenting knowledge.
C) By denying that the impossible can be known.
D) By redefining the term 'thing'.

Key Takeaway
The impossible is known through the necessary judgment of impossibility, and denying this is both obstinate and self-contradictory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever obstinately denied a truth that your own mind finds impossible to escape? What was that truth, and why did you resist it? Will you now surrender to it?
?
When you judge something as impossible—like Allah having a partner—do you realize that this very judgment is a form of knowledge? Does that awareness increase your certainty? If not, what is holding you back from conviction?
?
Is there any area of your life where you are making a judgment about something you claim not to know? How is that self-deception? Will you confront it today?
