The Linguistic Excuse for the Impossible and Its Weakness
Al-Jurjani critiques the linguistic excuse for the impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the linguistic excuse for including the impossible in the definition of knowledge is invalid, because it uses metaphorical language without contextual evidence and contradicts the position of the People of Truth.

Core Idea
Definitions require literal terms, not metaphors.

Al-Jurjani now addresses a possible objection to the previous criticism. Some might argue that the impossible is called a 'thing' in everyday language, so the Mu'tazila definition of knowledge—as encompassing both possible and impossible—stands. But this excuse fails, as we'll see, because definitions need precise, non-metaphorical language. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the excuse
Read the first part of the passage: 'Yes, they may be excused ... by saying that the impossible is called a thing in language'. This is the argument that the impossible is a 'thing' linguistically, so knowledge of it is not excluded from the definition.
Notice that this excuse tries to salvage the Mu'tazila definition.
2
Identify the proponents
Al-Jurjani notes that this view is held by the Mu'tazila, as stated by al-Zamakhshari. Recognizing who holds this position is key to understanding the theological context.
The Mu'tazila are known for their rationalist approach.
3
Recognize the weakness
The passage points out that the excuse uses metaphorical meaning (majaz) in a definition without any contextual clue (qarina). In technical definitions, even literal linguistic meanings become metaphorical when used in a technical context, so using metaphor is impermissible.
A definition must be clear and literal to avoid ambiguity.
4
Note the counter from Ahl al-Haqq
The People of Truth (Ahl al-Haqq) hold that linguists do not apply the term 'thing' to the non-existent. This directly contradicts the claim that the impossible is called a 'thing' in language.
This shows the linguistic premise of the excuse is contested.
5
Conclude
Al-Jurjani concludes that carrying the naming metaphorically is incompatible with the context of definition, so the excuse is weak.
Thus, the original objection to the Mu'tazila definition stands.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نعم قد يعتذر) لهم (بأن المستحيل يُسمى شيئاً لغة فلا يخرج العلم به عن تعريفهم (وكونه ليس بشيء بمعنى أنه غير ثابت في نفسه لا يمنع ذلك ) أي كونه شيئاً لغة. قوله : ( نعم قد يعتذر (الخ فيه إشارة إلى ضعفه لأنه يلزم استعمال المجاز في التعريف من غير قرينة، لأن المعنى اللغوي سواء كان حقيقياً أو مجازياً معنى مجازي عند أهل الاصطلاح. قوله: (يسمى شيئاً لغة أي عند أصحاب هذا التعريف وهم المعتزلة، وقد صرح به صاحب الكشاف فلا يرد أن هذا مخالف لما صرح به في بحث الوجود من أن أهل اللغة لا يطلقون الشيء على المعدوم، لأنه مذهب أهل الحق وحمل التسمية على الإطلاق المجازي يأباه مقام التعريف .
Yes, they may be excused (i.e., the proponents of the above definition) by saying that the impossible is called a thing in language, so knowledge of it is not excluded from their definition. And its not being a thing in the sense that it is not established in itself does not prevent that (i.e., its being a thing in language). His saying: ('Yes, they may be excused' etc.) contains an indication of its weakness because it necessitates the use of metaphor in a definition without any contextual clue, for the linguistic meaning, whether it is literal or metaphorical, is a metaphorical meaning according to the technical usage. His saying: ('It is called a thing in language') i.e., according to the proponents of this definition, who are the Mu'tazila; and the author of al-Kashshaf has stated it. So it is not objected that this contradicts what was stated in the discussion of existence that the people of language do not apply 'thing' to the non-existent, because that is the doctrine of the People of Truth, and carrying the naming metaphorically is incompatible with the context of definition.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage deals with a possible excuse for the first objection. The excuse is that the impossible is called a thing in language (i.e., Arabs use the word 'thing' for the impossible), so knowledge of it is not excluded from the definition. The author notes that the proponents of this excuse are the Mu'tazila, as stated by al-Zamakhshari in al-Kashshaf. However, the author points out a weakness: using a metaphorical meaning in a definition without evidence is invalid, because in technical terminology, even a literal linguistic meaning becomes metaphorical when used in a technical context. Furthermore, the People of Truth (Ahl al-Haqq) hold that linguists do not call the non-existent a thing, so the excuse relies on a contested linguistic claim. The context of definition requires precise literal terms, not metaphorical ones. Thus, the excuse is weak.

Key Terms
شيء لغة
المجاز
المعتزلة
أهل الحق
التعريف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any linguistic usage, even metaphorical, is acceptable in a definition as long as it is commonly understood.
Correction: Definitions require literal and unambiguous terms; metaphorical usage without contextual evidence makes the definition imprecise and invalid in technical contexts.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the linguistic excuse for the impossible invalid?
A) Because the impossible is never called a thing in language.
B) Because it uses metaphorical meaning in a definition without evidence.
C) Because the Mu'tazila do not accept this excuse.
D) Because the definition is too narrow.

Key Takeaway
The linguistic excuse for including the impossible in the definition of knowledge fails because it uses metaphor without context and contradicts the position of Ahl al-Haqq.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you define your beliefs—whether about God or about life—do you ever rely on comfortable, imprecise language that avoids the hard literal truth? What would it cost you to speak with exactness?
?
This lesson shows that even a small looseness in language can weaken a whole definition. Where in your own understanding of faith have you allowed a 'metaphor without context' to stand in for a rigorous truth? Will you examine it today?
?
The Mu'tazila used a linguistic excuse to include the impossible in their definition. Are there any excuses you make for holding onto beliefs that, at their core, do not align with the literal teachings of the Qur'an and Sunnah? Will you let them go?

Objection: Divine Knowledge Excluded
Al-Jurjani presents the first objection and discusses its implications for different schools.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that al-Baqillani's definition of knowledge excludes divine knowledge and how this objection is answered differently by the Mu'tazila and al-Razi.

Core Idea
A definition of knowledge that excludes God's knowledge cannot be absolute.

Al-Jurjani raises a powerful critique: al-Baqillani defines knowledge as 'ma'rifa of the known as it is', but God's knowledge is never called 'ma'rifa' by consensus. Since al-Baqillani affirms superadded knowledge for God, his definition fails to cover divine knowledge, revealing a flaw in the claim to an absolute definition. The commentary explains why the Mu'tazila and Imam al-Razi are not affected by this objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its translation, to understand the first objection: that the definition excludes God's knowledge.
Notice the phrase 'فيخرج عن حده علم الله سبحانه'.
2
Understand why it applies to al-Baqillani
Al-Baqillani affirms superadded knowledge for God ('ilm zā'id), so his definition must cover both divine and human knowledge. The objection shows it does not.
The term 'ma'rifa' is not used for divine knowledge by consensus.
3
See the exceptions: Mu'tazila and al-Razi
The Mu'tazila deny superadded knowledge and consider God's knowledge identical to His essence, so 'knowledge' for them is a homonym. Al-Razi negates knowledge as an attribute altogether. Thus the objection does not apply to their definitions.
Al-Razi interprets omniscience as a relation, not a quality inherent to God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني للقاضي أبي بكر الباقلاني ( أنه معرفة المعلوم على ما هو به فيخرج) عن حده (علم الله سبحانه ( مع كونه معترفاً بأن الله علماً ( إذ لا يسمى ( علمه تعالى ( معرفة) إجماعاً لا اصطلاحاً ولا لغة وأيضاً ففيه دور إذ المعلوم مشتق من العلم، فلا يعرف إلا بعد. قوله: (مع كونه معترفاً إلخ) حيث أثبت له تعالى علماً وعالمية وتعلقاً، إما لأحدهما أو لكليهما كما أثبت في الشاهد، فيكون العلم المطلق مشتركاً بين علم الواجب وعلم الممكن اشتراكاً معنوياً، فلا بد من دخوله في تعريف مطلق العلم بخلاف المعتزلة، فإنهم لا يعترفون بالعلم الزائد، ويقولون إنه عين ذاته تعالى فلفظ العلم عندهم مشترك لفظي، فالتعريف المذكور يكون لمطلق العلم الحادث إذ لا مطلق سواه، ولذا لم يورد النقض على تعريفهم بعلمه تعالى فتدبر، ومن هذا ظهر أنه لا يرد النقض بعلمه تعالى على تعريف الإمام لأنه اختار في المطالب العالية نفي العلم عن ذاته تعالى، وإثبات العالمية التي فسرها بالتعلق بين العالم والمعلوم. قوله : ( إذ المعلوم الخ يعني أن المعلوم وإن كان المراد منه ما صدق عليه، لكنه لا بد من ملاحظة مفهومه الذي صار آلة لملاحظة أفراده ومفهومه ما تعلق به العلم والمراد هاهنا ما من شأنه أن يتعلق العلم به فيلزم الدور فتدبر، فإنه زل فيه الأقدام.
The second [definition] is that of al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani: that [knowledge] is knowledge (ma'rifa) of the known as it is. So there escapes from his definition the knowledge of God, despite his acknowledging that God has knowledge, because His knowledge is not called 'ma'rifa' by consensus, neither technically nor linguistically. Also, there is circularity in it because 'the known' (al-ma'lum) is derived from knowledge, so it is not known except after [knowledge]. His saying: (Despite his acknowledging etc.) where he affirmed for Him, exalted, knowledge, omniscience, and relation — either to one of them or to both, as he affirmed in the witnessed [realm]. So absolute knowledge is shared between the knowledge of the Necessary and the knowledge of the possible with a shared meaning (ishtirak ma'nawi). Therefore, it must be included in the definition of absolute knowledge, unlike the Mu'tazila, for they do not acknowledge superadded knowledge and say it is identical to His essence. So the word 'knowledge' for them is a homonym (mushtarak lafzi), and the mentioned definition applies only to absolute created knowledge, since there is no other absolute. That is why they did not raise the objection regarding His knowledge. So reflect. From this it appears that the objection regarding His knowledge does not apply to the definition of the Imam (Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) because he chose in al-Matalib al-Aliya to negate knowledge from His essence and to affirm omniscience, which he interpreted as the relation between the knower and the known. His saying: (For 'the known' etc.) means that 'the known', although what is intended is its instances, it is necessary to observe its concept, which has become a tool for observing its instances. Its concept is that to which knowledge pertains, and what is meant here is that which is apt to have knowledge pertain to it, so circularity follows. So reflect, for feet have slipped here.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage presents objections to al-Baqillani's definition of knowledge as 'knowledge (ma'rifa) of the known as it is'. Two objections are raised. First, it excludes God's knowledge because His knowledge is never called 'ma'rifa' by consensus, either technically or linguistically. This is problematic because al-Baqillani himself affirms that God has knowledge, and absolute knowledge should include both divine and human knowledge. The commentary explains that al-Baqillani's position affirms superadded knowledge for God, so the term 'knowledge' is shared in meaning (ishtirak ma'nawi) between God and creatures. Thus, it should be included. The Mu'tazila, who deny superadded knowledge and consider God's knowledge identical to His essence, would not face this objection because for them 'knowledge' is a homonym. Similarly, Imam al-Razi, who negates knowledge from God's essence and instead affirms 'omniscience' as relation, is not affected. The second objection is circularity: 'the known' (al-ma'lum) is derived from 'knowledge' ('ilm), so defining knowledge by the known requires prior knowledge of 'known', which itself depends on knowledge. The commentary explains that even if the intended meaning of 'known' is its instances, the concept is still needed, and that concept is 'that to which knowledge pertains', so circularity results. The author warns that many have slipped on this point.

Key Terms
معرفة المعلوم
علم الله
الاشتراك المعنوي
الاشتراك اللفظي
الإمام (فخر الدين الرازي)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that excluding divine knowledge from the definition is a minor issue, but it forces a rethinking of whether the definition is truly absolute and comprehensive.
Correction: The exclusion is significant because al-Baqillani himself affirms divine knowledge as real and superadded; a definition that fails to include it is incomplete. The responses of the Mu'tazila and al-Razi show that the objection only applies to certain views on divine attributes.

Checkpoint
Why are the Mu'tazila not affected by the objection that al-Baqillani's definition excludes divine knowledge?
A) They do not believe God has knowledge.
B) They consider God's knowledge identical to His essence, so 'knowledge' is a homonym when applied to God and creatures.
C) They accept the definition as is.
D) They use a different definition of knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The definition of knowledge must be inclusive of both divine and human knowledge unless one holds that 'knowledge' is used homonymously or denies the attribute of knowledge for God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you define concepts like 'knowledge', do you unconsciously limit them to your own experience, excluding how they apply to God? How would you rephrase a definition of a concept to make it inclusive of the divine?
?
This lesson shows that even scholars debated whether a definition must be universal. Are there areas in your life where you hold a definition or rule as absolute, but it fails to account for God's reality? What would it cost you to adjust that definition?
?
If you had to defend the proposition that God's knowledge is truly 'knowledge' in the same sense as human knowledge, what evidence from revelation would you bring? And what does that tell you about the limits of human language in describing God?

Objection: Circularity in al-Baqillani's Definition
Al-Jurjani explains the circularity objection and warns that many have erred on this point.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the circularity objection against al-Baqillani's definition and the subtlety of the concept of 'the known'.

Core Idea
Defining knowledge by 'the known' is circular because 'the known' already implies knowledge.

The second objection is a logical one: al-Baqillani's definition uses 'the known' (al-ma'lum), which is derived from 'knowledge' ('ilm). This creates a vicious circle — to know what 'the known' is, one must already have knowledge. Al-Jurjani explains that even if we consider instances, the concept of 'the known' still depends on knowledge, warning that many have slipped on this point.

Guided Path
1
Identify the circularity
Read the passage and notice that the definition uses 'the known' (al-ma'lum), which is derived from 'ilm (knowledge). This means the definition presupposes what it defines.
The word 'al-ma'lum' is a passive participle from 'alima (to know).
2
Understand the derivation
Since 'al-ma'lum' is derived from 'ilm, to understand what 'the known' means, one must already know what knowledge is. This makes the definition circular.
3
Clarify the concept vs. instances
The commentary explains that even if 'the known' refers to instances (external objects), the concept is still needed, and that concept is 'that to which knowledge pertains', which depends on knowledge.
The author warns: 'feet have slipped here' (i.e., many have erred).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Correction: The term 'the known' (al-ma'lum) is inherently relational; it means 'that which is known' and thus presupposes knowledge. Even the concept of 'the known' as a tool for picking out objects is defined in terms of knowledge, so circularity remains.

What is the basis of the circularity objection against al-Baqillani's definition?
A) The definition uses a term that is not Arabic.

C) The definition is too broad.
D) The definition uses a synonym.

Key Takeaway
The term 'the known' is not independent of knowledge; using it in a definition of knowledge leads to logical circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own knowledge of Allah, do you ever rely on definitions that assume what they are trying to prove? Have you ever caught yourself in a circular argument about faith?
?
The author warns that many have erred on this subtle point. Have you ever been satisfied with a definition that you later realized was circular? What does that reveal about your own intellectual honesty before Allah?

The Objection of the Impossible Thing
Definition of knowledge with objection and response about the impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that the impossible cannot be known is answered by noting that in language the impossible is still called a 'thing', thus preserving the definition of knowledge as 'knowledge of something'.

Core Idea
The impossible is a thing in language, so knowledge of it is possible.

This passage introduces an important nuance: the definition of knowledge as 'knowledge of something' seems challenged by our inability to know the impossible. However, the author shows that even the impossible is linguistically considered a 'thing', so the definition remains valid. Let's examine how this objection and response work.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation to get a sense of the argument's flow.
Notice the structure: definition, objection, response.
2
Identify the objection
Pinpoint the objection: if knowledge is 'of something', then the impossible cannot be known because it is not a thing in reality.
The objection hinges on 'thing' being understood as existent.
3
Understand the response
See how the author responds by distinguishing between linguistic and real senses of 'thing'. In language, the impossible is still called a thing, so knowledge of it fits the definition.
The key phrase is 'المستحيل يُسمى شيئاً لغة'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - في تعريف مطلق العلم
( العلم به ) لامتناع الحكم على ما ليس معلوماً أصلاً (نعم قد يعتذر) لهم (بأن المستحيل يُسمى شيئاً لغة فلا يخرج العلم به عن تعريفهم (وكونه ليس بشيء بمعنى أنه غير ثابت في نفسه لا يمنع ذلك ) أي كونه شيئاً لغة
The second watchtower - on the definition of absolute knowledge. (Knowledge of it) because judgment on what is not known at all is impossible. (Yes, they may excuse) them (that the impossible is called a thing in language, so knowledge of it does not exit from their definition. (And its not being a thing in the sense that it is not established in itself does not prevent that), that is, its being a thing in language.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage presents an initial definition of knowledge as 'knowledge of something' (العلم به), then raises and answers an objection. The objection states that the impossible (المستحيل) is not known, so knowledge of it would be impossible, but the proponents of this definition may respond that in language the impossible is still called a 'thing,' so knowledge of it does not fall outside their definition. The author clarifies that 'not being a thing' means not being established in itself, but that does not prevent it from being called a thing in language. Thus the definition holds.

Al-Baqillani's Definition of Knowledge and Its Critiques
Al-Baqillani's definition of knowledge and two objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Baqillani's definition of knowledge and the two main objections raised against it.

Core Idea
Al-Baqillani defines knowledge as 'knowing the known as it is,' but critics object that it excludes God's knowledge and is circular.

Al-Baqillani's definition of knowledge was influential but faced strong critiques. This lesson presents his definition and the two key objections: that it cannot apply to God's knowledge, and that it is logically circular. Additionally, the phrase 'as it is' is argued to be redundant.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its accompanying translation. Notice the structure: a definition followed by objections.
Pay attention to the technical terms: معرفة (ma'rifa), معلوم (ma'lum), علم ('ilm).
2
Identify the definition and objections
Locate the definition: it is معرفة المعلوم على ما هو به. Then find the two objections: 1) it excludes God's knowledge, 2) it is circular. Also note the third point about 'as it is' being unnecessary.
The circularity objection relies on the derivation of معلوم from علم.
3
Reflect on the critiques
Think about why these objections might be considered serious. The first objection highlights a problem with the term معرفة. The second points to a logical flaw in using derived terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني للقاضي أبي بكر الباقلاني ( أنه معرفة المعلوم على ما هو به فيخرج) عن حده (علم الله سبحانه ( مع كونه معترفاً بأن الله علماً ( إذ لا يسمى ( علمه تعالى ( معرفة) إجماعاً لا اصطلاحاً ولا لغة وأيضاً ففيه دور إذ المعلوم مشتق من العلم، فلا يعرف إلا بعد معرفته) لأن المشتق مشتمل على معنى المشتق منه مع زيادة (و) أيضاً (فعلى ما هو به قید (زائد) لا حاجة إليه ( إذ المعرفة لا تكون إلا كذلك لأن إدراك الشيء لا على ما هو به جهالة لا معرفة
The second [definition] by Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani: that it is knowing the known as it is. So it excludes from his definition the knowledge of God, the Exalted, despite his acknowledgment that God has knowledge, because His knowledge, the Exalted, is not called 'ma'rifa' (recognition) unanimously, neither technically nor linguistically. And also there is circularity in it because 'ma'lum' is derived from 'ilm' (knowledge), so it is not known except after knowing it. Because the derived term contains the meaning of the derivative with an addition. And also, the qualification 'as it is' is an extra unnecessary addition, because ma'rifa is not except thus, since perception of a thing not as it is is ignorance, not knowledge.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage presents al-Baqillani's definition of knowledge as 'knowing the known as it is' (معرفة المعلوم على ما هو به). Two objections are then raised. First, it excludes God's knowledge because God's knowledge is not called 'ma'rifa' (a term that implies recognition after prior acquaintance) by consensus in technical or linguistic usage. Second, the definition is circular because 'ma'lum' (known) is derived from 'ilm' (knowledge), so the definition uses a term that presupposes knowledge. Additionally, the phrase 'as it is' is deemed an unnecessary qualification, since ma'rifa inherently means perception in accordance with reality; otherwise it would be ignorance, not knowledge.

Key Terms
معرفة
معلوم
علم
دور
القاضي أبو بكر الباقلاني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the first objection denies God's knowledge altogether.
Correction: The objection is not that God lacks knowledge, but that the term 'ma'rifa' is inappropriate for God's knowledge because it implies prior acquaintance.

Checkpoint
Why does the objection claim that al-Baqillani's definition is circular?
A) Because 'ma'lum' is derived from 'ilm'
B) Because it uses the word 'ma'rifa'
C) Because it includes the phrase 'as it is'
D) Because it assumes knowledge exists

Key Takeaway
Al-Baqillani's definition of knowledge faces two serious objections: it cannot apply to God's knowledge, and it is circular.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You use the word 'knowledge' for your own understanding and for God's. But this lesson shows that the term 'ma'rifa' cannot be applied to God. How often do you conflate your limited recognition with His absolute knowledge? When you think you 'know' God, are you aware that your knowledge is fundamentally different in kind from His? How should this realization change the way you approach learning about Allah?
?
The circularity objection shows that any definition of knowledge presupposes knowledge. If you cannot define knowledge without already knowing it, then your attempts to intellectually grasp the foundations of faith are inherently limited. Does this disturb your confidence in rational theology? In what areas of your deen do you rely on definitions that might be circular? What does this teach you about humility before divine truth?
?
Al-Baqillani's definition is critiqued for adding an unnecessary qualification 'as it is' because true knowledge is always accurate. But you often settle for incomplete or distorted perceptions. When have you been content with knowing something 'as it is not' about your own soul or about Allah? What would it mean for you to insist on knowing truth exactly as it is, even when it is uncomfortable?

Al-Ash'ari's Two Definitions and Their Flaws
Al-Ash'ari gives two definitions of knowledge, both criticized for circularity and metaphor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Ash'ari's definitions of knowledge are criticized for circularity and metaphor, and why this matters for Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Defining knowledge is tricky; even great scholars like al-Ash'ari faltered.

Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, the founder of the Ash'ari school, proposed two formulations for defining knowledge. However, both were found to be logically flawed. This lesson examines his attempts and the criticisms that led to their rejection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two formulations al-Ash'ari gives.
Focus on the terms 'alim and ma'lum.
2
Identify the two definitions
The first definition relates to the subject (the knower) and the second to the object (the known). Write them down.
3
Understand the criticisms
The first is criticized for circularity because it uses 'alim (knower) to define 'ilm (knowledge). The second is criticized both for circularity (using ma'lum) and for using a metaphor (idrak).
Metaphors are not allowed in formal definitions because they are not precise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث للشيخ ( أبي الحسن الأشعري ( فقال : تارة ) بالقياس إلى المحل ) هو الذي يوجب كون من قام به عالماً أو ) هو الذي يوجب (لمن قام به اسم العالم ومؤدى العبارتين واحد ) وفيه دور (ظاهر لأخذ العالم في تعريف العلم (و) قال : ( أخرى ) بالقياس إلى متعلق العلم إدراك المعلوم على ما هو به وفيه الدور) لأخذ المعلوم في الحد (و) فيه ) أن الإدراك مجاز عن العلم لأن معناه الحقيقي هو اللحوق والوصول والمجاز لا يستعمل في الحدود فإن أجيب باشتهاره معنى العلم قلنا : لم يندفع بذلك تعريف الشيء بنفسه لأن المعنى المجازي هو العلم
The third [definition] by Sheikh (Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari): He said: (sometimes, by relation to the subject) it is that which necessitates the one in whom it exists to be knowledgeable, or it is that which necessitates (for the one in whom it exists) the name of 'alim (knower). The purport of both expressions is one. And in it is (manifest circularity) because 'alim is taken in the definition of 'ilm. (And) He said: (Another [formulation], by relation to the object of knowledge) is perception (idrak) of the known as it is. And in it is (circularity) because 'ma'lum' is taken in the limit. (And) in it is that perception is a metaphor for knowledge, because its literal meaning is overtaking and reaching, and metaphor is not used in definitions. If it is responded with its common usage meaning knowledge, we say: that does not remove the definition of a thing by itself, because the metaphorical meaning is knowledge.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage presents al-Ash'ari's two formulations for defining knowledge. The first, by relation to the subject, defines knowledge as 'that which necessitates the one in whom it exists to be an 'alim (knower)' or 'that which necessitates the name of 'alim'. The author points out that this is circular because 'alim' (knower) is used in defining 'ilm' (knowledge). The second formulation, by relation to the object, defines knowledge as 'perception (idrak) of the known as it is'. This is also circular because 'ma'lum' (known) is used, and moreover 'idrak' is a metaphor for knowledge, as its literal meaning is 'overtaking'. Metaphor is not allowed in definitions. If one argues that 'idrak' is commonly used to mean knowledge, the reply is that the metaphorical meaning is still knowledge itself, so it is still defining a thing by itself.

Key Terms

عالم
معلوم
إدراك
مجاز
دور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The criticism of circularity means the definitions are false.
Correction: The objection is about logical form for formal definitions; it doesn't address the truth of what knowledge is, only that the definition is unacceptably circular.

Checkpoint
Why is al-Ash'ari's second definition criticized for using a metaphor?
A) Because 'idrak' literally means overtaking, not knowing
B) Because 'ma'lum' is not defined
C) Because perception is impossible
D) Because it is too vague

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari's definitions of knowledge fail because they are circular or rely on metaphor, showing that defining fundamental concepts requires great precision.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If the greatest theologians can fall into circular reasoning, how much more careful must you be in defining the foundations of your own belief? What step will you take today to examine and strengthen the logical structure of your understanding of Allah's attributes?

Al-Ash'ari's Two Definitions of Knowledge and the Problem of Circularity
Critical exposition of al-Ash'ari's two definitions of knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ash'ari's two definitions of knowledge and why they are criticized for circularity and using metaphor.

Core Idea
Al-Ash'ari's definitions of knowledge are flawed because they presuppose what they define.

In this passage, the author presents the third definition of knowledge, that of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari. Al-Ash'ari gives two formulations—one focusing on the locus of knowledge and the other on its object. Both are shown to suffer from circular reasoning, making them unacceptable in formal definition. Additionally, the second definition uses the term 'idrāk', which is argued to be metaphorical and therefore unfit for definition.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
This passage is part of a critical survey of definitions of knowledge. Al-Ash'ari's is the third definition discussed.
The author is presenting and then critiquing various definitions.
2
Identify the first definition
Al-Ash'ari defines knowledge with respect to its locus (mahall) as 'that which necessitates that the one in whom it subsists be called knower'.
3
Recognize the circularity
The definition uses the term 'knower', which itself derives from knowledge, creating a loop. The author calls this 'an apparent circularity'.
Circularity means the definiendum is used in the definiens.
4
Examine the second definition
Al-Ash'ari also defines knowledge as 'apprehension of the known as it is'. Note the use of 'the known'.
5
Understand the dual critique
This definition is circular because it uses 'the known', and also problematic because 'idrāk' (apprehension) is a metaphor for knowledge, and metaphors are not allowed in definitions.
The literal meaning of idrāk is 'overtaking' or 'reaching', not knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث للشيخ أبي الحسن الأشعري فقال: تارة بالقياس إلى المحل هو الذي يوجب كون من قام به عالماً أو هو الذي يوجب لمن قام به اسم العالم ومؤدى العبارتين واحد وفيه دور ظاهر لأخذ العالم في تعريف العلم وقال: أخرى بالقياس إلى متعلق العلم إدراك المعلوم على ما هو به وفيه الدور لأخذ المعلوم في الحد وفيه أن الإدراك مجاز عن العلم لأن معناه الحقيقي هو اللحوق والوصول والمجاز لا يستعمل في الحدود
The third [definition] is by Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari. He said: sometimes with respect to the locus [of knowledge], it is that which necessitates that the one in whom it subsists be knowing, or that which necessitates for the one in whom it subsists the name 'knower'; the content of the two expressions is one. In it there is an apparent circularity because it takes 'knower' in the definition of knowledge. And he said: another time with respect to the object of knowledge, it is the apprehension of the known as it is. In it there is circularity because it takes 'the known' in the definition. And in it [there is the objection] that 'idrāk' (apprehension) is a metaphor for knowledge, because its real meaning is overtaking and reaching, and metaphor is not used in definitions.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage presents al-Ash'ari's two definitions of knowledge and the criticisms against them. The passage is a critical exposition of definitions. First, al-Ash'ari defines knowledge with respect to its locus (al-mahall): it is that which necessitates that the person in whom it resides is called 'knower'. The commentator notes that this involves circularity (dawr) because the word 'knower' is used in defining knowledge. Second, al-Ash'ari defines knowledge with respect to its object: apprehension (idrāk) of the known as it is. This also involves circularity because it uses 'the known'. Additionally, the term 'idrāk' is objected to as a metaphor (majāz) for knowledge, since its literal meaning is 'overtaking' or 'reaching'. Metaphors are not considered valid in formal definitions. The passage thus identifies two logical flaws in al-Ash'ari's definitions.

Key Terms
العلم
الدور
المجال
المتعلق
الإدراك
المجاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Using the derived term 'knower' in the definition of knowledge is acceptable.
Correction: It leads to circularity because 'knower' is derived from 'knowledge', so the definition presupposes what it defines.

Checkpoint
What is the first criticism leveled against al-Ash'ari's definition of knowledge with respect to the locus?
A) It is too vague.
B) It is circular because it uses 'knower' in the definition.
C) It is not a real definition.
D) It relies on an unknown term.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari's definitions of knowledge are rejected due to circularity and the use of metaphorical language.

?

?

?

Defense of the Metaphorical Use of Idrak
Responds to the objection that 'idrak' is metaphor, defending its use.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the metaphorical use of 'idrak' in defining knowledge is defended against the objection of self-definition.

Core Idea
Metaphor can save a definition from circularity if the metaphorical meaning is broader.

The objection claims that using 'idrak' as a metaphor for knowledge leads to defining knowledge by itself. The verifying professor responds by pointing out that the well-known metaphorical meaning of 'idrak'—the occurrence of the form in the mind—is actually more general, thus avoiding self-definition. However, this answer relies on the concept of mental existence, which some reject. Ultimately, the passage resolves that 'idrak' as metaphor for attainment is clear enough.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argumentative flow come through.
Notice the back-and-forth structure: objection, answer, counter-objection, final resolution.
2
Identify the objection and first response
Find the initial objection: if 'idrak' is a metaphor for knowledge, then the definition becomes 'knowledge of the known', which is circular. Then see the professor's answer: the metaphorical meaning is 'occurrence of the form in the mind', which is broader.
The professor is called 'al-ustādh al-muḥaqqiq'.
3
Analyze the counter-objection and resolution
Notice that the professor's answer is criticized for relying on 'mental existence' (al-wujūd al-dhihnī), which some do not accept. Then read the final resolution: 'idrak' originally means 'attainment' (wuṣūl), and as a metaphor for that general meaning, the definition is valid. Quibbling over wording is dismissed.
The passage concludes that verbal disputes after clarity of intent are not heeded.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن أجيب باشتهاره معنى العلم قلنا: لم يندفع بذلك تعريف الشيء بنفسه لأن المعنى المجازي هو العلم نفسه فكأنه قيل: هو علم المعلوم وفيه الزيادة المذكورة يعني أن قوله على ما هو به زائد فإن المعلوم لا يكون إلا كذلك. وأجاب الأستاذ المحقق بأن المعنى المجازي المشهور للإدراك هو العلم بمعنى حصول الصورة في العقل وهو أعم من الذي نحن بصدد تعريفه فاندفع تعريف الشيء بنفسه ورد بأنه مبني على الوجود الذهني الذي هم لا يقولون به. ويمكن أن يقال: لا شبهة في تحقق المعنى الأول المتناول للعلم المعرف وغيره وهو الوصول إلى معنى أو إضافة مخصوصة بين العالم والمعلوم ومقصود المجيب أن الإدراك مجاز عن ذلك المعنى الأعم، والمناقشة في العبارة بعد وضوح المقصود لا يلتفت إليه فلا محذور.
If it is answered with its common usage in the meaning of knowledge, we say: that does not repel defining a thing by itself, because the metaphorical meaning is knowledge itself, so it is as if it were said: it is knowledge of the known. And in it is the mentioned addition, meaning that his saying 'as it is' is redundant, for the known is only so. And the verifying professor answered that the well-known metaphorical meaning of 'idrāk' is knowledge in the sense of the occurrence of the form in the mind, and it is more general than that which we are in the process of defining, so defining a thing by itself is repelled. But it was replied that [this answer] is based on mental existence (al-wujūd al-dhihnī), which they do not affirm. And it can be said: there is no doubt in the realization of the first meaning which includes the defined knowledge and other things, which is attainment (al-wuṣūl) to a meaning or a specific relation between the knower and the known. The intention of the answerer is that 'idrāk' is a metaphor for that more general meaning. Disputation over the expression after clarity of intent is not heeded, so there is no harm.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer sequence defending the use of 'idrāk' (apprehension) in al-Ash'ari's definition. The objector claims that if 'idrāk' is taken as a metaphor for knowledge, it leads to defining knowledge by itself. The defender (al-ustādh al-muḥaqqiq) responds that the well-known metaphorical meaning of 'idrāk' is 'the occurrence of the form in the mind' (ḥuṣūl al-ṣūra fī al-ʿaql), which is more general than the specific knowledge being defined, thus avoiding self-definition. The objection is then raised that this answer presupposes 'mental existence' (al-wujūd al-dhihnī), which the opponents (possibly those who reject mental existence) do not accept. The passage concludes with a final resolution: the first meaning of 'idrāk'—'attainment' (wuṣūl) to a meaning or a specific relation—is undeniably real. The defender's point is that 'idrāk' is a metaphor for that general meaning. Since the intended meaning is clear, quibbling over wording is dismissed. Thus, the metaphorical use is justified.

Key Terms
الإدراك
المجاز
الوجود الذهني
حصول الصورة
الوصول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Metaphor in definitions is always invalid and leads to circularity.
Correction: The passage shows that if the metaphorical meaning is broader than the defined term, it can avoid circularity. Moreover, if the intended meaning is clear, quibbling over wording is dismissed.

Checkpoint
How does the verifying professor defend the use of 'idrak' as a metaphor for knowledge?
A) By saying it has no metaphorical meaning.
B) By saying the metaphorical meaning is 'attainment' (wuṣūl).
C) By saying the metaphorical meaning is 'occurrence of the form in the mind', which is broader.
D) By saying it is not a metaphor at all.

Key Takeaway
The metaphorical use of 'idrak' in defining knowledge is defended by showing that its metaphorical meaning is broader, thus avoiding self-definition, and any remaining verbal dispute is dismissed due to clarity of intent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a theological objection that seems purely technical, do you dismiss it as wordplay, or do you dig deeper to understand the underlying intent like the professor here? How can this lesson change your approach to such debates in your own faith?
?
Have you ever used a metaphor to explain a complex religious concept, only to be accused of circular reasoning? How does the final resolution in this passage—that clarity of intent overrides verbal quibbling—give you confidence to stand by clear explanations even when opponents nitpick?
?
The passage shows that even a strong response (based on mental existence) can be challenged, but a deeper original meaning resolves the issue. In your own understanding of Islamic theology, where might you be relying on an intermediate explanation that could be undermined, and how can you seek the more fundamental meaning that withstands objections?

Ibn Furak's Definition of Knowledge and Its Critique
Presents Ibn Furak's definition and critiques its implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Furak's definition of knowledge as that which makes perfection of action possible, the critique that it either fails or excludes human knowledge, and the defense within the Ash'ari framework.

Core Idea
Knowledge enables perfect action, but in Ash'arism, our actions are not our own.

Ibn Furak defines knowledge by its relation to action: it is that which makes the perfection (itqān) of an act possible. This seems intuitive, but it raises deep questions about causality and agency. Does knowledge alone produce perfect actions? Or does it only contribute? If only contribute, then power (qudra) must also be part of the definition, and in the Ash'ari view, our knowledge would be irrelevant since God creates our actions. The passage presents this dilemma and a defense that seeks to preserve the definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to get the gist.
Notice the dense argument style typical of kalam works.
2
Identify the two interpretations
Ibn Furak's definition can be taken in two ways: either knowledge independently suffices for perfection, or it merely contributes. Find these two possibilities in the text.
The critic argues the first is false, the second leads to problems.
3
Understand the critique
If knowledge only contributes, then power must be part of the definition, and our knowledge is excluded because in Ash'ari theology, we do not originate our actions. Trace this reasoning in the passage.
This critique challenges the Ash'ari stance on human agency.
4
Grasp the defense
The defender responds that the definition does not require actual perfection—only potential. Moreover, the definition covers both acquired (kasb) and originated (ijad) actions. See how this rebuttal fits within Ash'ari doctrine.
The defense preserves the definition without contradicting core Ash'ari beliefs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع لابن فورك ما يصح ممن قام به إتقان الفعل أي إحكامه وتخليته عن وجوه الخلل فإن أراد ما يستقل بالصحة فهو باطل قطعاً، وإن أراد ما له دخل فيها فتدخل القدرة في الحد ويخرج عنه علمنا إذ لا مدخل له في صحة الإتقان على رأينا فإن أفعالنا ليست بإيجادنا وقد أجيب بأن صحة الإتقان به لا يستلزم الإتقان بالفعل فعلمنا الحاصل لنا يصح به إتقان أفعالنا لو كان أفعالنا بإيجادنا، على أن المراد إتقان الفعل كسباً كان أو إيجاداً فلا يخرج علمنا.
The fourth [definition] is by Ibn Furak: that which makes valid for the one in whom it subsists the perfection of the act, i.e., its soundness and its being free from aspects of defect. If he intends that which is independently valid, then it is absolutely false. And if he intends that which has a role in it, then power enters into the definition, and our knowledge is excluded from it, since it has no role in the validity of perfection according to our view, for our actions are not by our origination. And it has been answered that the validity of perfection through it does not entail actual perfection; thus our knowledge that we have can validly serve to perfect our actions if our actions were by our origination. Moreover, the intended is the perfection of the act whether it is acquired (kasb) or originated, so our knowledge is not excluded.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Furak's definition of knowledge: that which makes the perfection (itqān) of an action possible. The commentator first clarifies 'perfection' as 'soundness and freedom from defects'. Then he raises a dilemma: if Ibn Furak means that knowledge independently suffices for perfection, that is false. If he means that knowledge contributes to perfection, then power (qudra) must also be part of the definition, and our knowledge is excluded because in the Ash'ari view, our actions are not originated by us (they are created by God). Thus, our knowledge cannot cause perfection. The defender counters that the definition does not require actual perfection; knowledge can potentially perfect actions if they were originated by us. Additionally, the definition implicitly includes both acquired (kasbī) and originated (ījādī) actions, so our knowledge is not excluded. The passage thus subjects Ibn Furak's definition to scrutiny and offers a defense within the Ash'ari framework.

القدرة
الكسب
الإيجاد

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Ibn Furak's definition is straightforward and does not involve assumptions about causality or human agency.
Correction: The definition carries implications for the role of power (qudra) and the nature of human actions. In the Ash'ari view, because actions are created by God, the definition must be carefully interpreted to avoid excluding human knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the critic, why does Ibn Furak's definition exclude our knowledge if power is included?
A) Because we do not have power over our actions.
B) Because our actions are not originated by us in the Ash'ari view.
C) Because knowledge is not relevant to perfection.
D) Because the definition only applies to God.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Furak's definition of knowledge as enabling action perfection is defended by clarifying that it refers to potential perfection, not actual, and includes both acquired and originated actions, thus aligning with Ash'ari theology.

?
Ibn Furak's definition implies that knowledge should lead to perfection of action. Reflect on an area of your life where your knowledge has not translated into better actions. What is the barrier? Is it a lack of true knowledge, or is it relying on your own power rather than God's?
?
The defense distinguishes between potential and actual perfection. In your daily worship, do you settle for potential perfection (knowing what is right) without actual perfection (doing it)? What would it take to move from potential to actual?

Critique of Defining Knowledge as Knowledge of the Known
Critique of defining knowledge as 'knowledge of the known' for circularity and redundancy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why defining knowledge as 'knowledge of the known' is considered circular and redundant, and how a metaphorical interpretation fails to rescue it.

Core Idea
Defining knowledge as 'knowledge of the known' is circular and adds an unnecessary qualifier.

In classical Islamic epistemology, defining knowledge has been a contentious issue. One proposed definition states that knowledge is 'knowledge of the known' (ʿilm al-maʿlūm). At first glance, this seems informative, but careful analysis reveals it is circular and includes an extra qualifier. This lesson unpacks the critique, the counterargument from metaphorical usage, and the rebuttal that limits that usage to a specific philosophical tradition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its English translation. Notice the structure: critique, counterargument, and rebuttal.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the main critique
The definition is criticized for two things: circularity (defining knowledge by itself) and redundancy (adding 'as it is' is extra). Understand why these are problematic.
3
Examine the counterargument
Some argue that the term 'perception' (al-idrāk) can be taken metaphorically to avoid circularity. Note how this shifts the meaning.
This counterargument is rejected because it relies on a philosophical usage, not the intended tradition.
4
Understand the final rebuttal
The passage clarifies that if 'the known' is understood as 'apt to be known', the qualifier is not necessary. Only if it means 'known by this perception' does the critique hold.
This shows the definition's dependence on interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما المرصد الثاني - في تعريف مطلق العلم
العلم نفسه فكأنه قيل : هو علم المعلوم ) وفيه الزيادة المذكورة ) يعني أن قوله على هو به زائد فإن المعلوم لا يكون إلا كذلك
التعريف من غير قرينة وما قيل : إِن المعنى المجازي للإدراك المشهور هو العلم بمعنى الصورة الحاصلة مطلقاً، فلا يلزم تعريف الشيء بنفسه، ولا زيادة قيد على ما هو به فمدفوع بأن ذلك المعنى مشهور عند الحكماء، لا عند أصحاب هذا التعريف
قوله : ( فإن المعلوم إلخ) فيه بحث لأن المراد بالمعلوم ما هو من شأنه أن يعلم، ولا يلزم أن يكون الإدراك المتعلق بما هو من شأنه العلم أن يكون على ما هو به، نعم لو أريد بالمعلوم ما هو معلوم بهذا الإدراك لاتجه ذلك .
What is the second observatory? On the definition of absolute knowledge. Knowledge itself, as if it were said: it is knowledge of the known. In it is the mentioned redundancy, meaning that its statement 'as it is' is extra, for the known is only like that. The definition is without a clue [؟]. And what was said: that the metaphorical meaning of perception (al-idrāk) well-known is knowledge in the sense of the form obtained absolutely, so neither defining a thing by itself nor adding a restriction beyond what it is follows. But it is refuted by [the fact] that that meaning is well-known among the philosophers (al-ḥukamā'), not among the proponents of this definition. His statement: 'for the known, etc.' – in it is a discussion, because what is meant by the known is that which is apt to be known, and it is not necessary that the perception related to that which is apt for knowledge be as it is. Indeed, if the known were meant as what is known by this perception, that would be consistent.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage critiques a definition of knowledge (al-ʿilm) as 'knowledge of the known' (ʿilm al-maʿlūm). The author first notes that the definition contains a redundancy: the qualifier 'as it is' (ʿalā mā huwa bihī) is extra because the known is by nature what it is. Then a counterargument is presented: the metaphorical sense of perception (al-idrāk) is universally understood as knowledge in the sense of the mental form obtained absolutely, so there is no circularity or added restriction. However, this counterargument is rejected on the grounds that such a metaphorical usage is familiar among the philosophers (al-ḥukamā'), not among the scholars who formulated this definition. Finally, the commentary on the phrase 'for the known, etc.' explains that if 'the known' is understood as that which is apt to be known, then perception does not have to represent it 'as it is'; only if 'the known' is meant as 'what is known through this perception' would the objection hold.

Key Terms
العلم
المعلوم
الإدراك
الزيادة
الدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the definition 'knowledge of the known' is always circular with no possible escape.
Correction: The passage shows that a metaphorical interpretation could avoid circularity, but it is rejected because it is not accepted by the tradition that proposed the definition.

Checkpoint
Why is the qualifier 'as it is' (ʿalā mā huwa bihī) considered redundant in the definition 'knowledge of the known'?
A) Because it is an unnecessary addition that does not change the meaning
B) Because 'the known' is inherently what it is, so the qualifier adds nothing
C) Because it makes the definition longer
D) Because it refers to a different concept

Key Takeaway
The definition of knowledge as 'knowledge of the known' fails due to circularity and redundancy, and attempts to salvage it via metaphorical interpretation are not accepted within the tradition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author rejects an argument because it comes from philosophers rather than the tradition. Is your own knowledge of Islam built on the authentic sources, or have you unknowingly adopted concepts that are foreign to the tradition? What concrete step can you take today to check the source of your beliefs?
?
The nuance about 'the known' shows that precision in terminology matters. When you say 'Allah knows everything,' do you reflect on what 'knowledge' means in that context? How can you deepen your appreciation of Allah's attribute of knowledge by avoiding careless definitions?

Ibn Furak's Definition of Knowledge
Ibn Furak's definition with two interpretations and initial exclusion of our knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Furak's definition of knowledge as 'what qualifies one to perfect an act' and its two possible interpretations.

Core Idea
Knowledge is what allows perfection of an act, but its exact role is disputed.

Ibn Furak, a prominent Ash'ari theologian, offered a definition of knowledge based on its practical effect: enabling the perfection of an action. This definition, however, opens up several interpretive possibilities that affect whether it holds true. We will examine the definition itself and the initial objections regarding its scope.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the Arabic passage containing Ibn Furak's definition: 'ما يصح ممن قام به إتقان الفعل'. Notice that 'perfection' means making the act sound and free from defects.
The author immediately distinguishes two interpretations.
2
Identify the two interpretations
The commentary explains two senses: (1) what independently makes perfection correct (invalid), and (2) what plays a role in it (leads to power entering the definition).
This distinction is crucial for understanding the critique.
3
Consider the consequence for our knowledge
Under the second interpretation, our knowledge would be excluded because we do not create our acts (Ash'ari view). Reflect on why that is a problem.
This connects to the broader issue of human agency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : لابن فورك ما يصح ممن قام به إتقان الفعل) أي إحكامه وتخليته عن وجوه الخلل فإن أراد ما يستقل بالصحة فهو باطل قطعاً، وإن أراد ما له دخل فيها ( فتدخل القدرة في الحد (ويخرج) عنه ) ) علمنا إذ لا مدخل له في ( صحة الإتقان على رأينا ) فإن أفعالنا ليست بإيجادنا
قوله : (ما يصح ممن قال إلخ والتقليد والظن الغالب لا يدخلان في هذا التعريف، لأن إتقان الفعل وتخليته عن وجوه الخلل، إنما يتصور إذا كان عالماً بالمفاسد والمصالح، علماً يقينياً تفصيلياً ولذا استدلوا بإتقان العالم على علمه تعالى .
قوله : (إذ لا مدخل إلخ) يعني أن الإتقان مضاء الإيجاد على وجه الأحكام، وذلك إنما يتصور عن الموجد، فيكون لعلمه بوجه المصالح مدخل في الإتقان، وأما غير الموجد فلا تعلق له بالإيجاد فلا يتصور منه الإتقان إذ لا يمكن إتقان فعل الغير فلا مدخل لعلمه في صحة الإتقان وأما القول بأنه على تقدير فرض إيجادنا لأفعالنا يكون علمنا مما يصح به إتقان الفعل فممنوع، ولا دليل على ذلك فإنه فرض محال يجوز أن يستلزم المحال، وكذا ما قيل : إن المراد به إتقان الفعل كسبياً كان أو إيجادياً، إذ الكسب عبارة عن صرف القدرة والإرادة نحو الفعل ولا تعلق له بالإيجاد .
قوله : ( الرابع إلخ) لا يخفى أن لا دخل لكون الإدراك عن دليل، بل لكونه قطعياً أيضاً في الإتقان، بل يكفيه التقليد والظن الغالب الذي لا يخطر خلافه بالبال فينتقض التعريف بهما .
قوله : ( فإن أفعالنا ليست بإيجادنا ) أجيب بأن صحة الإتقان به لا يستلزم الإتقان بالفعل فعلمنا الحاصل لنا يصح به إتقان أفعالنا لو كان أفعالنا بإيجادنا، على أن المراد إتقان الفعل كسباً كان أو إيجاداً فلا يخرج علمنا .
أورد عليه بعد تسليم أن فعل العبد بإيجاده ( علم أحدنا بنفسه وبالباري) تعالى وبالمستحيل فإن ما تعلق به هذا العلم ليس فعلا ولا مما يصح إتفانه به ( وإنما يرد ) عليه هذا ( إن لو أراد ما يصح به إتقان متعلقه وأما لو أراد ما يصح به ) الإتقان (في الجملة ) وإن لم يكن مصححا بحسب شخصه ( فلا) ورود لهذا عليه
The fourth: by Ibn Furak: what qualifies one by whom it is established to perfect an act, i.e., to make it firm and free from aspects of defect. If he means what is independently valid, it is absolutely false. If he means what has a role in it, then power enters the definition, and our knowledge exits it, since it has no role in the correctness of perfection according to our view, for our acts are not by our bringing into existence. His statement: 'what qualifies one said etc.' – imitation (al-taqīd) and predominant opinion (al-ẓann al-ghālib) do not enter this definition, because the perfection of an act and its freeing from aspects of defect is only conceivable if one knows the harms and benefits with detailed certainty. That is why they used the perfection of the world to prove His knowledge. His statement: 'since there is no role etc.' means that perfection is the execution of bringing into existence in an accurate manner, and that is only conceivable from the originator. So his knowledge of the beneficial aspect has a role in perfection. As for the non-originator, he has no connection to bringing into existence, so perfection from him is inconceivable, since one cannot perfect the act of another. So his knowledge has no role in the correctness of perfection. As for the claim that, assuming our acts are by our bringing into existence, our knowledge would be what qualifies for perfection of the act, it is rejected. There is no proof for that, because it is an impossible supposition that may entail impossibility. Likewise what was said: 'what is meant is perfection of the act whether it is acquired (kasbī) or originated (ījādī),' since acquisition (al-kasb) is the directing of power and will towards the act, and it has no connection to bringing into existence. His statement: 'the fourth etc.' – it is not hidden that there is no role for perception being from proof, nor for it being definitive, in perfection. Rather, imitation and predominant opinion that does not occur to the mind contrary suffice, so the definition is refuted by them. His statement: 'for our acts are not by our bringing into existence' – it is answered that the correctness of perfection through it does not necessitate actual perfection. Our acquired knowledge qualifies for perfection of our acts if our acts were by our bringing into existence, given that what is meant is perfection of the act whether acquired or originated, so our knowledge is not excluded. An objection was raised after conceding that the slave's act is by his bringing into existence: our knowledge of ourselves, of the Creator, and of the impossible – what this knowledge relates to is not an act nor does it qualify for perfection through it. And this only applies if he meant what qualifies for perfection of its own object. But if he meant what qualifies for perfection in general, even if not a corrector for its own object, then this does not apply.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth definition of knowledge by Ibn Furak: 'what qualifies one to perfect an act.' The author first distinguishes two interpretations: if it means independent validity, it is false; if it means having a role in validity, then power (al-qudra) enters the definition and our knowledge is excluded, since on the Ash'ari view our acts are not our own creation. Then commentary explains that the definition excludes fallible knowledge like imitation (taqlīd) and predominant opinion, because perfection requires certain detailed knowledge. It also argues that a non-originator cannot perfect a act; so our knowledge, on the view that we do not create our acts, cannot contribute to perfection. A response suggests that even if we did create our acts, our knowledge would qualify, but the supposition is impossible. The discussion continues with an objection: our knowledge of necessary things (self, God, impossible) is not related to acts, so it would be excluded. The answer is that the definition intends 'qualifies for perfection in general,' not necessarily for its own object. This definition and its critique illustrate the complexity of defining knowledge and the interplay of causal and epistemological considerations.

Key Terms
إتقان الفعل
القدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does 'perfection of an act' (إتقان الفعل) mean?
A) Doing an act quickly
B) Making the act firm and free from defects
C) Performing an act with complete certainty
D) Creating an act from nothing

Key Takeaway
Ibn Furak's definition fails because either it is false or it inadvertently includes power and excludes our knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever feel that your knowledge alone is enough to perfect your actions? How does the Ash'ari view that your act is not truly your own challenge your sense of self-reliance?
?
When you perform a good deed, do you attribute its perfection to your own knowledge and skill, or do you recognize that even that knowledge is a gift from Allah? What would change if you truly internalized that your knowledge has no independent role in action?
?
Ibn Furak's definition fails because it tries to isolate knowledge. In your life, have you ever relied on knowledge without seeking Allah's help, only to find your efforts flawed? How can you remind yourself daily that all perfection comes from Him?

Refuting Ibn Furak's Definition: Imitation and Predominant Opinion
Objection that imitation and predominant opinion refute the definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Furak's definition of knowledge is refuted by the fact that imitation and predominant opinion can also lead to perfect action.

Core Idea
Perfect action does not require certain knowledge; imitation and probable opinion suffice.

Ibn Furak's definition requires that the knowledge leading to perfect action be certain and detailed. But everyday examples show that people often perform actions correctly based on imitation or predominant opinion. This undermines the definition, as it would exclude such valid knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage where the objector points out that imitation (taqlīd) and predominant opinion (ẓann ghalib) do not enter the definition.
The definition requires detailed certain knowledge of benefits and harms.
2
Understand why they are excluded
The definition insists on certain detailed knowledge to perfect an act. Imitation and predominant opinion lack that certainty and detail.
The objector uses this to show the definition is too narrow.
3
Recognize the refutation
The objector argues that despite lacking certainty, imitation and predominant opinion can indeed produce perfect actions. Therefore the definition is invalid.
The phrase 'ينتقض التعريف بهما' means 'the definition is refuted by them'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : لابن فورك ما يصح ممن قام به إتقان الفعل) أي إحكامه وتخليته عن وجوه الخلل فإن أراد ما يستقل بالصحة فهو باطل قطعاً، وإن أراد ما له دخل فيها ( فتدخل القدرة في الحد (ويخرج) عنه ) ) علمنا إذ لا مدخل له في ( صحة الإتقان على رأينا ) فإن أفعالنا ليست بإيجادنا
قوله : (ما يصح ممن قال إلخ والتقليد والظن الغالب لا يدخلان في هذا التعريف، لأن إتقان الفعل وتخليته عن وجوه الخلل، إنما يتصور إذا كان عالماً بالمفاسد والمصالح، علماً يقينياً تفصيلياً ولذا استدلوا بإتقان العالم على علمه تعالى .
قوله : (إذ لا مدخل إلخ) يعني أن الإتقان مضاء الإيجاد على وجه الأحكام، وذلك إنما يتصور عن الموجد، فيكون لعلمه بوجه المصالح مدخل في الإتقان، وأما غير الموجد فلا تعلق له بالإيجاد فلا يتصور منه الإتقان إذ لا يمكن إتقان فعل الغير فلا مدخل لعلمه في صحة الإتقان وأما القول بأنه على تقدير فرض إيجادنا لأفعالنا يكون علمنا مما يصح به إتقان الفعل فممنوع، ولا دليل على ذلك فإنه فرض محال يجوز أن يستلزم المحال، وكذا ما قيل : إن المراد به إتقان الفعل كسبياً كان أو إيجادياً، إذ الكسب عبارة عن صرف القدرة والإرادة نحو الفعل ولا تعلق له بالإيجاد .
قوله : ( الرابع إلخ) لا يخفى أن لا دخل لكون الإدراك عن دليل، بل لكونه قطعياً أيضاً في الإتقان، بل يكفيه التقليد والظن الغالب الذي لا يخطر خلافه بالبال فينتقض التعريف بهما .
قوله : ( فإن أفعالنا ليست بإيجادنا ) أجيب بأن صحة الإتقان به لا يستلزم الإتقان بالفعل فعلمنا الحاصل لنا يصح به إتقان أفعالنا لو كان أفعالنا بإيجادنا، على أن المراد إتقان الفعل كسباً كان أو إيجاداً فلا يخرج علمنا .
أورد عليه بعد تسليم أن فعل العبد بإيجاده ( علم أحدنا بنفسه وبالباري) تعالى وبالمستحيل فإن ما تعلق به هذا العلم ليس فعلا ولا مما يصح إتفانه به ( وإنما يرد ) عليه هذا ( إن لو أراد ما يصح به إتقان متعلقه وأما لو أراد ما يصح به ) الإتقان (في الجملة ) وإن لم يكن مصححا بحسب شخصه ( فلا) ورود لهذا عليه
The fourth: by Ibn Furak: what qualifies one by whom it is established to perfect an act, i.e., to make it firm and free from aspects of defect. If he means what is independently valid, it is absolutely false. If he means what has a role in it, then power enters the definition, and our knowledge exits it, since it has no role in the correctness of perfection according to our view, for our acts are not by our bringing into existence. His statement: 'what qualifies one said etc.' – imitation (al-taqīd) and predominant opinion (al-ẓann al-ghālib) do not enter this definition, because the perfection of an act and its freeing from aspects of defect is only conceivable if one knows the harms and benefits with detailed certainty. That is why they used the perfection of the world to prove His knowledge. His statement: 'since there is no role etc.' means that perfection is the execution of bringing into existence in an accurate manner, and that is only conceivable from the originator. So his knowledge of the beneficial aspect has a role in perfection. As for the non-originator, he has no connection to bringing into existence, so perfection from him is inconceivable, since one cannot perfect the act of another. So his knowledge has no role in the correctness of perfection. As for the claim that, assuming our acts are by our bringing into existence, our knowledge would be what qualifies for perfection of the act, it is rejected. There is no proof for that, because it is an impossible supposition that may entail impossibility. Likewise what was said: 'what is meant is perfection of the act whether it is acquired (kasbī) or originated (ījādī),' since acquisition (al-kasb) is the directing of power and will towards the act, and it has no connection to bringing into existence. His statement: 'the fourth etc.' – it is not hidden that there is no role for perception being from proof, nor for it being definitive, in perfection. Rather, imitation and predominant opinion that does not occur to the mind contrary suffice, so the definition is refuted by them. His statement: 'for our acts are not by our bringing into existence' – it is answered that the correctness of perfection through it does not necessitate actual perfection. Our acquired knowledge qualifies for perfection of our acts if our acts were by our bringing into existence, given that what is meant is perfection of the act whether acquired or originated, so our knowledge is not excluded. An objection was raised after conceding that the slave's act is by his bringing into existence: our knowledge of ourselves, of the Creator, and of the impossible – what this knowledge relates to is not an act nor does it qualify for perfection through it. And this only applies if he meant what qualifies for perfection of its own object. But if he meant what qualifies for perfection in general, even if not a corrector for its own object, then this does not apply.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth definition of knowledge by Ibn Furak: 'what qualifies one to perfect an act.' The author first distinguishes two interpretations: if it means independent validity, it is false; if it means having a role in validity, then power (al-qudra) enters the definition and our knowledge is excluded, since on the Ash'ari view our acts are not our own creation. Then commentary explains that the definition excludes fallible knowledge like imitation (taqlīd) and predominant opinion, because perfection requires certain detailed knowledge. It also argues that a non-originator cannot perfect a act; so our knowledge, on the view that we do not create our acts, cannot contribute to perfection. A response suggests that even if we did create our acts, our knowledge would qualify, but the supposition is impossible. The discussion continues with an objection: our knowledge of necessary things (self, God, impossible) is not related to acts, so it would be excluded. The answer is that the definition intends 'qualifies for perfection in general,' not necessarily for its own object. This definition and its critique illustrate the complexity of defining knowledge and the interplay of causal and epistemological considerations.

Key Terms
التقليد
الظن الغالب
يقيني
تفصيلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only certain, evidence-based knowledge can lead to perfect action.
Correction: The passage shows that imitation and predominant opinion can also lead to perfect action, refuting that belief.

Checkpoint

B) They are not real knowledge at all
C) They require more certainty
D) They are only useful in religious matters

Key Takeaway
Ibn Furak's definition fails because it excludes valid forms of knowledge like taqlid and zann that can produce perfect actions.

Objection: Knowledge of Necessary Truths Excluded
Objection and answer concerning knowledge of necessary truths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that Ibn Furak's definition cannot include knowledge of necessary truths (self, God, impossible) and how the response saves the definition.

Core Idea
Knowledge of necessary truths is not about any act, challenging definitions linking knowledge to action.

A further objection is raised: even if we accept that humans create their acts, our knowledge of ourselves, of God, and of the impossible does not relate to any act, and therefore would not qualify as 'what perfects an act'. The response clarifies that the definition intends 'qualifies for perfection in general', not necessarily for its own object.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage where the objector concedes that acts are created by the agent but points out that knowledge of necessary truths is not about an act.
The knowledge in question is of the self, the Creator, and the impossible.
2
Understand the exclusion
Since such knowledge does not pertain to any act, it cannot be said to 'qualify one to perfect an act'. Thus it would be excluded from the definition.
This is a serious problem because knowledge of these matters is paradigmatic knowledge.
3
Examine the response
The author answers that the definition is meant to capture 'what qualifies for perfection in general', not necessarily for the specific object of knowledge. So even if the knowledge itself is not about an act, it still qualifies the knower to perfect acts in general.
This preserves the definition's universality.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : لابن فورك ما يصح ممن قام به إتقان الفعل) أي إحكامه وتخليته عن وجوه الخلل فإن أراد ما يستقل بالصحة فهو باطل قطعاً، وإن أراد ما له دخل فيها ( فتدخل القدرة في الحد (ويخرج) عنه ) ) علمنا إذ لا مدخل له في ( صحة الإتقان على رأينا ) فإن أفعالنا ليست بإيجادنا
قوله : (ما يصح ممن قال إلخ والتقليد والظن الغالب لا يدخلان في هذا التعريف، لأن إتقان الفعل وتخليته عن وجوه الخلل، إنما يتصور إذا كان عالماً بالمفاسد والمصالح، علماً يقينياً تفصيلياً ولذا استدلوا بإتقان العالم على علمه تعالى .
قوله : (إذ لا مدخل إلخ) يعني أن الإتقان مضاء الإيجاد على وجه الأحكام، وذلك إنما يتصور عن الموجد، فيكون لعلمه بوجه المصالح مدخل في الإتقان، وأما غير الموجد فلا تعلق له بالإيجاد فلا يتصور منه الإتقان إذ لا يمكن إتقان فعل الغير فلا مدخل لعلمه في صحة الإتقان وأما القول بأنه على تقدير فرض إيجادنا لأفعالنا يكون علمنا مما يصح به إتقان الفعل فممنوع، ولا دليل على ذلك فإنه فرض محال يجوز أن يستلزم المحال، وكذا ما قيل : إن المراد به إتقان الفعل كسبياً كان أو إيجادياً، إذ الكسب عبارة عن صرف القدرة والإرادة نحو الفعل ولا تعلق له بالإيجاد .

أورد عليه بعد تسليم أن فعل العبد بإيجاده ( علم أحدنا بنفسه وبالباري) تعالى وبالمستحيل فإن ما تعلق به هذا العلم ليس فعلا ولا مما يصح إتفانه به ( وإنما يرد ) عليه هذا ( إن لو أراد ما يصح به إتقان متعلقه وأما لو أراد ما يصح به ) الإتقان (في الجملة ) وإن لم يكن مصححا بحسب شخصه ( فلا) ورود لهذا عليه
The fourth: by Ibn Furak: what qualifies one by whom it is established to perfect an act, i.e., to make it firm and free from aspects of defect. If he means what is independently valid, it is absolutely false. If he means what has a role in it, then power enters the definition, and our knowledge exits it, since it has no role in the correctness of perfection according to our view, for our acts are not by our bringing into existence. His statement: 'what qualifies one said etc.' – imitation (al-taqīd) and predominant opinion (al-ẓann al-ghālib) do not enter this definition, because the perfection of an act and its freeing from aspects of defect is only conceivable if one knows the harms and benefits with detailed certainty. That is why they used the perfection of the world to prove His knowledge. His statement: 'since there is no role etc.' means that perfection is the execution of bringing into existence in an accurate manner, and that is only conceivable from the originator. So his knowledge of the beneficial aspect has a role in perfection. As for the non-originator, he has no connection to bringing into existence, so perfection from him is inconceivable, since one cannot perfect the act of another. So his knowledge has no role in the correctness of perfection. As for the claim that, assuming our acts are by our bringing into existence, our knowledge would be what qualifies for perfection of the act, it is rejected. There is no proof for that, because it is an impossible supposition that may entail impossibility. Rather, imitation and predominant opinion that does not occur to the mind contrary suffice, so the definition is refuted by them. His statement: 'for our acts are not by our bringing into existence' – it is answered that the correctness of perfection through it does not necessitate actual perfection. Our acquired knowledge qualifies for perfection of our acts if our acts were by our bringing into existence, given that what is meant is perfection of the act whether acquired or originated, so our knowledge is not excluded. And this only applies if he meant what qualifies for perfection of its own object. But if he meant what qualifies for perfection in general, even if not a corrector for its own object, then this does not apply.
