Objections and Responses: Knowledge of Particulars
Al-Jurjani addresses objections about knowledge of particulars and defends the critique of the added restriction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objections raised against the definition of knowledge regarding particulars and the responses defending the definition's comprehensiveness.

Core Idea
Knowledge of external particulars before sensory contact is of universal meanings, not the external entities themselves.

After presenting the definition and its critique, Al-Jurjani addresses objections. Critics argue that the definition seems to exclude knowledge of external particulars like Zayd before seeing him. In this lesson, we examine the responses that clarify that such knowledge is really of universal meanings, and that the definition's exclusion of internal particulars (like pains) is actually intended because they are not 'external entities' but subjective experiences.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the initial response
The objection claims the definition fails for knowledge of Zayd before seeing. The response distinguishes between Zayd as external entity and as universal meaning. Read carefully.
This is a key distinction in Islamic epistemology.
2
Understand the distinction between external particulars and universal meanings
The response says that before sensory perception, we have knowledge of a universal meaning that corresponds to the external entity, not the entity itself.
3
Analyze the clarification on why the addition is still harmful
The passage then clarifies that the addition is harmful for internal particulars like pains, which are not external entities. This shows the addition is still problematic.
This leads to a defense of the addition based on terminology, which is refuted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيقول صفة توجب تمييزاً لا يحتمل النقيض، ومنهم من يزيد قيداً في الحد المختار ويقول: بين المعاني الكلية، وهذه الزيادة مع الغنى عنها تخل بالطرد أي طرد الحد في جميع أفراد المحدود وجريانه فيها وشموله إياها، فهو محمول على معناه اللغوي دون الاصطلاحي إذ يخرج بها عن الحد العلم بالجزئيات كالعلم بآلامنا ولذاتنا وهذا المختار إنما هو حد للعلم عند من يقول: العلم صفة ذات تعلق بالمعلوم. قوله: (إذ المراد بها ما يقابل العينية) قيل: يرد عليهم أنهم صرحوا بأن الجزئيات العينية تدرك علماً كإدراك زيد قبل رؤيته، وإحساساً كإدراكه عند الرؤية، ومقتضى التعريف أن لا يعلم تلك الجزئيات، وأجيب بأن مثل زيد إذا أخذ جزئياً فعين وعلى وجه كلي فمعنى ولا يدرك قبل الرؤية إلا على وجه كلي كمال سيصرح به في مباحث العلم. فإن قلت: الأمر في إدراكه بعد غيبته عن الحواس مشكل. قلت: أجيب عنه أن المدرك في هذه الصورة أمر خيالي فلا يكون عيناً، وهو لا شيء محض عند المتكلمين، فليس من الأعيان بل من قبيل المعاني لكن بمطابقته للأمر الخارجي، وكونه وسيلة إلى معرفته بوجه ما اشتبه الحال. قوله: (مع الغنى عنها تخل بالطرد) ليس معنى الغنى ها هنا أن في التعريف قيداً آخر يؤدي مؤداها ويقوم مقامها وإلا فالتعريف أيضاً بدونها يخل بالطرد، بل إنه لا يحتاج إليها إذ لا فائدة لها بل لها مضرة. والأقرب أن يقال: الغنى بالنسبة إلى الجزئيات الظاهرة لأن المعاني تقابل العينية الخارجية فيخرجها والإخلال بالنسبة إلى الجزئيات الباطنية كالعلم بآلامنا ولذاتنا. قوله: (إذ يخرج بها العلم بالجزئيات) أجيب بأن من قيد المعاني بالكلية مال إلى العلم بالكليات والمعرفة بالجزئيات كما هو المشهور فلا إخلال بالطرد، وقد يدفع بأن التخصيص أمر حادث اصطلاحي، والمقصود تعريف ماهية العلم وفيه منع ظاهر أشير إليه فيما سبق، فإن مراد المجيب تخصيص إطلاق لفظ العلم بحسب أصل اللغة كما يدل عليه ما نقلته من شرح المقاصد لا تخصيص ماهيته بعد ثبوت عمومها.
He says: a quality necessitating discrimination that does not admit of the opposite, and among them is one who adds a restriction in the chosen definition and says: among universal conceptual meanings. And this addition, despite being unnecessary, harms comprehensiveness (al-tard), i.e., the definition's applicability to all individuals of the defined and its running over them and including them. So it is carried on its linguistic meaning, not the technical one. For it excludes from the definition knowledge of particulars (al-juz'iyyat), such as knowledge of our pains and pleasures. And this chosen one is only a definition of knowledge according to those who say: knowledge is a quality having a relation to the known. His statement: (since what is meant by it is what corresponds to the external) it is said: an objection is raised against them that they explicitly stated that external particulars are known as knowledge, like the knowledge of Zayd before seeing him, and as sensation, like perceiving him at the time of seeing. And the implication of the definition is that those particulars are not known. And it was answered: that like Zayd, if taken as a particular, it is an external entity, and if taken as universal, it is a meaning, and it is not perceived before seeing except in a universal way, as he will clarify in the topics of knowledge. For if you say: the matter of perceiving him after his absence from the senses is problematic. I say: it was answered that what is perceived in this form is an imaginary thing, so it is not an external entity, and it is a pure non-thing according to the mutakallimun, so it is not among external entities but rather among meanings, but due to its correspondence to the external thing and its being a means to knowing it in some way, the matter becomes ambiguous. His statement: (despite being unnecessary, it harms comprehensiveness) – the meaning of 'unnecessary' here is not that there is another restriction in the definition that serves its purpose and takes its place; otherwise, the definition without it also harms comprehensiveness; rather it is not needed because there is no benefit in it, rather it is harmful. The closest is to say: the lack of need is with respect to apparent particulars, because meanings correspond to external entities, so it excludes them, and the harm is with respect to internal particulars, like knowledge of our pains and pleasures. His statement: (for it excludes knowledge of particulars) it was answered: that whoever restricts meanings to universals tends to knowledge of universals and recognition of particulars, as is well-known, so there is no harm to comprehensiveness. And it may be refuted: that specification is an emergent terminological matter, and the intent is to define the essence of knowledge, and therein is a manifest prevention indicated previously, because the respondent's intent is the specification of the application of the word 'knowledge' according to the original language, as indicated by what I transmitted from Sharh al-Maqasid, not the specification of its essence after the establishment of its generality.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage discusses the definition of absolute knowledge. It presents the definition 'a quality necessitating discrimination that does not admit of the opposite' and then notes that some add the restriction 'among universal conceptual meanings'. The commentary explains that this addition is unnecessary and actually harms the comprehensiveness (tard) of the definition, because it excludes knowledge of particulars such as knowledge of one's own pains. An objection is raised: the definition seems to exclude knowledge of external particulars (like knowing Zayd before seeing him), but the response is that such knowledge before sensory perception is only of a universal meaning, not the particular external entity. The discussion continues on page 92 by clarifying the meaning of 'unnecessary'—it does not mean there is an alternative restriction, but that the addition has no benefit and is harmful. The harm is to internal particulars (like pains), while the exclusion of external particulars is actually intended because the definition's 'meanings' correspond to external entities. Some respond that restricting to universals is meant to distinguish knowledge from recognition, but this is defended as a terminological specification, not a definition of the essence.

Knowledge as Discrimination of a Concept in the Soul
Al-Jurjani presents and defends the definition of knowledge as discrimination of a concept in the soul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of knowledge as 'discrimination of a concept in the soul' and how al-Jurjani defends it from objections by appealing to the unity of essence and relation.

Core Idea
The best definition of knowledge is discrimination of a concept in the soul, resting on the unity of essence and relation.

Al-Jurjani presents what he considers the best definition of knowledge: the discrimination of a concept in the soul that does not admit of its opposite. This lesson unpacks this definition, explains its philosophical basis in Avicenna's principle of the unity of teaching and learning, and shows how it answers the objection that discrimination belongs to the known, not the knower.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and its justification
Read the Arabic passage once to see the definition and the claim that this is the best definition. Notice the key terms 'تميز معنى' and 'تميز لا يحتمل النقيض'.
2
Understand the philosophical basis
The definition relies on the principle that teaching and learning are one in essence but differ in relation. Similarly, obligation and being obligatory are one. Understand how this makes discrimination, when related to the soul, an act of the knower.
This resolves the objection about mixing properties.
3
Reflect on the superiority and critique
Consider why this definition is praised as 'the best' due to lack of complexity. Also note the brief critique that knowledge is a property of the knower while discrimination is a property of the concept, but the author addresses this via relative consideration.
The passage ends mid-sentence, so the full critique may continue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ومن قال: إنه نفس التعلق المخصوص بين العالم والمعلوم كما سيأتي) حده بأنه تميز معنى عند النفس تميزاً لا يحتمل النقيض، واعلم أن أحسن ما قيل. قوله: (تميز معنى عند النفس) هذا مبني على ما قال الشيخ الرئيس: إن التعليم والتعلم متحدان بالذات مختلفان بالاعتبار وما ذكره المصنف في شرح مختصر الأصول من اتحاد الإيجاب والوجوب بالذات، فالتميز إذا اعتبر نسبته إلى النفس كان تمييزاً، فلا يرد أن التميز صفة المعنى والعلم صفة العالم، فلا يجوز تعريف أحدهما بالآخر، والقول بأن المراد بالتميز ما به التميز أعني التمييز واعتمد فيه على ظهور المراد مما لا يرضى به الطبع. قوله: (إن أحسن ما قيل إلخ) لعدم التعقيد فيه بخلاف التعاريف السابقة. أن البهائم ليس من أولي العلم في شيء منهما، لكن هذا المؤيد يدل على أن الإدراك بالآلات الباطنة لا يسمى علما بهما أيضا لحصوله للبهائم فإن إدراك الجوع ونحوه حاصل لها بلا شبهة. قوله: (بأنه تميز معنى عند النفس إلخ) فيه مسامحة لأن العلم صفة العالم، والتميز صفة
His statement: (and whoever says that it is the specific relation between the knower and the known, as will come) defines it as: a discrimination of a concept in the soul, a discrimination that does not admit of the opposite. And know that the best that has been said is... His statement: (discrimination of a concept in the soul) is based on what the Chief Master (al-Shaykh al-Ra'is) said: that teaching and learning are one in essence, differing in consideration, and what the author mentioned in Sharh Mukhtasar al-Usul about the unity of obligation and being obligatory in essence. So discrimination, when its relation to the soul is considered, is discrimination. Thus it is not an objection that discrimination is a property of the concept and knowledge is a property of the knower, for it is not permissible to define one by the other. And the saying that what is meant by discrimination is that by which discrimination occurs, i.e., the act of discriminating, and relying on the apparent meaning is not acceptable to the intellect. His statement: (that the best that has been said, etc.) because of the lack of complexity in it, unlike the previous definitions. (He said) that animals are not among the possessors of knowledge in either of them, but this evidence indicates that perception by the internal faculties is not called knowledge by them either, because it occurs in animals; for perception of hunger and the like occurs in them without doubt. His statement: (that it is a discrimination of a concept in the soul, etc.) has some imprecision, because knowledge is a property of the knower, and discrimination is a property...
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage presents the definition of knowledge according to those who hold that knowledge is the specific relation between the knower and the known. The definition is 'discrimination of a concept in the soul, a discrimination that does not admit of the opposite'. The author then says 'and know that the best that has been said' — implying this definition is the best. The commentary explains that this definition is based on the principle of Avicenna (al-Shaykh al-Ra'is) that teaching and learning are identical in essence but differ in consideration, and similarly obligation and being obligatory are identical. Thus discrimination, when considered in relation to the soul, is the act of discrimination, not a property of the concept. This answers the objection that discrimination belongs to the concept, not the knower. The definition is praised for its lack of complexity compared to earlier definitions. Then the passage states that animals are not considered possessors of knowledge, which shows that internal perceptions (like hunger) are not called 'knowledge' in either definition (the quality-definition or the relation-definition). Finally, a brief critique is noted: the definition has some imprecision because knowledge is a quality of the knower while discrimination is a quality of the known, but the commentary suggests this is resolved by the relative consideration.

The Definition of Knowledge and the Critique of 'Among Universal Meanings'
Defines knowledge and critiques the added restriction 'among universal meanings'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why adding 'among universal meanings' to the definition of knowledge harms its comprehensiveness (tard).

Core Idea
Adding 'universal' to knowledge's definition excludes valid knowledge of particulars.

The definition of knowledge is foundational in Islamic theology. The author presents the standard definition as an attribute that entails a non-opposable distinction. Some scholars add 'among universal meanings' to restrict knowledge to universals, but this addition is criticized for being unnecessary and for excluding knowledge of particulars, such as our own pains and pleasures.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the base definition and the added phrase 'among universal meanings'.
Pay attention to the logical flow: definition, addition, critique, and alternative definition.
2
Identify the core definition and the addition
The base definition is 'an attribute that necessitates a distinction that does not admit its opposite'. The addition is 'among universal meanings'. Ask: what does this addition aim to do?
3
Understand the critique of the addition
The commentator argues the addition is unnecessary and disrupts tard (comprehensiveness). Find the reason: it excludes knowledge of particulars like pains and pleasures, which are valid instances of knowledge.
Tard here means the definition covers all individuals of the defined.
4
Note the alternative definitions
The passage mentions that for those who define knowledge as the specific relation itself, the definition changes slightly. Compare the two versions.
5
Clarify the commentator's notes on 'tard'
The commentator explains that the objection about reversing terms is invalid because 'tard' means prevention (of non-individuals) and 'aks' means inclusion.
This resolves a potential confusion about the technical terms.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Second Observatory: On the Definition of Absolute Knowledge. He says: an attribute that necessitates a distinction that does not admit its opposite. And some of them add a restriction to the chosen definition and say: among universal meanings. And this addition, despite being unnecessary, disrupts the comprehensiveness (al-tard) meaning the comprehensiveness of the definition over all individuals of the defined, its running over them, and its including them; it is thus applied in its linguistic sense, not the technical one, because it excludes from the definition knowledge of particulars, such as knowledge of our pains and pleasures. And this chosen definition is only a definition of knowledge according to those who say: knowledge is an attribute having a relation to the known. And whoever says: it is the specific relation itself between the knower and the known, as will come, defines it as: a distinction of a meaning in the soul, a distinction that does not admit its opposite. And know that the best that has been said... His statement: 'despite being unnecessary for it' — because it does not benefit excluding something that is not an individual of the defined, it disrupts the inclusion, not that it excludes some individuals of the defined. His statement: 'it is thus applied, etc.' — so it is not objected that the correct is the opposite, because 'tard' (comprehensiveness) is prevention, and 'aks' (converse) is inclusion.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage defines absolute knowledge and critiques a proposed addition to the definition. The author begins by presenting the definition of knowledge as an attribute that necessitates a distinction that does not admit its opposite. Some scholars add the restriction 'among universal meanings' to limit knowledge to universals. The commentator argues that this addition is unnecessary and actually harms the definition's comprehensiveness (tard). He explains that 'tard' here refers to the definition covering all individuals of the defined, in its linguistic sense, not the technical sense of 'preventing non-individuals'. The addition excludes knowledge of particulars, like knowledge of our own pains and pleasures, which is clearly a form of knowledge. The passage then notes that the chosen definition applies to those who hold that knowledge is an attribute related to the known; for those who hold that knowledge is the specific relation itself, the definition uses 'distinction of a meaning in the soul'. The commentator then explains the phrase 'despite being unnecessary for it' — meaning the addition does not help exclude things that are genuinely not individuals of the defined, but it does exclude legitimate individuals. He also clarifies that the objection about reversing the terms is invalid because 'tard' means prevention and 'aks' means inclusion, not the opposite.

Knowledge as Distinction: Definition and Defense
Definition and defense of knowledge as distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of knowledge as 'distinction of a meaning in the soul' and the main objections and defenses surrounding it.

Core Idea
Knowledge is a distinction that leaves no room for its opposite.

This passage examines a definition of knowledge that builds on the idea of a specific relation between knower and known. The definition 'distinction of a meaning in the soul' aims to avoid previous complexities but faces its own objections. Our commentary will unpack the reasoning behind it, showing how scholars defended this formulation.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and its basis
Carefully read the Arabic passage that defines knowledge as 'distinction of a meaning in the soul, a distinction that does not admit its opposite.' Note that this definition is for those who hold that knowledge is a specific relation. Then read the translation.
The definition is concise; pay attention to the phrase 'does not admit its opposite'.
2
Identify the key principle from Avicenna
The passage grounds the definition in Avicenna's principle that teaching and learning are identical in essence but differ in consideration, and a similar idea from al-Musannif about affirmation and obligation. This principle allows distinction to be considered as an attribute of the knower when related to the soul.
This avoids the objection that distinction belongs to the meaning, not the knower.
3
Understand the objection and the commentator's criticism
The commentator notes a looseness: knowledge is an attribute of the knower, but distinction seems to be an attribute of the meaning. The claim that 'distinction in the soul' makes it an attribute of the knower is refuted by the commentator's own glosses. The preferred solution is to interpret 'distinction' as 'that by which distinction occurs', i.e., discrimination.
This resolution shows the importance of precise terminology in kalam.
4
Reflect on the overall teaching
The commentator concludes that the best definition is the one without complexity, unlike previous definitions. This underscores the value of clear and simple formulations in theological discourse.
Contrast this definition with the earlier ones mentioned in the lesson.

ومن قال : إنه نفس التعلق المخصوص بين العالم والمعلوم كما سيأتي ( حده بأنه تميز معنى عند النفس تميزاً لا يحتمل النقيض)، واعلم أن أحسن ما قيل
نقیض
قوله: تميز معنى عند النفس هذا مبني على ما قال الشيخ الرئيس: إن التعليم والتعلم متحدان بالذات مختلفان بالاعتبار وما ذكره المصنف في شرح مختصر الأصول من اتحاد الإيجاب والوجوب بالذات، فالتميز إذا اعتبر نسبته إلى النفس كان تمييزاً، فلا يرد أن التميز صفة المعنى والعلم صفة العالم، فلا يجوز تعريف أحدهما بالآخر، والقول بأن المراد بالتميز ما به التميز أعني التمييز واعتمد فيه على ظهور المراد مما لا يرضى به الطبع .
قوله : ( إن أحسن ما قيل : إِلخ ) لعدم التعقيد فيه بخلاف التعاريف السابقة.

قوله : ( بأنه تميز معنى عند النفس إلخ) فيه مسامحة لأن العلم صفة العالم، والتميز صفة
المعنى الذي هو معلوم والقول بأن تميزه عند النفس صفة العالم، وإن كان التميز المجرد صفة المعنى مدفوع بما حققه الشارح في أوائل البيان في حواشي المطول، بل المراد ما به التميز أعني التمييز واعتمد فيه على ظهور المراد.
And whoever says that it is the specific relation itself between the knower and the known, as will come, defines it as: a distinction of a meaning in the soul, a distinction that does not admit its opposite. And know that the best that has been said... His statement: 'distinction of a meaning in the soul' is based on what the Chief Master (al-Shaykh al-Ra'is) said: that teaching and learning are identical in essence but different in consideration, and what the author (al-Musannif) mentioned in Sharh Mukhtasar al-Usul about the identity of affirmation and obligation in essence. So the distinction, when its relation to the soul is considered, becomes a distinction. Thus it is not objected that distinction is an attribute of the meaning and knowledge is an attribute of the knower, so it is not permissible to define one by the other. And the statement that what is meant by distinction is that by which distinction occurs, i.e., discrimination, and reliance on the apparent meaning is not satisfactory to the intellect. His statement: 'The best that has been said, etc.' is due to the lack of complexity in it, unlike the previous definitions. His statement: 'that it is a distinction of a meaning in the soul, etc.' has a looseness because knowledge is an attribute of the knower, and distinction is an attribute of the meaning which is known. The claim that its distinction in the soul is an attribute of the knower, even if bare distinction is an attribute of the meaning, is refuted by what the commentator (al-Sharih) established in the beginning of al-Bayan in the glosses on al-Mutawwal. Rather, what is meant is that by which distinction occurs, i.e., discrimination, and reliance on the apparent meaning.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of definitions of knowledge from the perspective that knowledge is a specific relation. For those who hold that knowledge is the relation itself, the definition is restated as: a distinction of a meaning in the soul that does not admit its opposite. The commentator notes that this definition is based on a principle from Avicenna (al-Shaykh al-Ra'is) that teaching and learning are identical in essence but differ in consideration, and similarly, the author (al-Musannif) in his commentary on Mukhtasar al-Usul states that affirmation and obligation are identical in essence. Thus, when distinction is considered relative to the soul, it becomes a distinction. This avoids the objection that distinction is an attribute of the meaning while knowledge is an attribute of the knower, so one cannot be defined by the other. However, the commentator criticizes relying on the apparent meaning to avoid this objection, saying it is not intellectually satisfying. He then remarks that the best definition is one without complexity, unlike previous definitions. Finally, he points out a looseness in the definition 'distinction of a meaning in the soul' because knowledge is an attribute of the knower, but distinction is an attribute of the meaning. This objection is refuted by appealing to the commentator's glosses, but the preferred interpretation is that 'distinction' means 'that by which distinction occurs', i.e., discrimination.

The Best Definition of Knowledge: Disclosure (Tajalli)
Presents and defends the best definition of knowledge as complete disclosure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the definition of knowledge as complete disclosure (tajalli) excludes conjecture, compound ignorance, and even correct belief of a blind follower, and how the commentator defends this definition against objections.

Core Idea
True knowledge is complete disclosure with no doubt, not mere correct belief.

This lesson examines the celebrated definition of knowledge as an attribute by which the mentioned thing is fully disclosed to the knower. Al-Sanusi presents this definition and immediately shows its power: it excludes not only mere conjecture and compound ignorance, but also the seemingly correct belief of a blind follower. We will see how the commentator defends this strict standard against objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and its scope
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice that knowledge is defined as an attribute (sifa) that discloses the mentioned thing (al-madhkur) to the knower. The scope of 'madhkur' includes all possible objects: existent, non-existent, possible, impossible, simple, compound, universal, particular.
This is a very comprehensive definition deliberately crafted to cover all knowable objects.
2
Understand the exclusions
The definition explicitly excludes conjecture (zan), compound ignorance (jahl murakkab), and even the correct belief of a blind follower (muqallid musib). Understand why each fails: they lack complete disclosure and are likened to a 'knot on the heart'.
The exclusion of correct blind belief is surprising but central to the definition's rigour.
3
Consider the objection about 'complete'
The commentator raises an objection: if 'tajalli' just means manifestation, why add 'complete' (tam)? The response: the absolute term immediately implies perfection, and the common understanding of manifestation is the complete one.
This shows how careful the author is to avoid unnecessary additions.
4
See the answer about future doubt
Another objection: 'complete' is ambiguous and taqlid may seem to have no present doubt. The answer: the definition requires no doubt at present or in the future, and future absence of doubt is known because such knowledge admits no opposite whatsoever.
This reinforces the absolute certainty required for true knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الكشف عن ماهية العلم هو إنه صفة يتجلى بها المذكور لمن قامت هي به فالمذكور يتناول الموجود والمعدوم الممكن والمستحيل بلا خلاف ويتناول المفرد والمركب والكلي والجزئي والتجلي هو الانكشاف التام فالمعنى أنه صفة ينكشف بها لمن قامت به ما من شأنه أن يذكر انكشافاً تاماً لا اشتباه فيه، فيخرج الحد الظن والجهل المركب واعتقاد المقلد المصيب أيضاً، لأنه في الحقيقة عقدة على القلب فليس فيه انكشاف تام وانشراح تنحل به العقدة.

قوله : ( والتجلي هو الانكشاف التام إما لأن صيغة التفعل للمبالغة كالتكبر، وإما لأن المطلق ينصرف إلى الكامل.
قوله: (لمن قامت) يخرج به النور فإنه يتجلى به لغير من قامت به، واختار كلمة لإخراج التجلي الحاصل للحيوانات العجم .
من
المعنى الذي هو معلوم والقول بأن تميزه عند النفس صفة العالم، وإن كان التميز المجرد صفة المعنى مدفوع بما حققه الشارح في أوائل البيان في حواشي المطول، بل المراد ما به التميز أعني التمييز واعتمد فيه على ظهور المراد .
قوله : ( والتجلي هو الانكشاف التام فإن قلت : التجلي هو الانكشاف مطلقاً، فالتقييد بالتام عناية في التعريف وذا غير جائز قلت : لو سلم فالمتبادر من المطلق الكامل منه، وحمل التعريف على المتبادر مما يجب، نعم يرد أن فيه جهالة لأن تمامه عبارة عن أي شيء غير معلوم، والانكشاف بلا دغدغة حالية موجودة في التقليد والجهل ،المركب والجواب أنه عبارة عما لا دغدغة فيه لا حالاً ولا مالاً فإن قلت : انتفاء الدغدغة في المآل لم يعلم، قلت: مما يعلم
عدم احتمال النقيض بوجه من الوجوه .
منه
In unveiling the essence of knowledge, it is that it is an attribute by which the mentioned thing (al-madhkur) is disclosed (tajalla) to the one in whom it is present. So the mentioned thing includes the existent and the non-existent, the possible and the impossible, without dispute, and includes the simple and the compound, the universal and the particular. And disclosure (tajalli) is complete manifestation (inkishaf tam). So the meaning is that it is an attribute by which what is apt to be mentioned is disclosed to the one in whom it is present, a complete manifestation with no ambiguity in it. Thus the definition excludes conjecture (zan), compound ignorance (jahl murakkab), and even the correct belief of the blind follower (muqallid musib), because it is in reality a knot on the heart, so there is no complete manifestation and expansion that loosens the knot. His statement: 'And disclosure is complete manifestation', either because the tafa''ul form indicates intensification, like takabbur, or because the absolute term refers to the perfect. His statement: 'to the one in whom it is present' excludes light (nur), for it is disclosed to others than the one in whom it is present. And he chose the word 'for' to exclude the disclosure that occurs for dumb animals. His statement: 'that the meaning which is known...' and the claim that its distinction in the soul is an attribute of the knower... [continues from previous lesson? Actually the text jumps a bit but we include the relevant part.] His statement: 'And disclosure is complete manifestation.' If you say: disclosure is manifestation absolutely, then restricting it to 'complete' is a stipulation in the definition, and that is not permissible. I say: Even if granted, the immediate understanding from the absolute is the complete one, and carrying the definition to the immediate understanding is obligatory. Yes, it is objected that there is ambiguity because 'its completeness' is not known what it is, and manifestation without any present doubt exists in taqlid and compound ignorance. The answer is that it is what has no doubt, neither present nor future. If you say: the absence of doubt in the future is not known, I say: it is known that it does not admit the opposite in any way.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage presents and defends the best definition of knowledge: it is an attribute by which the mentioned thing (al-madhkur) is disclosed (tajalla) to the knower. The definition covers all types of objects: existent, non-existent, possible, impossible, simple, compound, universal, particular. 'Disclosure' is defined as complete manifestation, which excludes conjecture, compound ignorance, and even correct belief of a blind follower, because such belief is a knot on the heart without full expansion. The commentator explains that the tafa''ul form of 'tajalli' indicates intensity, or the absolute term implies perfection. The phrase 'to the one in whom it is present' excludes things like light, which disclose to others. The definition also excludes animals' perception. The commentator then addresses an objection: if 'tajalli' is just manifestation absolutely, then adding 'complete' is an unnecessary restriction. He responds that the absolute term immediately conveys the complete sense. Further objection: 'complete' is ambiguous and taqlid may seem to have no present doubt. The answer: the definition means no doubt at present or in the future, and future absence of doubt is known because such knowledge admits no opposite.

Correction: The definition of knowledge requires complete disclosure, not just correct belief. The blind follower's belief is a knot on the heart without genuine intellectual expansion, so it is excluded.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge is complete, unambiguous disclosure of the object; any belief that harbors potential doubt or is based on blind acceptance does not qualify.

?

Defining Absolute Knowledge (al-ʿilm al-muṭlaq) and Its Exclusions
Defines absolute knowledge as complete disclosure and lists exclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical scholars define absolute knowledge as a quality of complete disclosure, and which types of cognition it excludes.

Core Idea
Knowledge is complete, unquestioning disclosure — anything less is not knowledge.

In this passage, the author defines 'ilm (knowledge) as a quality that brings about complete disclosure of the object known. This is a core definition in Islamic epistemology. The definition is then refined by excluding things that fall short of complete certainty, such as guesswork, compound ignorance, and even correct belief without evidence. The commentary also defends the phrase 'complete disclosure' against objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get its rhythm, then read the translation. Notice the compressed style and the key terms.
The passage includes both the main definition and a commentary with objections and replies.
2
Understand the core definition
Identify the definition: knowledge is a quality (ṣifa) by which the mentioned thing (al-madhkūr) becomes manifest (tajallā) to the subject with complete disclosure (inkishāf tāmm).
The term 'mentioned thing' covers all entities: existent, non-existent, possible, impossible, simple, compound, universal, particular.
3
Identify the three exclusions
Find the three things excluded from the definition: guesswork (ẓann), compound ignorance (jahl murakkab), and the correct belief of the blind follower (muqallid muṣīb). Note why each is excluded.
They lack complete disclosure because they involve a 'knot on the heart' or unresolved doubt.
4
Follow the commentary's defense
The commentary clarifies why 'complete disclosure' is needed and responds to objections. Notice how it argues that the absolute form implies perfection and that 'complete' means absence of qualm both present and future.
The defense shows the rigor of kalam reasoning.
5
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: why define knowledge so strictly? This definition ensures that only certain, unquestionable cognition counts as knowledge, which is essential for theological certainty.
This connects to the broader purpose of kalam: establishing firm belief in Allah's attributes.

المرصد الثاني - في تعريف مطلق العلم
في الكشف عن ماهية العلم هو إنه صفة يتجلى بها المذكور لمن قامت هي به فالمذكور يتناول الموجود والمعدوم الممكن والمستحيل بلا خلاف ويتناول المفرد والمركب والكلي والجزئي والتجلي هو الانكشاف التام فالمعنى أنه صفة ينكشف بها لمن قامت به ما من شأنه أن يذكر انكشافاً تاماً لا اشتباه فيه، فيخرج الحد الظن والجهل المركب واعتقاد المقلد المصيب أيضاً، لأنه في الحقيقة عقدة على القلب فليس فيه انكشاف تام وانشراح تنحل به العقدة.
عن
قوله : ( والتجلي هو الانكشاف التام إما لأن صيغة التفعل للمبالغة كالتكبر، وإما لأن المطلق ينصرف إلى الكامل.
قوله: (لمن قامت) يخرج به النور فإنه يتجلى به لغير من قامت به، واختار كلمة لإخراج التجلي الحاصل للحيوانات العجم .
من
المعنى الذي هو معلوم والقول بأن تميزه عند النفس صفة العالم، وإن كان التميز المجرد صفة المعنى مدفوع بما حققه الشارح في أوائل البيان في حواشي المطول، بل المراد ما به التميز أعني التمييز واعتمد فيه على ظهور المراد .
قوله : ( والتجلي هو الانكشاف التام فإن قلت : التجلي هو الانكشاف مطلقاً، فالتقييد بالتام عناية في التعريف وذا غير جائز قلت : لو سلم فالمتبادر من المطلق الكامل منه، وحمل التعريف على المتبادر مما يجب، نعم يرد أن فيه جهالة لأن تمامه عبارة عن أي شيء غير معلوم، والانكشاف بلا دغدغة حالية موجودة في التقليد والجهل المركب والجواب أنه عبارة عما لا دغدغة فيه لا حالاً ولا مالاً فإن قلت : انتفاء الدغدغة في المآل لم يعلم، قلت: مما يعلم عدم احتمال النقيض بوجه من الوجوه .
منه
The second observation - on the definition of absolute knowledge. In uncovering the essence of knowledge: it is a quality by which the mentioned thing becomes manifest to the one in whom that quality subsists. The 'mentioned thing' encompasses the existent and the non-existent, the possible and the impossible without dispute, and it encompasses the simple and the compound, the universal and the particular. 'Manifestation' (tajallī) is complete disclosure (al-inkishāf al-tāmm). So the meaning is that it is a quality by which what is liable to be mentioned becomes disclosed to the one in whom it subsists, with a complete disclosure in which there is no ambiguity. Thus it excludes guesswork (al-ẓann), compound ignorance (al-jahl al-murakkab), and also the correct belief of the blind follower (al-muqallid al-muṣīb), because it is in reality a knot upon the heart, so there is not in it complete disclosure and expansion by which the knot is untied. ... (then commentary) He said: 'And manifestation is complete disclosure' either because the form tafa‘ʿul is for intensification like takabbur, or because the absolute (al-muṭlaq) is taken to refer to the perfect. He said: 'To the one in whom it subsists' excludes light (al-nūr), for it becomes manifest to other than the one in whom it subsists. And he chose the word 'to exclude the manifestation that occurs for brute animals. ... He said: 'And manifestation is complete disclosure' for if you say: 'Manifestation is disclosure absolutely, so restricting it to complete is an arbitrary addition to the definition, and that is not permissible,' I say: 'Even if conceded, the immediate understanding from the absolute is the perfect one, and it is necessary to apply the definition to what is immediately understood.' Yes, it is objected that there is ignorance in it, because its completeness is an expression for something unknown. And disclosure without any present qualm exists in blind following and compound ignorance. The answer: it is an expression for that which has no qualm neither present nor future. For if you say: 'The absence of qualm in the future is not known,' I say: 'It is known by the impossibility of the opposite in any respect.'
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage defines absolute knowledge (ʿilm) and explains the components of its definition with commentary. It begins by stating that knowledge is a quality (ṣifa) by which the mentioned thing (al-madhkūr) becomes manifest to the one who possesses this quality. The term 'mentioned thing' is broad, covering existent and non-existent, possible and impossible, and all types of concepts. The manifestation is specified as complete disclosure (al-inkishāf al-tāmm), meaning full and unambiguous revelation. This excludes guesswork (ẓann), compound ignorance (jahl murakkab), and even correct belief of a blind follower, because these involve a knot or qualm, not full disclosure. The commentary then defends the phrase 'complete disclosure' by noting that the Arabic form tafaʿʿul often indicates intensity, and the absolute term is understood to imply perfection. It also explains that the specification 'to the one in whom it subsists' excludes external things like light that manifest to others. Further, it addresses an objection: if manifestation is absolute, adding 'complete' is arbitrary. The response is that the absolute immediately suggests the perfect, and it is necessary to apply definitions accordingly. Another objection is that 'complete' is unknown and that blind following and compound ignorance also have no present qualm. The answer is that complete disclosure means absence of qualm both present and future, which is known by the impossibility of the opposite. Thus the definition is defended.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The definition excludes all forms of non-certain knowledge, including probable knowledge.
Correction: The definition only excludes those that lack complete disclosure, such as guesswork and blind following, not all probable knowledge. It focuses on the absence of any qualm or doubt.

Checkpoint
According to the definition, why is the correct belief of the blind follower (muqallid muṣīb) excluded from knowledge?
A) Because it is not based on evidence.
B) Because it lacks complete disclosure and involves a knot on the heart.
C) Because it is a type of guesswork.
D) Because it is not about the mentioned thing.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge, for classical scholars, is not merely true belief but requires a state of complete, unquestioned disclosure where the opposite is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage says that even correct belief from blind following is 'a knot upon the heart' not true knowledge. In what areas of your faith do you hold beliefs simply because you were taught them, without having that complete disclosure and personal conviction? Will you commit this week to studying one such belief until you can say you are certain beyond any doubt?
?
Complete disclosure requires that there is no qualm, not even in the future. Is there any doctrine or command of Allah that you still have a lurking possibility of doubt in your heart? What practical step can you take today to remove that possibility, such as seeking deeper evidence or asking a scholar?
?
If knowledge is complete disclosure, then how does your salah reflect your knowledge of Allah? Are you truly present, fully disclosed in His greatness, or is there a knot of distraction? Resolve to recite Al-Fatiha in your next prayer with such presence that it becomes a moment of complete disclosure.

The Correct Division: Bare Conception and Assent
The author states the correct division of knowledge and clarifies a potential confusion in describing assent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the correct view divides knowledge into bare conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq) without making judgment (hukm) a separate part.

Core Idea
Knowledge is either bare conception or conception accompanied by judgment.

This lesson introduces the foundational classification of knowledge in Islamic logic and theology. The author argues against an incorrect division that makes judgment a third component. Instead, he establishes that assent (tasdiq) is a conception accompanied by judgment, but judgment itself is not a distinct kind of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Note the author's concise style.
2
Identify the correct division
The passage states that judgment should not be a part of knowledge. Find the exact wording and note the two acceptable parts of knowledge.
The two parts are tasawwur (bare conception) and tasdiq (conception with judgment).
3
Understand the problem with descriptions of tasdiq
The passage points out that descriptions like 'self-evident' apply to judgment, not to the conception part of tasdiq. Consider why this is a problem.
This shows the subtlety of the division.

Source Text

قوله : فالصواب إلخ) أي الصواب أن لا يجعل الحكم نفسه ولا المركب منه، ومن غيره قسماً من العلم وأما إطلاق التصديق على التصور المقارن للحكم حتى ينقسم العلم إلى تصور ساذج، وإلى التصديق أي تصور معه حكم كما يتبادر من عبارة متن الكتاب فجائز، لكن يخالف وصف التصديق بالبداهة والظنية وغيرها فإنها أوصاف للحكم لا للتصور المقارن له، إلا أن يتسامح فيوصف ذلك التصور بوصف عارض له، وإنه تعسف . قوله : ( إلى تصور ساذج إلخ) والمقصود من التقسيم ظهور ذلك العارض المنفرد من معروضه بكاسب مخصوص وقد جعل بعضهم لفظ العلم مشتركاً بين المعروض، وذلك العارض، وقسم العلم إليهما فكأنه قيل : ما يطلق عليه لفظ العلم، إما تصور وإما حكم، وهو التصديق وتكلف آخرون بجعل الاشتراك معنوياً فقالوا : كان الأوائل قسموا المعاني الذهنية إلى نفس الإدراك، وإلى ما يلحقه وقسموا ما يلحقه إلى ما يجعله محتملاً للصدق والكذب، وإلى ما لا يجعله كذلك كالهيئات اللاحقة به في الأمر والنهي، والاستفهام والتمني وغير ذلك، وسموا المشترك بين القسمين الأولين علماً هذا كله على أن الحكم فعل، والصواب خلافه كذا نقل عنه . قوله: (كما وقع إلخ) وفي بعض النسخ كما ورد أي تقسيماً مماثلاً لما ورد في الكتب قوله : (كما توهمه العبارات إلخ قال الشارح في حواشي المطالع : لا عبرة بإيهام تلك العبارات فإن أهل اللغة لا يفرقون بين القبول والفعل، ويسمون القابل اسم فاعل والمقبول اسم مفعول وفيه نظر، إذ ليس الكلام في لفظ الفعل، والانفعال بل في مثل الإسناد، والإيقاع، ولا شك أن أهل اللغة وضعوها بإزاء الفعل فلا يجوز استعمالها بطريق الحقيقة في الكيف، والانفعال إلا مجازاً وهذا كما أنهم وضعوا بإزاء الفعل نحو الكسر وبإزاء الانفعال نحو الانكسار، فلا تقريب لما ذكره نعم لو استدل على فعلية الحكم بأن أهل اللغة يطلقون عليه الفعل وعلى الحاكم الفاعل، وعلى المحكوم به المفعول به لكان فيما ذكره تقريب ظاهر . قوله : ( فالصواب أن يقسم العلم إلخ) فعلى هذا يلزم توقف التصديق على خمسة أشياء. قوله : ( كما ورد في بعض الكتب المعتبرة قيل عليه قاسم العلم إلى القسمين المذكورين بعض الكتب المعتبرة هو أبو علي بن سينا، كما نقله في شرح المطالع، والحكم عنده إدراك لا فعل، فما ذكره صلح لا عن تراضي الخصمين والجواب أن مراد الشارح أن الصواب حينئذ أن يقسم مطلق العلم إلى القسمين المذكورين والشيخ إنما قسم إليهما العلم التصوري لا مطلق العلم، كما صرح به الشارح في حواشيه على ذلك الشرح، فإن أراد ببعض الكتب المعتبرة غير كتاب الشيخ فالأمر ظاهر وإن أراد كتابه فالضمير في ورد راجع إلى تقسيم العلم بالمعنى ولعارضه المسمى بالحكم والتصديق طريق خاص آخر، وأما جعل التصديق قسماً من العلم مع تركيبه من الحكم، وغيره فلا وجه له فعلاً كان الحكم، أو إدراكاً
(His saying: 'The correct view, etc.') i.e., the correct view is that one should not make the judgment itself or the composite of it and something else a part of knowledge. As for applying 'tasdiq' to the conception accompanying judgment so that knowledge is divided into bare conception and into tasdiq—i.e., a conception with a judgment—as understood from the phrasing of the text of the book, it is permissible. However, it contradicts the description of tasdiq as self-evident, probable, etc., for these are descriptions of the judgment, not of the conception accompanying it, unless one is lenient and describes that conception by an accident accruing to it, and that is forced. His saying: (into bare conception, etc.) And the purpose of the division is the manifestation of that isolated accident from its subject by a specific acquisitive means. Some have made the word 'ilm' common between the subject and that accident, and divided knowledge into the two: as if it were said, 'What is called knowledge is either conception or judgment, which is tasdiq.' Others, by making the commonality conceptual, said: The early scholars divided mental meanings into pure perception and into what attaches to it, and they divided what attaches to it into that which makes it capable of truth and falsehood, and into that which does not make it so, such as the forms that attach to it in command, prohibition, interrogation, wish, etc. They called the common between the first two divisions 'ilm.' All this is on the view that judgment is an act, but the correct view is contrary to this, as it is transmitted from him. His saying: (as occurred, etc.) And in some copies 'as was reported,' i.e., a division similar to what was reported in the books. His saying: (as the expressions suggest, etc.) The commentator said in the glosses of al-Matali': 'No attention is paid to the suggestion of those expressions, for the people of language do not distinguish between acceptance and act; they call the acceptor a subject (ism fa'il) and the accepted an object (ism maf'ul).' There is a consideration, for the discussion is not about the word 'act' and 'passivity,' but about such things as predication, effectuation, etc. There is no doubt that the people of language have placed these expressions for the act, so it is not permissible to use them in a real sense for the quality and passivity except metaphorically. This is like how they placed for the act such as 'breaking' and for passivity such as 'being broken.' So there is no approximation in what he mentioned. Yes, if one argued for the actuality of judgment by saying that the people of language apply 'act' to it, 'agent' to the one judging, and 'object acted upon' to the thing judged, then there would be a clear approximation in what he mentioned. His saying: (So the correct view is to divide knowledge, etc.) According to this, tasdiq would depend on five things. His saying: (as reported in some authoritative books) It was said against him: 'The one who divided knowledge into the two mentioned divisions from some authoritative books is Abu Ali ibn Sina, as transmitted in Sharh al-Matali', and judgment according to him is perception, not an act. So what he mentioned is a reconciliation, not from the mutual satisfaction of the two opponents.' The answer is that the commentator intended that the correct view then is to divide absolute knowledge into the two mentioned divisions, while the Shaykh only divided conceptual knowledge, not absolute knowledge, as the commentator clarified in his glosses on that commentary. If he intended by 'some authoritative books' other than the Shaykh's book, the matter is clear; if he intended his book, then the pronoun in 'occurred' refers to the division of knowledge in the sense. And for its accident called judgment and tasdiq, there is another specific path. As for making tasdiq a part of knowledge while it is composed of judgment and something else, it has no justification, whether judgment is an act or perception.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is a commentary defending the correct division of knowledge into tasawwur (conception) and tasdiq (assent). It begins by stating that the correct view is not to make judgment (hukm) itself or a composite of judgment and conception a separate part of knowledge. The division into bare conception (tasawwur saadhij) and conception accompanied by judgment (tasdiq) is permissible, but there is a problem: descriptions like 'self-evident' or 'probable' apply to the judgment, not to the accompanying conception, unless one is lax. The passage then explains that the purpose of the division is to show that each type has its own acquisitive means. Some scholars made the term 'ilm' common between the subject (the conception) and the accident (the judgment), but others insisted on a conceptual commonality: the early scholars divided mental meanings into pure perception and its attachments, then subdivided attachments into those that allow truth/falsity and those that do not (e.g., imperative, interrogative forms). This was based on judgment being an act, but the correct view rejects that. Next, a linguistic argument is refuted: the commentator of al-Matali' notes that language does not equate grammatical labels with metaphysical reality; words like 'predication' (isnad) are placed for acts, not for qualities or passions. However, if one argues from language (calling judgment 'act' and the judge 'agent') it would be more plausible. Then the passage addresses an objection: the correct division leads to tasdiq depending on five things. It clarifies that Ibn Sina's division was for conceptual knowledge only, not absolute knowledge, so the objection is answered. Finally, it concludes that there is no justification for making tasdiq a separate part of knowledge, whether judgment is an act or a perception.

Key Terms
تصور (tasawwur)
تصديق (tasdiq)
حكم (hukm)
علم ('ilm)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Judgment (hukm) is a third part of knowledge alongside conception and assent.
Correction: The correct view is that judgment is not a separate part; knowledge is only divided into bare conception and conception with judgment (assent).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the correct division of knowledge?
A) Conception, judgment, and assent as three parts
B) Bare conception and conception with judgment (tasdiq) as two parts
C) Judgment is the only part
D) Knowledge is indivisible

Key Takeaway
The correct division of knowledge is into bare conception and conception with judgment (tasdiq), and judgment itself is not a separate part.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say you 'know' something in your daily life, do you ever distinguish between simply having a concept in your mind and actually judging it to be true? How does this lesson challenge your usual way of thinking about knowledge?
?
The author warns against describing the conception with the attributes of the judgment. In your own spiritual life, when you recite the Shahada, do you feel a clear separation between your mental image of God and your judgment of His oneness? Which one do you find more subject to doubt?
?
Do you ever treat your assumptions as self-evident truths without examining the judgment behind them? What would it take for you to critically evaluate the 'self-evident' beliefs you hold?

Purpose of Division and Scholarly Views on 'Ilm
The author explains the purpose of the division and discusses different theories of the term 'ilm'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the purpose of dividing knowledge into conception and assent, and the different scholarly interpretations of the term 'ilm'.

Core Idea
The division reveals the unique acquisitive means of each type of knowledge.

The author now explains why the division into bare conception and assent is meaningful: it shows that each has its own method of acquisition. He then reviews various scholarly attempts to unify the term 'ilm' across conception and judgment, including the view that 'ilm' is common between subject and accident, and the conceptual commonality approach of early scholars.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the shift from defending the division to explaining its purpose.
2
Identify the purpose of the division
Find the sentence that states the purpose of the division. It mentions 'ظهور ذلك العارض المنفرد من معروضه بكاسب مخصوص'.
The purpose is to show that each type has its own means of acquisition.
3
Examine the two scholarly views
The passage presents two views on the term 'ilm': one considers it common between subject and accident, another considers it conceptually common. Note the details of each view.
Both views assume judgment is an act, but the correct view is different.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary defending the correct division of knowledge into tasawwur (conception) and tasdiq (assent). It begins by stating that the correct view is not to make judgment (hukm) itself or a composite of judgment and conception a separate part of knowledge. Next, a linguistic argument is refuted: the commentator of al-Matali' notes that language does not equate grammatical labels with metaphysical reality; words like 'predication' (isnad) are placed for acts, not for qualities or passions. Then the passage addresses an objection: the correct division leads to tasdiq depending on five things. It clarifies that Ibn Sina's division was for conceptual knowledge only, not absolute knowledge, so the objection is answered. Finally, it concludes that there is no justification for making tasdiq a separate part of knowledge, whether judgment is an act or a perception.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'ilm' has a univocal meaning across conception and judgment.

Checkpoint
What is the purpose of dividing knowledge into conception and assent according to the passage?
A) To distinguish between true and false beliefs
B) To show that each type has its own means of acquisition
C) To prove that judgment is an act
D) To reject the early scholars' views

Key Takeaway
The division of knowledge serves to highlight that each type has its own means of acquisition, and scholars have debated the relationship between conception and judgment under the term 'ilm'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you treat 'knowing about something' (conception) as enough, or do you actively seek judgment (tasdiq) to confirm truth? Which one do you tend to neglect, and how does that affect your certainty?
?
The early scholars debated the nature of 'ilm. If the correct view is that judgment is not an act but a perception, how does that change your responsibility in verifying the truth of your beliefs? What will you do differently to ensure your judgments are genuine perceptions rather than mere acts?
?
Consider a core belief you hold about God or the unseen. Is it merely a conception (an idea you have heard) or a tasdiq that you have truly perceived as truth? If it falls short, what step can you take today to move from conception to assent?

Linguistic Argument and Resolution of Objection about Ibn Sina
The author discusses a linguistic argument and an objection by Ibn Sina, then resolves them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic argument about judgment as an act and how the objection regarding Ibn Sina's division is resolved.

Core Idea
Linguistic usage does not settle the metaphysical nature of judgment.

The author addresses a linguistic argument that since language calls judgment an 'act' and the judge an 'agent', judgment must be an act. The commentator refutes this by distinguishing language from reality. Then an objection is raised: the division of knowledge into conception and assent is attributed to Ibn Sina, but he only divided conceptual knowledge, not absolute knowledge. The answer clarifies that the correct division applies to absolute knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
This is the longest passage. Read it once to get the overall flow: linguistic argument, refutation, then objection and answer.
2
Analyze the linguistic argument and refutation
Identify the claim that language shows judgment is an act, and the commentator's response. Notice the distinction between grammatical forms and philosophical reality.
The commentator says language does not equate grammatical labels with metaphysical facts.
3
Examine the objection about Ibn Sina
The objection states that Ibn Sina divided knowledge into conception and assent, but he considered judgment a perception, not an act. Find the answer given.
The answer is that Ibn Sina divided conceptual knowledge, not absolute knowledge.

قوله : فالصواب إلخ) أي الصواب أن لا يجعل الحكم نفسه ولا المركب منه، ومن غيره قسماً من العلم وأما إطلاق التصديق على التصور المقارن للحكم حتى ينقسم العلم إلى تصور ساذج، وإلى التصديق أي تصور معه حكم كما يتبادر من عبارة متن الكتاب فجائز، لكن يخالف وصف التصديق بالبداهة والظنية وغيرها فإنها أوصاف للحكم لا للتصور المقارن له، إلا أن يتسامح فيوصف ذلك التصور بوصف عارض له، وإنه تعسف . قوله : ( إلى تصور ساذج إلخ) والمقصود من التقسيم ظهور ذلك العارض المنفرد من معروضه بكاسب مخصوص وقد جعل بعضهم لفظ العلم مشتركاً بين المعروض، وذلك العارض، وقسم العلم إليهما فكأنه قيل : ما يطلق عليه لفظ العلم، إما تصور وإما حكم، وهو التصديق وتكلف آخرون بجعل الاشتراك معنوياً فقالوا : كان الأوائل قسموا المعاني الذهنية إلى نفس الإدراك، وإلى ما يلحقه وقسموا ما يلحقه إلى ما يجعله محتملاً للصدق والكذب، وإلى ما لا يجعله كذلك كالهيئات اللاحقة به في الأمر والنهي، والاستفهام والتمني وغير ذلك، وسموا المشترك بين القسمين الأولين علماً هذا كله على أن الحكم فعل، والصواب خلافه كذا نقل عنه . قوله: (كما وقع إلخ) وفي بعض النسخ كما ورد أي تقسيماً مماثلاً لما ورد في الكتب قوله : (كما توهمه العبارات إلخ قال الشارح في حواشي المطالع : لا عبرة بإيهام تلك العبارات فإن أهل اللغة لا يفرقون بين القبول والفعل، ويسمون القابل اسم فاعل والمقبول اسم مفعول وفيه نظر، إذ ليس الكلام في لفظ الفعل، والانفعال بل في مثل الإسناد، والإيقاع، ولا شك أن أهل اللغة وضعوها بإزاء الفعل فلا يجوز استعمالها بطريق الحقيقة في الكيف، والانفعال إلا مجازاً وهذا كما أنهم وضعوا بإزاء الفعل نحو الكسر وبإزاء الانفعال نحو الانكسار، فلا تقريب لما ذكره نعم لو استدل على فعلية الحكم بأن أهل اللغة يطلقون عليه الفعل وعلى الحاكم الفاعل، وعلى المحكوم به المفعول به لكان فيما ذكره تقريب ظاهر . قوله : ( فالصواب أن يقسم العلم إلخ) فعلى هذا يلزم توقف التصديق على خمسة أشياء. قوله : ( كما ورد في بعض الكتب المعتبرة قيل عليه قاسم العلم إلى القسمين المذكورين بعض الكتب المعتبرة هو أبو علي بن سينا، كما نقله في شرح المطالع، والحكم عنده إدراك لا فعل، فما ذكره صلح لا عن تراضي الخصمين والجواب أن مراد الشارح أن الصواب حينئذ أن يقسم مطلق العلم إلى القسمين المذكورين والشيخ إنما قسم إليهما العلم التصوري لا مطلق العلم، كما صرح به الشارح في حواشيه على ذلك الشرح، فإن أراد ببعض الكتب المعتبرة غير كتاب الشيخ فالأمر ظاهر وإن أراد كتابه فالضمير في ورد راجع إلى تقسيم العلم بالمعنى ولعارضه المسمى بالحكم والتصديق طريق خاص آخر، وأما جعل التصديق قسماً من العلم مع تركيبه من الحكم، وغيره فلا وجه له فعلاً كان الحكم، أو إدراكاً
(His saying: 'The correct view, etc.') i.e., the correct view is that one should not make the judgment itself or the composite of it and something else a part of knowledge. As for applying 'tasdiq' to the conception accompanying judgment so that knowledge is divided into bare conception and into tasdiq—i.e., a conception with a judgment—as understood from the phrasing of the text of the book, it is permissible. However, it contradicts the description of tasdiq as self-evident, probable, etc., for these are descriptions of the judgment, not of the conception accompanying it, unless one is lenient and describes that conception by an accident accruing to it, and that is forced. His saying: (into bare conception, etc.) And the purpose of the division is the manifestation of that isolated accident from its subject by a specific acquisitive means. Some have made the word 'ilm' common between the subject and that accident, and divided knowledge into the two: as if it were said, 'What is called knowledge is either conception or judgment, which is tasdiq.' Others, by making the commonality conceptual, said: The early scholars divided mental meanings into pure perception and into what attaches to it, and they divided what attaches to it into that which makes it capable of truth and falsehood, and into that which does not make it so, such as the forms that attach to it in command, prohibition, interrogation, wish, etc. They called the common between the first two divisions 'ilm.' All this is on the view that judgment is an act, but the correct view is contrary to this, as it is transmitted from him. His saying: (as occurred, etc.) And in some copies 'as was reported,' i.e., a division similar to what was reported in the books. His saying: (as the expressions suggest, etc.) The commentator said in the glosses of al-Matali': 'No attention is paid to the suggestion of those expressions, for the people of language do not distinguish between acceptance and act; they call the acceptor a subject (ism fa'il) and the accepted an object (ism maf'ul).' There is a consideration, for the discussion is not about the word 'act' and 'passivity,' but about such things as predication, effectuation, etc. There is no doubt that the people of language have placed these expressions for the act, so it is not permissible to use them in a real sense for the quality and passivity except metaphorically. This is like how they placed for the act such as 'breaking' and for passivity such as 'being broken.' So there is no approximation in what he mentioned. Yes, if one argued for the actuality of judgment by saying that the people of language apply 'act' to it, 'agent' to the one judging, and 'object acted upon' to the thing judged, then there would be a clear approximation in what he mentioned. His saying: (So the correct view is to divide knowledge, etc.) According to this, tasdiq would depend on five things. His saying: (as reported in some authoritative books) It was said against him: 'The one who divided knowledge into the two mentioned divisions from some authoritative books is Abu Ali ibn Sina, as transmitted in Sharh al-Matali', and judgment according to him is perception, not an act. So what he mentioned is a reconciliation, not from the mutual satisfaction of the two opponents.' The answer is that the commentator intended that the correct view then is to divide absolute knowledge into the two mentioned divisions, while the Shaykh only divided conceptual knowledge, not absolute knowledge, as the commentator clarified in his glosses on that commentary. If he intended by 'some authoritative books' other than the Shaykh's book, the matter is clear; if he intended his book, then the pronoun in 'occurred' refers to the division of knowledge in the sense. And for its accident called judgment and tasdiq, there is another specific path. As for making tasdiq a part of knowledge while it is composed of judgment and something else, it has no justification, whether judgment is an act or perception.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is a commentary defending the correct division of knowledge into tasawwur (conception) and tasdiq (assent). It begins by stating that the correct view is not to make judgment (hukm) itself or a composite of judgment and conception a separate part of knowledge. The division into bare conception (tasawwur saadhij) and conception accompanied by judgment (tasdiq) is permissible, but there is a problem: descriptions like 'self-evident' or 'probable' apply to the judgment, not to the accompanying conception, unless one is lax. The passage then explains that the purpose of the division is to show that each type has its own acquisitive means. Some scholars made the term 'ilm' common between the subject (the conception) and the accident (the judgment), but others insisted on a conceptual commonality: the early scholars divided mental meanings into pure perception and its attachments, then subdivided attachments into those that allow truth/falsity and those that do not (e.g., imperative, interrogative forms). This was based on judgment being an act, but the correct view rejects that. Next, a linguistic argument is refuted: the commentator of al-Matali' notes that language does not equate grammatical labels with metaphysical reality; words like 'predication' (isnad) are placed for acts, not for qualities or passions. However, if one argues from language (calling judgment 'act' and the judge 'agent') it would be more plausible. Then the passage addresses an objection: the correct division leads to tasdiq depending on five things. It clarifies that Ibn Sina's division was for conceptual knowledge only, not absolute knowledge, so the objection is answered. Finally, it concludes that there is no justification for making tasdiq a separate part of knowledge, whether judgment is an act or a perception.

Key Terms
فعل (fi'l)
انفعال (infi'āl)
إسناد (isnād)
إيقاع (īqā')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Linguistic usage of 'act' and 'agent' proves judgment is an act.
Correction: The commentator shows that grammatical forms are used metaphorically; the real issue is conceptual, not linguistic.

Checkpoint
How is the objection that Ibn Sina divided knowledge into conception and assent while considering judgment a perception resolved?
A) It is shown that Ibn Sina was wrong
B) It is clarified that Ibn Sina divided conceptual knowledge only
C) The objection is ignored
D) It is argued that language supports the division

Key Takeaway
Linguistic arguments do not settle the nature of judgment, and the division of knowledge into conception and assent is consistent with Ibn Sina's teachings when applied to absolute knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the linguistic argument that judgment is called an 'act' in language, did you feel an immediate pull to accept it? What does that tell you about your tendency to rely on surface-level language rather than deeper reasoning? How will you train yourself to push past linguistic intuitions to grasp the underlying philosophical concepts?
?
The objection against Ibn Sina shows how easy it is to misunderstand a scholar's position by taking their words out of context. Have you ever misapplied or misquoted a scholar's statement in your own learning? What steps will you take to ensure you understand the full context and intent before using a statement to support a position?
?
The passage concludes that making tasdiq a part of knowledge has no justification, whether judgment is an act or perception. Does this unsettle your understanding of the relationship between knowledge, belief, and certitude? Are you willing to re-examine your own epistemology—how you claim to 'know' something—in light of this rigorous intellectual scrutiny?

What Is Kalam?
beginner
4 min
The Essential Distinction Between Conception and Assent
Defines tasawwur and tasdiq as two types distinct in essence and by truth/falsity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two ways in which tasawwur (conception) and tasdiq (assent) are distinguished: by essence and by the concomitant of truth/falsity.

Core Idea
Conception and assent differ both in their very nature and in whether they can be true or false.

The author begins by establishing that the two fundamental types of knowledge, conception and assent, are not merely different by convention but are distinct in their essence. He then provides an intuitive example to make this clear, and finally adds a well-known rule-of-thumb distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
First, read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the English translation to get the overall meaning.
Notice the parentheses in the Arabic—they indicate the original text of the matn.
2
Understand the example
The passage gives an example: when you doubt a relation between two things, you still have conception of those things and the relation. Then when you judge, you have assent. Reflect on how these two states feel different.
3
Grasp the essential distinction
The author says the two types are distinct 'by essence' (bi al-dhat), meaning their very quiddity (mahiyya) is different. The example illustrates this intuitive difference.
This is not just a matter of definition but a real difference we can experience.
4
Learn the concomitant distinction
The second distinction is by a well-known concomitant: the possibility of truth and falsehood. Assent can be true or false, while conception cannot. This is a practical way to tell them apart.
This is a 'lazim' (necessary property) that helps identify each type.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( وهما ) أي التصور والتصديق ( نوعان متمايزان بالذات ) أي بالماهية فإنك إذا تصورت نسبة أمر إلى آخر، وشككت فيها فقد علمت ذينك الأمرين، والنسبة بينهما قطعاً فلك في هذه الحالة نوع من العلم، ثم إذا زال عنك الشك، وحكمت بأحد طرفي النسبة فقد علمت تلك النسبة نوعاً آخر من العلم، ممتازاً عن الأول بحقيقته وجدانا ( وباعتبار اللازم المشهور وهو احتمال الصدق والكذب ) في التصديق ( وعدمه ) في التصور .
(And they two) i.e., conception and assent (are two types distinct in essence) i.e., in quiddity. For if you conceive a relation of one thing to another and doubt it, you have definitely known those two things and the relation between them, so in this state you have a type of knowledge. Then when the doubt leaves you and you judge one of the two sides of the relation, you have known that relation as another type of knowledge, distinguished from the first by its reality by direct intuition. (And by the consideration of the well-known concomitant, namely the possibility of truth and falsehood) in assent, (and its absence) in conception.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage defines the two types of knowledge—tasawwur (conception) and tasdiq (assent)—as distinct in essence (bi al-dhat) and by a well-known concomitant. It first explains the essential distinction: if you merely conceive a relation between two things while doubting it, you have a type of knowledge (tasawwur of the terms and relation). Then, when doubt is removed and you judge one side of the relation, you have another type of knowledge (tasdiq), which is intuitively different. Thus they differ in their very quiddity. The second distinction is by the necessary concomitant of possibility of truth and falsehood: tasdiq admits truth/falsity, while tasawwur does not. This is a standard kalam distinction.

Key Terms
تصور (tasawwur)
تصديق (tasdiq)
بالذات (bi al-dhat)
الماهية (al-mahiyya)
احتمال الصدق والكذب (ihtimal al-sidq wa al-kidhb)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the distinction between conception and assent is only by an external accident (like the accompanying judgment) rather than by essence.
Correction: The passage explicitly states they are distinct 'by essence' (bi al-dhat), meaning their very natures differ, not merely an added property. The example shows they are different types of knowledge in themselves.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is one way tasawwur and tasdiq are distinguished?
A) By their subject matter
B) By the possibility of truth and falsehood
C) By being based on revelation
D) By being certain or probable

Key Takeaway
Conception and assent are fundamentally different types of knowledge: they differ in their very essence and in whether they can be evaluated as true or false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the shahada, are you merely conceiving the words (tasawwur) or have you truly assented (tasdiq) to their meaning in a way that changes your actions? What evidence in your life would show the difference?
?
Think of a belief about Allah that you hold. Is your knowledge of it merely conceptual—you understand the words—or have you judged it to be true in your heart? How would you know the difference, and which do you want to strengthen?
?
The passage says assent can be false. Which belief do you hold most dear, and are you willing to test whether it is true or false using the criteria of sound reason and revelation? What would it cost you if you discovered it was only a conception you assumed was true?
