المرصد الثالث - المقصد الثالث : التصور والتصديق بعضه ضروري بالوجدان. أن يكون شيء من التصور والتصديق حاصلاً لنا، وهو باطل قطعاً. لا يقال: إذ فرض الكل نظري فهذا الذي ذكرته من لزوم الدور أو التسلسل وكونهما مانعين من الاكتساب، ومفضيين إلى أن لا يكون شيء من الإدراكات حاصلاً لنا أيضاً نظري على ذلك التقدير، وحينئذ يمتنع إثباته لأن إثباته إنما يكون بنظري آخر، فيلزم الدور أو التسلسل لما ذكرتم بعينه. والحاصل أن دليلكم على بطلان كون الكل نظرياً ليس يتم بجميع مقدماته، لأن كونه تاماً كذلك يستلزم المحال المذكور. ولأنا نقول: ما ذكرناه في دليلنا من التصورات والتصديقات نظري وغير معلوم على ذلك التقدير لا في نفس الأمر، بل هو معلوم لنا في نفس الأمر، فيبطل ذلك التقدير لاستلزامه خلاف الواقع، أعني كون تلك القضايا معلومة في نفس الأمر. والحق أن هذا الدليل الذي ذكرناه حجة قائمة على من اعترف بالمعلومات، أي اعترف بأن تلك القضايا المذكورة في الدليل معلومة في نفس الأمر، وزعم أنها كسبية على ذلك التقدير ولا تكون معلومة عليه، فكيف يجوز التمسك بها في إبطال ذلك التقدير؟ قوله : ( إذ حينئذ يجاب إلخ ) دليل على كونه حجة قائمة على من اعترف قوله : ( فلا) كلام في أنه يجوز التمسك بها . قوله : ( كان ذلك التقدير إلخ إذ الأمور الواقعة في نفس الأمر متجامعة . قوله : ( بأن لنا معلومات إلخ) فاللام في قوله بالمعلومات للجنس . قوله : (لأنا إذا أثبتنا إلخ) هذه الشرطية صادقة، وإن لم يكن مقدمها صادقاً فإن صدق الشرطية لا يتوقف على صدق طرفيها، فما قيل : لعل المعترف بمطلق المعلوم ينكر معلومية هذه القضايا التي استدللنا بها فلا يقوم حجة عليه حينئذ، فلا وجه لحمل كلام المصنف على هذا القول ليس بشيء، لأن ورود المنع على صدق المقدم لا يدفع ورود هذا الاعتراض على تقدير صدقه على الدليل المذكور. قوله : ( بالضرورة الوجدانية ) يعني أن مقابلة الضرورة بالوجدان وإن كان ظاهر الدلالة على أن يكون المراد بها غير الوجدان بناء على أن العام إذا قوبل بالخاص يراد به ما عدا الخاص، إلا أن مقصوده أن الضرورة هي الوجدانية، وفي اختلاف العبارة نكتة.
The third observation - the third purpose: Conception and assent, some of it is necessary by intuition. That something of conception and assent should be obtained by us is absolutely false. It may not be said: if all is theoretical, then this that you mentioned of the necessity of circularity or infinite regress and their being preventers of acquisition, and leading to that nothing of the perceptions is obtained by us, is also theoretical on that assumption, and then its proof is impossible because its proof would only be by another theoretical, so circularity or infinite regress follows as you mentioned. And the conclusion is that your proof of the invalidity of all being theoretical is not complete in all its premises, because its being complete would entail the mentioned absurdity. But we say: what we mentioned in our proof of the conceptions and assents is theoretical and unknown on that assumption not in reality, but it is known to us in reality, so that assumption is invalidated because it necessitates contradiction of reality, i.e., those propositions being known in reality. And the truth is that this proof we mentioned is an argument valid against whoever acknowledges information, i.e., acknowledges that those mentioned propositions in the proof are known in reality, and claims they are acquired on that assumption and are not known on it, so how is it permissible to use them in invalidating that assumption? His statement: 'then it is answered etc.' is evidence that it is an argument valid against whoever acknowledges. His statement: 'No' is speech about the permissibility of using it. His statement: 'that assumption etc.' because matters occurring in reality are compatible. His statement: 'that we have information etc.' the lām in 'information' is for the genus. His statement: 'for if we prove etc.' this conditional is true, even if its antecedent is not true, for the truth of the conditional does not depend on the truth of its two sides. So what is said: perhaps the one who acknowledges absolute information denies the known-ness of these propositions which we used as evidence, so it is not an argument against him then, so there is no basis for interpreting the author's words in this sense is nothing, because the occurrence of denial of the truth of the premise does not repel the occurrence of this objection on the assumption of its truth regarding the mentioned proof. His statement: 'by necessary intuition' means that opposing necessity to intuition, although its apparent indication is that what is meant by it is other than intuition based on the principle that when the general is opposed to the specific, what is meant is other than the specific, except that his intent is that necessity is intuitive, and in the difference of expression there is a nuance.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is a complex argumentative proof and subsequent discussion. It begins by asserting the thesis: some conception and assent are necessary by intuition. The proof is that if all were theoretical (i.e., acquired through reasoning), then we would never have any knowledge because acquiring theoretical knowledge requires prior concepts and assents, leading to either circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul). The objector claims that this proof itself is theoretical and therefore cannot be established without the same problems. The author replies that the premises of the proof—the concepts and assents used—are not merely assumed to be known on the opponent's hypothesis; rather, they are actually known in reality. Thus, the opponent's hypothesis (that all is theoretical) is false because it contradicts this actual knowledge. The author then clarifies that this proof only stands against one who admits that there are known things (ma'lumat) in reality but claims they are all acquired. If the opponent denies that these specific propositions are known, then the proof does not apply. The commentary then elaborates on the wording, explaining that the conditional argument is valid regardless of the truth of its antecedent, and that the opponent's potential denial of the known-ness of the premises does not undermine the proof, because the proof assumes their actual known-ness. Finally, it addresses a nuance in the phrasing: the author says 'some of it is necessary by intuition, and some of it is theoretical by necessity'—this contrasting wording might suggest that 'theoretical by necessity' is not intuitive, but the intent is that the necessity in both cases is the same intuitive necessity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the proof is self-defeating and therefore invalid.
Correction: The proof is presented as valid against those who accept that there is knowledge in reality, and the objection will be answered in the next lesson.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what would happen if all conception and assent were theoretical (nazari)?
A) We would have infinite knowledge.
B) Circularity or infinite regress would prevent any knowledge from being acquired.
C) Everything would be known by intuition.
D) There would be no need for proof.

Key Takeaway
Some knowledge must be intuitive to avoid the impossibility of acquiring any knowledge through reasoning alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of an area of your faith where you rely solely on arguments and proofs. Which of your beliefs are you most afraid to examine because they might fail under logical scrutiny? Are you willing to let your faith rest on intuitive certainty rather than just rational demonstration?
?
The objector claims that the proof itself is theoretical and thus self-defeating. In your own spiritual life, where do you hide behind intellectual objections rather than admitting that some truths are simply intuitive and undeniable? What would it cost you to accept the intuitive foundation of your belief?
?
If you had to identify one truth about Allah that you know with intuitive certainty (daruri) rather than through reasoning, what would it be? And is your daily life governed by that certainty or by doubts you entertain?

What Is Kalam?
intermediate
7 min
Defending the Proof: Real Knowledge Defeats the Objection
Sanusi replies to the objection and clarifies the scope of his proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi responds to the objection that his own proof is theoretical and therefore self-defeating.

Core Idea
The proof's premises are known in reality, not just assumed, so the objection collapses.

The objector claimed that Sanusi's proof itself is theoretical and thus cannot be established without the same problems. In this lesson, we examine Sanusi's response: the conceptions and assents used in the proof are actually known in reality (fi nafs al-amr), not merely assumed on the opponent's hypothesis. Therefore, the opponent's hypothesis (that all is theoretical) is invalidated because it contradicts this actual knowledge. We will also see how the proof only applies to those who admit that there is known information (ma'lumat) in reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the response
Read Sanusi's reply: he says the premises are known in reality, not on the opponent's assumption.
Note the phrase 'in reality' (fi nafs al-amr) as opposed to 'on that assumption'.
2
Understand the refutation of the objection
The opponent's assumption leads to a contradiction with reality, because those propositions are actually known.
The assumption is invalidated because it contradicts the fact that those propositions are known in reality.
3
See the limitation of the proof
Sanusi clarifies that the proof stands only against one who acknowledges that there are known things in reality but claims they are all acquired.
If someone denies the known-ness of the premises, the proof does not apply.
4
Examine the nuance about necessity
The final comments discuss the wording and the conditional nature of the argument, explaining that the proof's conditional statement is true regardless of the truth of its components.
The term 'necessary intuition' indicates that the necessity is ultimately the same intuitive necessity.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the proof is universally valid against all opponents.
Correction: The proof only works against those who admit that there is known information in reality but claim it is all acquired.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, against whom is his proof valid?
A) Against anyone who denies all knowledge.
B) Against anyone who acknowledges that there is known information in reality but claims it is all acquired.
C) Against anyone who accepts his proof as correct.
D) Against those who reject intuition.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's proof is not self-defeating because its premises are known in reality, and it refutes those who admit known things but claim they are all acquired.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a difficult truth claim about your faith, do you ever catch yourself assuming that it could not be known with certainty? How does Sanusi's insistence that the premises are known in reality challenge you to reflect on your own level of certainty about the foundations of your beliefs? Will you take a specific step this week to examine whether you truly know what you claim to believe?
?
The opponent's assumption that all knowledge is acquired (kasbi) led him to deny the reality of known truths. Is there an area of your life where you have accepted a comfortable assumption without checking whether it matches reality—perhaps about your own character, your relationship with Allah, or the teachings of Islam? What would it cost you to test that assumption against the truth?
?
Sanusi's argument hinges on the fact that the premises of the proof are known 'in reality' (fi nafs al-amr). Do you live as though the truths of Islam are realities independent of your feelings or experiences, or do you treat them as theoretical until they become convenient? When you next face doubt, will you anchor yourself in the objective reality of what you know, or will you let the doubt define your position?

What Is Kalam?

The Two Kinds of Knowledge: Necessary and Theoretical
The author states the division and provides a proof from the impossibility of all being acquired.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that conception and assent are partly necessary by intuition and partly theoretical by necessity, and that the impossibility of all knowledge being acquired proves that some knowledge must be necessary.

Core Idea
Knowledge is not all acquired; some must be necessary by intuition.

The author begins by stating a fundamental division: conception and assent are either necessary (by intuition) or theoretical (by necessity). This lesson explores that division and the logical proof that not all knowledge can be acquired, which necessitates the existence of necessary knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening statement
Read the Arabic passage stating that some conception and assent are necessary by intuition, and some are theoretical by necessity. Notice the two categories.
The terms 'ضروري' and 'نظري' are key.
2
Understand the proof
The passage argues that if all knowledge were acquired, it would lead to circularity or infinite regress. Follow the logical reasoning that the impossibility of all being acquired forces some knowledge to be necessary.
The proof targets those who admit knowledge but claim it's all acquired.
3
Reflect on intuitive necessity
The passage concludes that even theoretical knowledge ultimately relies on intuitive necessity. Consider how this unifies the two categories.
This will be expanded in the next lesson.

The Third Observation - The Third Purpose: Conception and assent, some of it is necessary by intuition. Its refutation? Then it is answered that reasoning by them depends on knowledge of their truth, and they occur in reality; if that estimation gathers them, then no objection, and if it does not gather them, then that estimation does not occur in actual fact, and that is the desired (no) for one who denies them absolutely, i.e., denies the knowledge of those propositions on that estimation and also in actual fact. For this proof does not stand against him definitively, because whatever is brought forth to establish the knowledge of the truth of its premises is met by him with a denial of that knowledge, since no necessary [knowledge] that cannot be denied has yet been established. And it may be said: He meant that what we mentioned regarding the impossibility of all being acquired only stands as a proof against one who acknowledges that we have conceptual and assertoric bits of knowledge, but that they are entirely acquired. For if we then establish that the whole of each of them is not acquired, it follows that some of each must be necessary. As for one who denies all bits of knowledge and does not acknowledge any of them, he may say: The impossibility of all being acquired does not entail the necessity of some, because it is possible that no knowledge is obtained. His statement: (For if we establish, etc.) this conditional is true even if its antecedent is not true, for the truth of a conditional does not depend on the truth of its two parts. Commentary
This passage is a combination of the original matn and the sharh commentary on the third purpose of the third observation. It defines and proves that some conception and assent are necessary by intuition and some are theoretical by necessity. The author begins by stating the topic: conception and assent are divided into necessary (by intuition) and theoretical (by necessity). Then the commentary addresses an objection: what if someone denies all knowledge? It explains that the proof from the impossibility of all being acquired only works against those who admit some knowledge. For the absolute denier, the proof fails because he can deny the premises. The commentary then clarifies that the author intended the proof for those who admit knowledge but claim it's all acquired. It also explains the phrase 'by intuitive necessity' to distinguish two types of necessity: immediate and mediate, both falling under intuition. The passage ends with a note on the stylistic nuance of the author's wording.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of all knowledge being acquired proves that some knowledge must be necessary by intuition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

What Is Kalam?
intermediate
4 min
Addressing the Absolute Denier and the Meaning of Necessity
The commentary addresses the absolute denier and explains that 'necessity' in both categories is intuitive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the proof of necessary knowledge fails against someone who denies all knowledge, and why even theoretical knowledge is ultimately necessary by intuitive necessity.

Core Idea
The proof only works for those who admit some knowledge; and all necessity traces back to intuition.

The previous lesson established that some knowledge must be necessary. However, this proof presupposes that the opponent acknowledges some knowledge. This lesson examines the case of the absolute denier and clarifies that even knowledge labeled 'theoretical' is still necessary in the sense of intuitive necessity.

Guided Path
1
Review the objection
Read the passage where the commentary considers someone who denies all knowledge. Note that the proof from impossibility of all being acquired does not apply to such a person.
This shows the limits of the argument.
2
Understand the counter-argument
The denier can say: 'Even if all knowledge cannot be acquired, it may be that no knowledge exists at all.' Recognize that this blocks the conclusion that some knowledge is necessary.
This is a radical skeptical position.
3
Grasp the resolution
The author clarifies that 'theoretical by necessity' does not mean non-intuitive; rather, it means that the necessity is mediated by reasoning but still ultimately founded on intuition. Both categories fall under intuitive necessity.
This unifies the division.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that 'theoretical by necessity' means it is not based on intuition, but on pure reasoning independent of self-evidence.

What Is Kalam?

Al-Razi's First Argument: Tasawwur Cannot Be Sought
Al-Razi's first argument and the refutation that the dichotomy is incomplete.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's first argument against the acquisition of conception and the key refutation that a thing can be known from one aspect and unknown from another.

Core Idea
Al-Razi's dichotomy fails: something can be known in part yet unknown in essence.

Imam al-Razi presents his first reason why conception (tasawwur) cannot be acquired: any desired conception is either already fully in the mind or completely unknown—both cases render seeking it impossible. The refutation, however, points out that this dichotomy ignores the possibility of partial knowledge through attributes. Let us examine the argument and its rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice how al-Razi sets up his dichotomy.
2
Identify the two parts of the dichotomy
Find the two options: the desired conception is either absolutely perceived or not perceived at all. Understand why each is claimed to make seeking impossible.
The first option leads to 'tahsil al-hasil' (obtaining what is already obtained), which is impossible.
3
Understand the refutation
The refutation rejects the dichotomy by introducing a third possibility: known from one aspect but unknown from another. Reflect on how this undermines al-Razi's argument.
This middle case is the key to the refutation.

قال في شرح المقاصد: هذا تحقيق لما هو المهم أعني إمكان اكتساب التصور بحسب الحقيقة وتنبيه على أن مجهولية الذات لازمة فيما يطلب تصوره حتى لو علم الشيء بحقيقته وقصد اكتساب بعض العوارض له كان ذلك بالدليل لا بالتعريف وفي المنبه عليه بحث ظاهر إذ اكتساب بعض العوارض للشيء بعد معرفة حقيقته، قد يكون من حيث إنه آلة لملاحظته ومرآة يتعرف حاله به، فيكون المطلوب التصور دون التصديق وكون التصور بالعارض أنقص من التصور بالكنية لا ينافي كون الأول مطلوباً قد قد يتعلق به الغرض دون الثاني والأولى أن تعيين جهة المجهولية للذات لكونها أغلب وأنسب لمن هو بصدد معرفة حقائق الأشياء، فلذا حمل الشارح الذات في عبارة المصنف على الحقيقة ولم يحمله على ذات المطلوب حتى يشمل أنواع التعريفات كما سيأتي مثله على أن فيه تنبيها على التوجيهين.
The third observation - the fourth objective: the weak schools of thought in this issue. (The second school of thought: in this issue (that conception is not acquired) through consideration, rather everything that is obtained from it is necessary, obtained without acquisition and consideration, unlike assent (tasdiq), for it is divided into necessary and acquired. (And al-Imam al-Razi held this view) and chose it in his books. (For two reasons: one is that the conceptual desired (al-matlab al-tasawwuri) is either absolutely perceived, so it is not sought (because obtaining what is already obtained is necessarily impossible), or it is not perceived at all, so it is also not sought because that which is completely neglected, which is called the absolutely unknown (al-majhul al-mutlaq), cannot have the soul direct itself (by seeking) towards it by necessity as well. (And it was answered) about this reason (that the dichotomy), i.e., dividing the conceptual desired into what is perceived from all aspects or not perceived at all, (is rejected because it is possible that it is known and perceived from one aspect but not from another, and he has not shown that this division is impossible to seek. (So the Imam returned) and said: the known aspect is absolutely known, and the unknown aspect is absolutely unknown, so nothing from them can be sought) due to what has passed of the impossibility of obtaining what is obtained and the impossibility of the soul directing itself towards that which is completely neglected. (And the answer) to this reason, after completing the three divisions, is to say: (we do not concede that the unknown aspect is absolutely unknown, i.e., in all aspects), for the absolutely unknown is that whose essence has not been conceived (in its reality). His saying: (unlike assent etc.) because that to which assent pertains, namely the relation, is one thing known in conception, unknown in assent, so the aforementioned doubt does not apply to it, and likewise the second, and that is apparent. His saying: (the conceptual desired) summarized: if the conceptual desired were acquired, its seeking would not be impossible, and the consequent is false. As for the implication, it is apparent; as for the falsity of the consequent, it is because the conceptual desired is either perceived or not perceived, and both are impossible to seek, so the conceptual desired is impossible to seek. And by what we have written is rebutted what was said: why is it not permissible that something of it be obtained by way of arranging things to see whether it leads to something or not? And then it happens that it leads to a specific conception. His saying: (rather everything that is obtained from it etc.) It was said: why is it not permissible that something from it be obtained? By way of arranging things to see whether it leads to something or not, and then it happens that it leads to a specific conception. Yes, it is possible to impose a necessary consequence on one who speaks of seeking. His saying: (the conceptual desired is either perceived etc.) It was objected against it: it is refuted by the acquisition of assent while the argument applies to it. It was answered: that to which assent pertains, like the proposition or the relation, is known in terms of conception, so directing oneself towards it is not impossible, though it is unknown in terms of assent, so seeking its attainment is not impossible. This is contrary to conception, for that which is unknown in terms of conception is absolutely unknown since there is no knowledge prior to conception. The summary is that the object of assent can have knowledge before assent, which is conception, unlike the object of conception. The third observation - the fourth objective: the weak schools of thought in this issue. (And nothing of what is true of it from its essential or accidental attributes) (and this unknown aspect is not like that, rather (something may be conceived) of what (is true of it, and that is the known aspect. For the (unknown) aspect, hypothetically, (is the essence and reality whose conception is sought in its reality) and the (known) aspect (is some of the established considerations that are true of it, whether essential to it or accidental to it) (just as the spirit is known) for example (as something by which life, sensation, and motion exist, and that it has a reality) also a conception. His saying: (in its reality) he estimated this word to make it correct to contrast it with his saying: and nothing of what is true of it, for this is for the essence, except that what is meant is 'and nothing of what is true of it' inasmuch as it is true of it. The evidence for this estimation is what has been established that when the general is contrasted with the particular, what is meant is other than the particular. And in some manuscripts it is 'nor with anything of what is true of it', so it is an estimation of the conjoined. His saying: (for the unknown aspect, hypothetically, is the essence etc.) He indicated by his word 'hypothetically' that considering the unknownness of the essence is by way of example, paying attention to what is most important, namely the acquisition of conception according to reality. And in Sharh al-Maqasid: the unknownness of the essence is necessary in what one seeks to conceive, so that even if a thing is known in its reality and one intends to acquire some of its accidents, that would be by proof, not by definition. End. And this is based on the fact that conceiving a thing in its reality is the thing obtaining itself, so its obtaining cannot be sought by virtue of an accidental matter obtaining for it, for when a thing is present, it is not sought by something else that serves as a tool for its presence. So what is sought is only the establishment of that accident for it and its being an aspect of its aspects, so it belongs to assent. His saying: (in its reality and nothing of what is true of it) which occurs in some manuscripts, 'nor with anything' with the preposition ba', and therefore the commentator estimated the word 'in its reality' to specify what is conjoined to his saying 'nor with anything'. And in some manuscripts, 'nor anything' with nominative as a conjunction of 'essence', so it appears to indicate that there is no difference between knowledge of the aspect and knowledge of the thing from that aspect, because he made the conception of the aspect that is true of the thing contradictory to its absolute unknownness, but what contradicts it is only its conception even from one aspect. The interpretation is that he means 'something of what is true of it' inasmuch as it is true of it, so the approximation is complete. His saying: (for the unknown aspect, hypothetically, is the essence and reality. It was said in Sharh al-Maqasid: this is a verification of what is important, namely the possibility of acquiring conception according to reality, and a reminder that the unknownness of the essence is necessary in what one seeks to conceive, so that even if a thing is known in its reality and one intends to acquire some of its accidents, that would be by proof, not by definition. And concerning what is pointed out, there is an apparent investigation, for acquiring some accidents of a thing after knowing its reality may be in terms of it being a tool for observing it and a mirror by which its state is known, and thus what is sought is conception, not assent. And the fact that conception by accident is inferior to conception by reality does not negate that the former is sought; sometimes the purpose attaches to it without the latter. And it is better to specify the direction of unknownness for the essence because it is more common and more appropriate for one who is in the position of knowing the realities of things. Therefore, the commentator interpreted the 'essence' in the author's expression as 'reality' and did not interpret it as the essence of the desired object, so that it includes the types of definitions, as will come later similarly, and there is also a reminder of the two directions.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage presents the second opinion on whether conception (tasawwur) is acquired (muktasab) through consideration (nazar). It states the view of Imam al-Razi and his two arguments, followed by their refutation.

The passage begins by stating the second madhhab: that conception is not acquired; rather, everything that is obtained from it is necessary (daruri), unlike assent (tasdiq), which divides into necessary and acquired. Al-Razi holds this view and gives two reasons.

**The first argument:** The conceptual desired (al-matlab al-tasawwuri) is either absolutely perceived (mash'ur bihi mutlaqan) or not perceived at all. If it is absolutely perceived, it cannot be sought because obtaining what is already obtained (tahsil al-hasil) is impossible. If it is not perceived at all, it also cannot be sought because the completely neglected (al-maghful 'anhu bilkulliyya), called the absolutely unknown (al-majhul al-mutlaq), cannot have the soul direct itself towards it. Thus, seeking conception is impossible.

**The answer to the first argument:** The dichotomy is rejected because it is possible for something to be known and perceived from one aspect but not from another. The arguer has not proven that this middle case is impossible to seek.

**The second argument (al-Razi returns):** Al-Razi responds that the known aspect is absolutely known, and the unknown aspect is absolutely unknown, so nothing from either can be sought. The known aspect is already obtained, and the unknown aspect is completely unknown.

**The answer to the second argument:** After completing the three divisions (absolutely known, absolutely unknown, and known from one aspect), we say: we do not concede that the unknown aspect is absolutely unknown in all aspects. The absolutely unknown is that whose essence (dhat) has not been conceived in its reality (bi-kunhihi). But here, the unknown aspect is the essence itself, while the known aspect consists of some of its essential (dhati) or accidental ('aradi) attributes that are true of it, such as our knowledge of the spirit as something that has life, sensation, and motion. So there is a known aspect, and therefore seeking the unknown essence via its known attributes is possible.

The commentary then explains various points, including a comparison with assent (tasdiq): the object of assent (the relation or proposition) is known in conception but unknown in assent, so the soul can direct itself towards it. This is unlike conception, where if something is unknown in conception, it is absolutely unknown since there is no prior conception. The commentary also discusses textual variants and the interpretation of 'essence' as 'reality' in the definition of the absolutely unknown, emphasizing that the acquisition of conception is possible through the knowledge of aspects (wujuh) even if the essence itself remains unknown in its reality.

Key Terms
مشعور به (mash'ur bihi)
مجهول مطلق (majhul mutlaq)
تحصيل الحاصل (tahsil al-hasil)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if something is partially known, it cannot be sought because it is already known.
Correction: Partial knowledge does not exhaust the object; what is known (e.g., attributes) can serve as a basis to seek the unknown essence.

Checkpoint
What is the key flaw in al-Razi's first argument according to the refutation?
A) It ignores the possibility of a thing being known from one aspect and unknown from another.
B) It assumes that tasawwur is necessary, which is false.
C) It confuses tasawwur with tasdiq.
D) It relies on an invalid analogy with assent.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's first argument is refuted by recognizing that partial knowledge through attributes makes it possible to seek further knowledge of the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you seek to know Allah, do you rely on describing His attributes (like Ar-Rahman, Al-Hakeem) without striving to understand His essence? What would it cost you to admit that you don't truly know Him, and to pursue that knowledge seriously today?
?
Think of an Islamic concept (e.g., tawheed, taqwa) that you think you understand. In what ways might your understanding be only through 'attributes'—general descriptions—while the essence remains unknown? What specific step will you take this week to move from partial knowledge to a deeper, lived conception?

What Is Kalam?
intermediate
7 min
Al-Razi's Second Argument and Its Refutation: The Known and Unknown Aspects
Al-Razi's second argument and the refutation distinguishing essence from attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's second argument that each aspect is either absolutely known or absolutely unknown, and its refutation that the unknown aspect is not absolutely unknown because we know it through its attributes.

Core Idea
What is unknown in itself can be approached through its known attributes.

Al-Razi counters the previous refutation by insisting that each aspect is either fully known or fully unknown. The refutation, however, carefully distinguishes between the essence (unknown in itself) and the attributes (known), showing that the absolutely unknown is only that whose essence has never been conceived at all. But for things known by their attributes, the essence can still be sought through those attributes. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the second argument and refutation
Read the passage once to grasp the flow: al-Razi's argument, then the answer that introduces three divisions and clarifies the meaning of 'absolutely unknown'.
2
Note the definition of 'absolutely unknown'
Identify that the absolutely unknown is defined as that whose essence (dhat) has not been conceived in its reality (bi-kunhihi). This is a key distinction.
This definition is used to show that the unknown aspect in question is not absolutely unknown.
3
Understand how knowledge of attributes allows seeking the essence
The refutation explains that the unknown aspect is the essence itself, while the known aspect consists of attributes that are true of it. The example of the spirit (ruh) illustrates that we know something through its attributes (life, sensation, motion) even if we do not know its reality in depth.
This shows that seeking the essence through its known attributes is possible.

The third observation - the fourth objective: the weak schools of thought in this issue. (The second school of thought: in this issue (that conception is not acquired) through consideration, rather everything that is obtained from it is necessary, obtained without acquisition and consideration, unlike assent (tasdiq), for it is divided into necessary and acquired. (And the answer) to this reason, after completing the three divisions, is to say: (we do not concede that the unknown aspect is absolutely unknown, i.e., in all aspects), for the absolutely unknown is that whose essence has not been conceived (in its reality). His saying: (unlike assent etc.) because that to which assent pertains, namely the relation, is one thing known in conception, unknown in assent, so the aforementioned doubt does not apply to it, and likewise the second, and that is apparent. His saying: (the conceptual desired) summarized: if the conceptual desired were acquired, its seeking would not be impossible, and the consequent is false. As for the implication, it is apparent; as for the falsity of the consequent, it is because the conceptual desired is either perceived or not perceived, and both are impossible to seek, so the conceptual desired is impossible to seek. And by what we have written is rebutted what was said: why is it not permissible that something of it be obtained by way of arranging things to see whether it leads to something or not? And then it happens that it leads to a specific conception. His saying: (rather everything that is obtained from it etc.) It was said: why is it not permissible that something from it be obtained? By way of arranging things to see whether it leads to something or not, and then it happens that it leads to a specific conception. Yes, it is possible to impose a necessary consequence on one who speaks of seeking. His saying: (the conceptual desired is either perceived etc.) It was objected against it: it is refuted by the acquisition of assent while the argument applies to it. It was answered: that to which assent pertains, like the proposition or the relation, is known in terms of conception, so directing oneself towards it is not impossible, though it is unknown in terms of assent, so seeking its attainment is not impossible. This is contrary to conception, for that which is unknown in terms of conception is absolutely unknown since there is no knowledge prior to conception. The summary is that the object of assent can have knowledge before assent, which is conception, unlike the object of conception. The third observation - the fourth objective: the weak schools of thought in this issue. (And nothing of what is true of it from its essential or accidental attributes) (and this unknown aspect is not like that, rather (something may be conceived) of what (is true of it, and that is the known aspect. For the (unknown) aspect, hypothetically, (is the essence and reality whose conception is sought in its reality) and the (known) aspect (is some of the established considerations that are true of it, whether essential to it or accidental to it) (just as the spirit is known) for example (as something by which life, sensation, and motion exist, and that it has a reality) also a conception. His saying: (in its reality) he estimated this word to make it correct to contrast it with his saying: and nothing of what is true of it, for this is for the essence, except that what is meant is 'and nothing of what is true of it' inasmuch as it is true of it. The evidence for this estimation is what has been established that when the general is contrasted with the particular, what is meant is other than the particular. And in some manuscripts it is 'nor with anything of what is true of it', so it is an estimation of the conjoined. His saying: (for the unknown aspect, hypothetically, is the essence etc.) He indicated by his word 'hypothetically' that considering the unknownness of the essence is by way of example, paying attention to what is most important, namely the acquisition of conception according to reality. And in Sharh al-Maqasid: the unknownness of the essence is necessary in what one seeks to conceive, so that even if a thing is known in its reality and one intends to acquire some of its accidents, that would be by proof, not by definition. End. And this is based on the fact that conceiving a thing in its reality is the thing obtaining itself, so its obtaining cannot be sought by virtue of an accidental matter obtaining for it, for when a thing is present, it is not sought by something else that serves as a tool for its presence. So what is sought is only the establishment of that accident for it and its being an aspect of its aspects, so it belongs to assent. His saying: (in its reality and nothing of what is true of it) which occurs in some manuscripts, 'nor with anything' with the preposition ba', and therefore the commentator estimated the word 'in its reality' to specify what is conjoined to his saying 'nor with anything'. And in some manuscripts, 'nor anything' with nominative as a conjunction of 'essence', so it appears to indicate that there is no difference between knowledge of the aspect and knowledge of the thing from that aspect, because he made the conception of the aspect that is true of the thing contradictory to its absolute unknownness, but what contradicts it is only its conception even from one aspect. The interpretation is that he means 'something of what is true of it' inasmuch as it is true of it, so the approximation is complete. His saying: (for the unknown aspect, hypothetically, is the essence and reality. It was said in Sharh al-Maqasid: this is a verification of what is important, namely the possibility of acquiring conception according to reality, and a reminder that the unknownness of the essence is necessary in what one seeks to conceive, so that even if a thing is known in its reality and one intends to acquire some of its accidents, that would be by proof, not by definition. And concerning what is pointed out, there is an apparent investigation, for acquiring some accidents of a thing after knowing its reality may be in terms of it being a tool for observing it and a mirror by which its state is known, and thus what is sought is conception, not assent. And the fact that conception by accident is inferior to conception by reality does not negate that the former is sought; sometimes the purpose attaches to it without the latter. And it is better to specify the direction of unknownness for the essence because it is more common and more appropriate for one who is in the position of knowing the realities of things. Therefore, the commentator interpreted the 'essence' in the author's expression as 'reality' and did not interpret it as the essence of the desired object, so that it includes the types of definitions, as will come later similarly, and there is also a reminder of the two directions.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage presents the second opinion on whether conception (tasawwur) is acquired (muktasab) through consideration (nazar). It states the view of Imam al-Razi and his two arguments, followed by their refutation.

The passage begins by stating the second madhhab: that conception is not acquired; rather, everything that is obtained from it is necessary (daruri), unlike assent (tasdiq), which divides into necessary and acquired. Al-Razi holds this view and gives two reasons.

**The first argument:** The conceptual desired (al-matlab al-tasawwuri) is either absolutely perceived (mash'ur bihi mutlaqan) or not perceived at all. If it is absolutely perceived, it cannot be sought because obtaining what is already obtained (tahsil al-hasil) is impossible. If it is not perceived at all, it also cannot be sought because the completely neglected (al-maghful 'anhu bilkulliyya), called the absolutely unknown (al-majhul al-mutlaq), cannot have the soul direct itself towards it. Thus, seeking conception is impossible.

**The answer to the first argument:** The dichotomy is rejected because it is possible for something to be known and perceived from one aspect but not from another. The arguer has not proven that this middle case is impossible to seek.

**The second argument (al-Razi returns):** Al-Razi responds that the known aspect is absolutely known, and the unknown aspect is absolutely unknown, so nothing from either can be sought. The known aspect is already obtained, and the unknown aspect is completely unknown.

**The answer to the second argument:** After completing the three divisions (absolutely known, absolutely unknown, and known from one aspect), we say: we do not concede that the unknown aspect is absolutely unknown in all aspects. The absolutely unknown is that whose essence (dhat) has not been conceived in its reality (bi-kunhihi). But here, the unknown aspect is the essence itself, while the known aspect consists of some of its essential (dhati) or accidental ('aradi) attributes that are true of it, such as our knowledge of the spirit as something that has life, sensation, and motion. So there is a known aspect, and therefore seeking the unknown essence via its known attributes is possible.

The commentary then explains various points, including a comparison with assent (tasdiq): the object of assent (the relation or proposition) is known in conception but unknown in assent, so the soul can direct itself towards it. This is unlike conception, where if something is unknown in conception, it is absolutely unknown since there is no prior conception. The commentary also discusses textual variants and the interpretation of 'essence' as 'reality' in the definition of the absolutely unknown, emphasizing that the acquisition of conception is possible through the knowledge of aspects (wujuh) even if the essence itself remains unknown in its reality.

Key Terms
ذات (dhat)
حقيقة (haqiqa)
عرض (arad)
بكنهه (bi-kunhihi)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if something is unknown in its essence, it is completely unknown and cannot be sought.
Correction: Even if the essence is unknown, we can know it through its attributes, allowing us to seek deeper understanding.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, what does 'absolutely unknown' (مجهول مطلق) mean?
A) Something that is not perceived at all.
B) Something whose essence has not been conceived in its reality.
C) Something that has no attributes.
D) Something that cannot be known by any means.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's second argument is refuted by showing that the unknown aspect is not absolutely unknown; we know it through its attributes, which makes seeking its essence possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there an area of your faith—like the reality of the afterlife or the nature of your own soul—that you have treated as 'absolutely unknown' and therefore never thought to explore? What known attributes or effects might guide you to a deeper understanding, and will you commit to seeking it this week?
?
When you encounter a concept in your religion that you cannot fully grasp, do you tend to dismiss it as inaccessible, or do you search for what you can know about it through its signs and manifestations? What is one such concept you have avoided, and what small step can you take today to approach it?
?
You know that you have a soul, yet its essence is hidden. Are you living as though that hidden reality matters? What single change in your daily routine would reflect a genuine seeking of your soul's purpose through its known effects, such as your conscience, your drive for meaning, or your longing for God?

The Known and Unknown Aspects in Conceptualization
Definition and explanation of known vs unknown aspects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between the known aspect and the unknown aspect of a thing, and why the unknown aspect is identified with the essence itself in the context of seeking conceptualization.

Core Idea
The unknowable essence is the sought reality, known only through its attributes.

This passage from a classical kalam text tackles a fundamental question: How can we seek to know something if its essence is unknown? It distinguishes between two aspects: one that is known through attributes, and another—the essence itself—that remains unknown and is the true goal of conceptualization. The discussion also addresses textual variations and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then follow the English translation. Notice the key terms 'al-wajh al-ma'lum' and 'al-wajh al-majhul'.
The passage includes commentary on textual variants; don't get lost in the details yet.
2
Identify the two aspects
Find where the passage defines the unknown aspect as the essence/reality (al-dhat wa al-haqiqa) and the known aspect as the attributes (essential or accidental).
The unknown aspect is hypothetical, set as an example to emphasize the main point.
3
Understand the role of 'hypothetically'
The passage uses 'faradan' (hypothetically) to indicate that considering the essence as unknown is a pedagogical device, not a permanent state.
This prepares the way for the idea that we can still seek conceptualization even without full knowledge.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends by stating that specific realities (like divine attributes) are sought either by their inner reality or by a more complete aspect.
This ties the discussion back to theology: seeking knowledge of Allah's attributes.

This is based on the fact that conceptualizing a thing in its reality is obtaining the thing itself, so it is impossible to seek its attainment by considering the attainment of an accident that accedes to it, for if the thing is present, it is not sought by something else that would be an instrument for its presence. And what he warned of is open to discussion, for acquiring some accidents of a thing after knowing its reality may be insofar as it is an instrument for observing it and a mirror by which its state is known, so what is sought is conceptualization, not assent. And that conceptualization by accident is inferior to conceptualization by inner reality does not negate that the former is sought, as the purpose may be attached to it rather than the latter. And it is better to specify the direction of unknownness for the essence because it is more prevalent and more appropriate for someone who is in the process of knowing the realities of things. Thus the commentator interpreted 'essence' in the author's statement as 'reality' and did not interpret it as the essence of the sought thing so that it would include the types of definitions, as will come similarly, and it contains a warning of the two directions. (These mentioned matters are His attributes, so that specific reality is sought itself to be conceptualized in its inner reality or by a more complete aspect than what was mentioned, even if it does not reach the inner reality.)
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the distinction between the known aspect (al-wajh al-ma'lum) and the unknown aspect (al-wajh al-majhul) in the context of seeking conceptualization of a thing. It begins by quoting the main text: 'And nothing that is predicated of it from its essential or accidental attributes'—meaning that the unknown aspect is not like that; rather, something can be conceived that is predicated, i.e., the known aspect. The unknown aspect, hypothetically, is the essence (dhat) or reality (haqiqa) itself, which is sought to be conceptualized in its inner reality (kunn). The known aspect is some established consideration, whether essential or accidental. The commentator then explains the textual variants: the original phrase 'bikunnihi' (by its inner reality) is estimated to contrast with 'and nothing that is predicated of it'. Some copies have 'wa la bi-shay' (and not with something) with the preposition ba', so the commentator supplies 'bikunnihi' to specify the coordinated item. He also discusses the meaning of 'hypothetically' (faradan) as an exemplification to emphasize acquiring conceptualization of the reality. Citing Sharh al-Maqasid, he explains that the unknownness of the essence is necessary so that even if one knows the thing in its reality, acquiring accidents is through proof, not definition. Then he delves into the difference between knowing a thing by its aspect and knowing the aspect itself, noting that some textual variants suggest no difference, but the correct interpretation is that the aspect is predicated of the thing. Finally, he concludes that the specific reality (al-haqiqa al-mukhtassa) is sought for conceptualization by its inner reality or by a more complete aspect.

Key Terms

الوجه المجهول
الذات
الحقيقة
الكنه
التصور
العوارض
التصديق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the unknown aspect is completely separate from the thing's essence, or that it is entirely unknowable.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the unknown aspect is the essence itself, which is sought but not yet attained; it is not a separate entity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the 'unknown aspect' (al-wajh al-majhul) hypothetically identified as?
A) The known aspect
B) The essence and reality of the thing
C) An accidental attribute
D) The instrument of observation

Key Takeaway
The unknown aspect in seeking conceptualization is the essence itself, while the known aspect consists of its attributes, and the goal is to grasp the reality either directly or through more complete aspects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Does your knowledge of Allah's attributes (e.g., Ar-Rahman, Al-Alim) ever bring you closer to His essence, or do you merely accumulate descriptions? What would change if you truly sought Him beyond the attributes?
?
When you learn about spiritual realities like the soul or the afterlife, do you settle for knowing 'about' them, or do you hunger to know them with your whole being, even if full comprehension is impossible?
?
The commentator argues that acquiring attributes after knowing the reality can be a mirror for conceptualization. Is your study of Islamic theology a mirror that reflects the truth, or a collection of facts that leaves you unchanged?

The Third Thing and the Distinction Between Two Kinds of Knowledge by Aspect
Enumerates two positions on the distinction between two types of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dispute over a third thing beyond the two aspects and how early and later scholars differ on knowledge of a thing by its aspect versus knowledge of the aspect itself.

Core Idea
The disagreement over a third thing hinges on how 'knowledge by aspect' is analyzed.

This lesson introduces a key debate in Islamic epistemology: when we know something by one of its aspects, is there a third entity beyond the known and unknown aspects that is sought? It then lays out the contrasting positions of early and later scholars on the nature of such knowledge, setting the stage for the responses that follow.

Guided Path
1
Identify the third thing claim
Read the first part of the passage where some scholars affirm a third thing (amr thalith) beyond the two aspects. Note the commentator's caution about the phrasing.
The two aspects are the known (al-wajh al-ma'lum) and the unknown (al-wajh al-majhul).
2
Understand the two types of knowledge
The passage distinguishes between 'knowledge of a thing by its aspect' and 'knowledge of the aspect itself'. Later scholars say they differ in essence; early scholars say they differ by consideration.
This distinction is crucial for the later argument.
3
Compare the two schools
Focus on how each school defines the difference. Later scholars make the aspect itself an instrument; early scholars see the same mental content viewed differently.
The terms taghayyur bi al-dhat and taghayyur bi al-i'tibar are key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من أثبت في جواب هذه الشبهة وراء الوجهين أي الوجه المعلوم والوجه المجهول أمراً ثالثاً هو المطلوب يقومان به أي الوجهان به، وهذا القيد أعني قيام الوجهين بالأمر الثالث زائد على كلام هذا المثبت وفيه حزازة لجواز أن يكون أحد الوجهين [؟] قال: من قوله : ( ومنهم من أثبت إلخ ) اعلم أنهم اختلفوا في علم الشيء بالوجه، وعلم وجه الشيء لا تحقيق له أنه لا تغاير بينهما أصلا وقال المتأخرون : بالتغاير بالذات إذ الأول الحاصل في الذهن نفس الوجه، وهو آلة ملاحظة الشيء والشيء معلوم بالذات، وفي الثاني الحاصل في الذهن صورة الوجه، وهو المعلوم بالذات من غير التفات إلى الشيء ذي الوجه، وقال المتقدمون : التغاير بينهما بالاعتبار إذ لا شك في أنه لا يمكن أن يشاهد بالضاحك أمر سواه، إلا أنه إذا عتبر صدقه عليه أمر واتحاده معه كما في موضوع القضية المحصورة كان علم الشيء بالوجه، وإذا اعتبر مع قطع النظر عن ذلك كان علم الوجه كما في موضوع القضية الطبيعية، إذا علمت هذا، فاعلم أن عود الإمام إما مبني على عدم التغاير مطلقا، وتقريره أن الشيء المشعور به من وجه دون وجه لا يطلب بوجهيه لأن الوجه المعلوم معلوم والوجه المجهول مجهول فلا يطلب شيء منهما ، فلا يمكن طلبه وإما مبني على رأي المتقدمين وتقريره أن المطلوب إذا كان مشعوراً به بوجه دون وجه كان المعلوم والمجهول في الحقيقة هما الوجهان، لكن من حيث صدقهما على ذلك الشيء، واتحادهما به والمعلوم معلوم مطلقاً، والمجهول مجهول مطلقاً لا يمكن طلب شيء منهما، فإن أجيب علي رأي المتقدمين فالجواب ما ذكره المصنف، وهو أنا لا نسلم أن الوجه المجهول مجهول مطلقاً، لأنه إذا كان الوجه المعلوم معلوماً من حيث الاتحاد بذلك الشيء، والمجهول مجهولاً من تلك الحيثية كان الوجه المجهول معلوماً من حيث اتحاد الوجه المعلوم به ولا معنى حينئذ لجواب نقد المحصل إذ المطلوب ليس أمرا ثالثا عندهم، وإن أجيب على رأي المتأخرين فالجواب ما ذكره في النقد وهو أنه لا يلزم من امتناع طلب الوجهين امتناع طلب الأمر الثالث الذي هو ذو الوجهين، فكما أن الوجه المعلوم صار آلة الملاحظة الشيء، ومرآة لانكشافه كذلك، يطلب ذلك الشيء بأن يصير أمر آخر آلة لملاحظته، ومرآة له وتفصيله أن عارض الشيء قد يلاحظ في نفسه، فيكون العارض معلوماً، والشيء مغفولاً عنه بالكلية، وقد يجعل آلة لملاحظته، وحينئذ يكون معلوماً باعتبار ذلك العارض مجهولاً باعتبار آخر، فيتحد المعلوم والمجهول لكنه معلوم من حيثية ومجهول من حيثية أخرى، ولا استمالة فيه ولا معنى حينئذ لجواب المصنف، إذ ليس المطلوب عندهم الوجه حتى يجاب بأن الوجه المجهول ليس مجهولاً مطلقاً، فتدبر والله الموفق وأما ما ذكره الشارح من أنه الزام للإمام حيث اعترف بمغايرة الوجهين لذي الوجهين ففيه إن العبارة المشعرة بالتغاير ليس إلا قوله : لكن لما اجتمعا في شيء واحد، إذ لا بد من التغاير بين الظرف والمظروف، وهو لا يقتضي التغاير بالذات لجواز قوله : ( أحد الوجهين جزءاً (الخ في كونه جزءاً كفاية في أن القيام ها هنا غير واقع موقعه، فلهذا اقتصر عليه، وإلا فيجوز أن يكون كلا الوجهين جزءاً
And among them is one who affirmed, in answer to this ambiguity, besides the two aspects—i.e., the known aspect and the unknown aspect—a third thing that is sought, and the two aspects subsist through it. This restriction, namely the subsistence of the two aspects through the third thing, is additional to the statement of this affirmer, and there is a difficulty in it because it is possible that one of the two aspects [؟] He said: from his statement: 'And among them is one who affirmed, etc.' Know that they differed concerning knowledge of a thing by its aspect and knowledge of the aspect of a thing; there is no verification that there is no distinction between them at all. The later scholars (al-muta'akhkhirun) said: distinction in essence, for the first—what occurs in the mind—is the aspect itself, and it is an instrument for observing the thing, and the thing is known in essence; in the second, what occurs in the mind is the form of the aspect, and it is what is known in essence without attending to the thing that has the aspect. The early scholars (al-mutaqaddimun) said: distinction between them is by consideration, for there is no doubt that it is impossible to observe by 'the laughing' something other than it, except that if one considers its truth regarding something and its union with it, as in the subject of a quantified proposition, it is knowledge of the thing by its aspect; and if one considers with disregard of that, it is knowledge of the aspect, as in the subject of a natural proposition. If you know this, then know that the return of the Imam is either based on absolute non-distinction, and its explanation: the thing perceived by one aspect without another is not sought by its two aspects because the known aspect is known and the unknown aspect is unknown, so nothing from them is sought, thus it cannot be sought; or based on the view of the early scholars, and its explanation: if what is sought is perceived by one aspect without another, then what is known and what is unknown in reality are the two aspects, but insofar as they are true of that thing and their union with it, and what is known is absolutely known, and what is unknown is absolutely unknown, nothing from them can be sought. If one answers according to the view of the early scholars, the answer is what the author mentioned: we do not concede that the unknown aspect is absolutely unknown, because if the known aspect is known from the perspective of union with that thing, and the unknown is unknown from that perspective, then the unknown aspect is known from the perspective of the union of the known aspect with it, and there is no meaning then to the answer of Naqd al-Muhassal, for what is sought with them is not a third thing. If one answers according to the view of the later scholars, the answer is what he mentioned in al-Naqd: it does not follow from the impossibility of seeking the two aspects the impossibility of seeking the third thing which is the possessor of the two aspects. For just as the known aspect became an instrument for observing the thing and a mirror for its disclosure, likewise that thing is sought by making another thing an instrument for its observation and a mirror for it. Its detail: the accident of a thing may be observed in itself, so the accident is known and the thing is completely neglected; and it may be made an instrument for its observation, and then it is known by virtue of that accident and unknown by virtue of another, so the known and the unknown are unified, but it is known from one perspective and unknown from another perspective. There is no impossibility in that, and there is no meaning then to the author's answer, for what is sought with them is not the aspect so that it could be answered that the unknown aspect is not absolutely unknown. So reflect, and God is the grantor of success. As for what the commentator mentioned, that it is an obligation upon the Imam since he acknowledged the difference between the two aspects and the possessor of the two aspects, there is in it that the expression indicating difference is only his statement: 'but when they meet in one thing', for there must be difference between the container and the contained, and that does not necessitate difference in essence because it is possible his statement: 'one of the two aspects is a part'—that it is a part suffices that the subsistence here is not in its proper place, so he limited himself to it, otherwise it is possible that both aspects are a part.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage discusses a response to a doubt (shubha) concerning the possibility of seeking conceptualization of a thing when it is known only by an aspect. Some scholars posited a third thing (amr thalith) beyond the known and unknown aspects as the object of seeking. The commentator notes that restricting the two aspects to subsisting through this third thing is an addition to the affirmer's statement and has a difficulty (hazaza) because one of the aspects might be a part. He then explains the disagreement between the early (mutaqaddimun) and later (muta'akhkhirun) scholars regarding knowledge of a thing by its aspect ('ilm al-shay' bi al-wajh) and knowledge of the aspect itself ('ilm wajh al-shay). The later scholars affirm essential distinction (taghayyur bi al-dhat): in the first, the aspect itself is in the mind as an instrument, and the thing is known in essence; in the second, the form of the aspect is known in essence without attending to the thing. The early scholars say distinction is by consideration (bi al-i'tibar): the same mental content can be viewed as knowledge of the thing by its aspect when considering its truth and union with the thing, or as knowledge of the aspect when disregarding that. The commentator then analyzes two bases for the Imam's argument: either absolute non-distinction, leading to the impossibility of seeking the thing by its aspects, or the early scholars' view, leading to the same impossibility. The author's response (al-Musannif) is then given: for the early scholars, he denies that the unknown aspect is absolutely unknown because union with the known aspect makes it known from that perspective. For the later scholars, the response from al-Naqd is that seeking the two aspects is not the same as seeking the third thing (the possessor of the aspects); just as the known aspect serves as an instrument, another instrument can be sought for the thing. The commentator concludes by reflecting on the commentator's (al-Sharh) point about the Imam's acknowledgment of difference between the aspects and their possessor.

Key Terms
الأمر الثالث
الوجه المعلوم
الوجه المجهول
التغاير بالذات
التغاير بالاعتبار
المتقدمون
المتأخرون
آلة الملاحظة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The third thing is an external entity distinct from the known and unknown aspects.
Correction: The third thing is a conceptual posit; the passage focuses on its logical role, not its ontology.

Checkpoint
According to the later scholars (al-muta'akhkhirun), how do 'knowledge of a thing by its aspect' and 'knowledge of the aspect itself' differ?
A) They are identical in essence
B) They differ by consideration only
C) They differ in essence: one is the aspect itself in the mind, the other is the form of the aspect
D) They differ because one is sought and the other is not

Key Takeaway
The dispute over a third thing and the nature of knowledge by aspect is foundational for the subsequent argument about the possibility of seeking an unknown thing.

What Is Kalam?
advanced
8 min
Responses Based on Early and Later Views and the Commentator's Point
Analyzes two responses to the Imam's argument and a commentator's clarification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Imam's argument against seeking a thing by its two aspects is answered differently based on early and later views, and why the commentator's objection differs.

Core Idea
The impossibility of seeking the two aspects does not entail impossibility of seeking the thing itself.

This lesson continues the discussion by presenting the Imam's argument based on two possible assumptions (non-distinction or early view) and then shows how each school provides a counter-response. It concludes with the commentator's remark on the Imam's acknowledgment of difference between the aspects and their possessor. The passage requires careful attention to the different levels of analysis.

Guided Path
1
Examine the two bases of the Imam's argument
The Imam's argument can be based either on absolute non-distinction or on the early scholars' view. Read how each leads to the impossibility of seeking the thing by its two aspects.
The two aspects are the known and unknown.
2
Follow the answer for the early view
The author (al-Musannif) denies that the unknown aspect is absolutely unknown because it is known through its union with the known aspect.
This response avoids needing a third thing.
3
Follow the answer for the later view
From al-Naqd: the impossibility of seeking the two aspects does not rule out seeking the third thing (the possessor of the aspects). The known aspect serves as an instrument to seek the thing via another instrument.
This preserves the possibility of seeking the thing itself.
4
Understand the commentator's point
The commentator (al-Sharh) argues that the Imam acknowledged a difference between the aspects and their possessor, but this does not force essential distinction.
The container-contained relationship can be conceptual.

ومنهم من أثبت في جواب هذه الشبهة وراء الوجهين أي الوجه المعلوم والوجه المجهول أمراً ثالثاً هو المطلوب يقومان به أي الوجهان به، وهذا القيد أعني قيام الوجهين بالأمر الثالث زائد على كلام هذا المثبت وفيه حزازة لجواز أن يكون أحد الوجهين [؟] قال: من قوله : ( ومنهم من أثبت إلخ ) اعلم أنهم اختلفوا في علم الشيء بالوجه، وعلم وجه الشيء لا تحقيق له أنه لا تغاير بينهما أصلا وقال المتأخرون : بالتغاير بالذات إذ الأول الحاصل في الذهن نفس الوجه، وهو آلة ملاحظة الشيء والشيء معلوم بالذات، وفي الثاني الحاصل في الذهن صورة الوجه، وهو المعلوم بالذات من غير التفات إلى الشيء ذي الوجه، وقال المتقدمون : التغاير بينهما بالاعتبار إذ لا شك في أنه لا يمكن أن يشاهد بالضاحك أمر سواه، إلا أنه إذا عتبر صدقه عليه أمر واتحاده معه كما في موضوع القضية المحصورة كان علم الشيء بالوجه، وإذا اعتبر مع قطع النظر عن ذلك كان علم الوجه كما في موضوع القضية الطبيعية، إذا علمت هذا، فاعلم أن عود الإمام إما مبني على عدم التغاير مطلقا، وتقريره أن الشيء المشعور به من وجه دون وجه لا يطلب بوجهيه لأن الوجه المعلوم معلوم والوجه المجهول مجهول المعلوم معلوماً من حيث الاتحاد بذلك الشيء، والمجهول مجهولاً من تلك الحيثية كان الوجه المجهول معلوماً من حيث اتحاد الوجه المعلوم به ولا معنى حينئذ لجواب نقد المحصل إذ المطلوب ليس أمرا ثالثا عندهم، وإن Commentary
This passage discusses a response to a doubt (shubha) concerning the possibility of seeking conceptualization of a thing when it is known only by an aspect. Some scholars posited a third thing (amr thalith) beyond the known and unknown aspects as the object of seeking. The commentator notes that restricting the two aspects to subsisting through this third thing is an addition to the affirmer's statement and has a difficulty (hazaza) because one of the aspects might be a part. He then explains the disagreement between the early (mutaqaddimun) and later (muta'akhkhirun) scholars regarding knowledge of a thing by its aspect ('ilm al-shay' bi al-wajh) and knowledge of the aspect itself ('ilm wajh al-shay). The later scholars affirm essential distinction (taghayyur bi al-dhat): in the first, the aspect itself is in the mind as an instrument, and the thing is known in essence; in the second, the form of the aspect is known in essence without attending to the thing. The early scholars say distinction is by consideration (bi al-i'tibar): the same mental content can be viewed as knowledge of the thing by its aspect when considering its truth and union with the thing, or as knowledge of the aspect when disregarding that. The commentator then analyzes two bases for the Imam's argument: either absolute non-distinction, leading to the impossibility of seeking the thing by its aspects, or the early scholars' view, leading to the same impossibility. The author's response (al-Musannif) is then given: for the early scholars, he denies that the unknown aspect is absolutely unknown because union with the known aspect makes it known from that perspective. For the later scholars, the response from al-Naqd is that seeking the two aspects is not the same as seeking the third thing (the possessor of the aspects); just as the known aspect serves as an instrument, another instrument can be sought for the thing. The commentator concludes by reflecting on the commentator's (al-Sharh) point about the Imam's acknowledgment of difference between the aspects and their possessor.

Misconception & Correction

Checkpoint
According to the response in al-Naqd for the later view, why does the impossibility of seeking the two aspects not preclude seeking the thing itself?
A) Because the thing itself is already known
B) Because the two aspects are irrelevant
C) Because another thing can serve as an instrument to seek the thing
D) Because the unknown aspect becomes known

Key Takeaway
The argument's success depends on which school's view is assumed, and each school has a way to uphold the possibility of seeking the unknown thing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you willing to admit that there is a 'face' of God's essence that is utterly unknown to you, or do you pretend to have full comprehension? How does that affect your pursuit of Him?
?
The later scholars say the known aspect becomes a mirror for the unknown. In your own journey, have you ever used a known attribute of God as a mirror to seek a deeper knowledge of His essence? What concrete step will you take this week to turn a familiar concept into a tool for deeper insight?
?
The commentator's charge of inconsistency forces the Imam to clarify his terms. When someone challenges your belief, do you defensively repeat what you already know, or do you dig deeper into the exact meaning of your words, even if it leads to uncomfortable refinement?

What Is Kalam?
advanced
8 min
Three Invalid Ways to Define Conceptual Essence
Al-Imam enumerates three invalid ways to define conceptual essence and refutes each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the conceptual essence cannot be defined by itself, by its part, or by something external, according to al-Imam al-Razi's argument and its refutation.

Core Idea
Defining conceptual essence by itself, its part, or external all lead to impossibility.

This lesson presents the second argument from al-Imam (Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) against the possibility of acquiring conceptual knowledge through acquisition. He lays out three potential ways to define a conceptual essence and demonstrates that each leads to an impossibility. The passage is dense but crucial for understanding the limits of definition.

Guided Path
1
Understand the three-way division
The passage divides the possible definers of conceptual essence into three categories: itself, its part, or something external. Note that the third category includes both complete and partial external definers.
This division is exhaustive for any potential definer.
2
Examine the refutation of self-definition
Definition by itself is invalid because it would require knowledge of the definer before knowledge of the defined, leading to circularity. This is self-evidently impossible.
The definer must be known prior to the defined, but here they are identical.
3
Examine the refutation of part-definition
Definition by a part fails because either the part defines itself (circularity) or it defines another part, which is external to it, leading to the problem of external definition.
The commentary also discusses the possibility of defining by all parts, which reduces to self-definition.
4
Examine the refutation of external definition
Definition by something external is invalid unless that external encompasses all individuals uniquely, but even then it faces issues of circularity or infinite regress.
The passage notes that defining 'for' the external is not the same as defining 'by' the external.
